“ From the Old Dog,” by Frank Fox,
is at once a text-book on Australian Radicalism
and a bright, witty, interesting yarn. It deals
with the big and the humorously small issues of
political philosophy, from “Titles” to “White
Australia, 3 ’ “The Good Man Candidate, 5 and
“The Disadvantages of Beirut Too Serious.”
Published by T. C. Lothian, Melbourne. On saje
at booksellers and at this office, post free 2s. 9d.*

The Red Page
The Over-Seas Bard .
LET us consider the sort of poetry
that the other British races are
producing just now. America has
Edwin Markham, who wrote * ‘ The Man
witli the Hoe,” and has written nothing
poetical else, and George Sylvester Viereck,
a much over-boomed bard. His Nineveh
and other Poems (Brown, Langham, Lon-
don), is made up of blank pages and
pages full of words, but bare of poetry.
Half-a-dozen of these poems are worth
while ; the rest is suet-pudding. His
“ Nineveh” opens with a sonnet upon New
York which has a kind of forced, feeble
strength :
Huge steel-rilibed monsters rise into the air
Her Babylonian towers, while on high
Like gilt-scaled serpents glide the swift trains by,
Or, underfoot, creep to their secret lair.
A thousand lights are jewels in her hair,
The sea her girdle, and her crown the sky,
Her life-blood throbs, the fevered pulses fly,
Immense, defiant, breathless she stands there
And ever listens in the ceaseless din,
Waiting for him, her lover who shall come,
Whose singing lips shall boldly claim their own
And render sonant what in her was dumb :
The splendor and the madness and the sin,
) Her dreams in iron and her thoughts of stone.
But Viereck, though he most valiantly
woos, is not that lover. He intends to be
modern ; and in the quatrains of “Nineveh”
he displays a viewpoint strangely like that in
Arthur H. Adams’ London Streets. For in-
stance, these stanzas might almost have been
looked for in Adams’ “ Hyde Park ” :
Through the long alleys of the park
On noiseless wheels and delicate springs,
Glide painted women, fair and dark,
Bedecked with silks and jewelled things.
In peacock splendor goes the,rout
With shrill, loud laughter of the mad—
Red lips to suck the life-blood out,
And eyes too weary to be sad !
But, as a whole, the poem cannot be com-
pared with London Streets —and this critical
estimate will doubtless be accepted at its
worth when it is admitted that the present
critic is the author of London Streets. And
beyond “ Nineveh ” there is little of dis-
tinction in the book except the titles of his
poems: “A Ballad of Sin,” “The Book of
Idols,” “The Ballad of Nun and Knight,”
“ The Three Sphinxes.” Viereck does not
mind saying dreary things like this :
Yet I went still onward, undeterred,
Ever onward, ever, ever onward,
Onward, onward through the darkling town.
And at last I came where stands thy dwelling ;
Ever slower grew my lagging footsteps,
Ever slower.
which is an easy way of making six lines do
duty for one ; or trite things like this :
For who shall speak with the raging flame,
Or stay the cataract in its leap ?
Yet he occasionally finds the good phrase.
As thus, in a sonnet to Swinburne :
Is it the sea that thunders in his song,
Or it his song reverberates in the sea?
\f/
But it is only occasionally, in fact, only in
this one poem, that Viereck strikes with a
sure hand the note of human passion.
THE HAUNTED HOUSE.
I lay beside you ... on your lips the while
Hovered, most strange . . . the mirage of a smile,
Such as a minstrel lover might have seen
Upon the visage of some antique queen
Flickering like flame, half-choked by wind and
dust,
Weary of all things, saving song and lust.
How many days and years and lovers’ lies
Gave you your knowledge? You are very wise
And tired, yet insatiate to the last.
These things I thought, but said not; and there
passed
Before my vision in voluptuous quest,
The pageant of the lovers who possessed
Your soul and body even as I possess,
Who marked your passion in its nakedness,
And all your love-sins when your love was new.
They saw as I your quivering breast, and drew
Nearer to the consuming flame that burns
Deep to the marrow of my bone, and turns
My heart to love even as theirs who knew
From head to girdle each sweet curve of you,
Each little way of loving. No caress,
But apes the part of former loves. Ah, yes,
Even thus your hand toyed in the locks of him
Who came before me, Was he fair of limb
Or very dark ? What matter, with such lures
You snared the heart of all your paramours !
To-night I feel the presence of the others,
Your lovers were they and are now my brothers ;
And I have nothing that has not been theirs,
No single bloom the tree of passion bears
They have not plucked. Beloved, can it be?
Is there no gift that you reserve for me—
No loving kindness or no subtle sin,
No secret shrine that none has entered in,
Whither no mocking memories pursue
Love’s wistful pilgrim ? lam weary too,
With weariness of all your lovers, and when
I follow in the ways of other men,
I know each spot of your sweet body is
A cross, the tombstone of some perished kiss.
With all its beauty and its faultless grace
Your body, dearest, is a haunted place.
When I did yield to passion’s swift demand.
One of your lovers touched me with his hand.
And in the pang of amorous delight
I hear strange voices calling through the night.
W
Alfred Noyes is doing good work in his
epic, Drake; but his occasional verse is dis-
appointing. His latest collection, Forty
Singing Seamen and Other Poems (Black-
wood), is of the commonplace-cultured
quality that is regularly turned out by most
of the English minors. In his lyrics he is a
sort of English Ogilvie, stringing off faintly
pretty phrases with a fatal ease. It is only
in his ballads that Noyes becomes interest-
ing; and in this volume “The Highwayman”
is excellently done. It starts thus, stir-
ringly :
The wind was a torrent of darkness among the
gusty trees.
The moon was a ghostly galleon tossed upon
cloudy seas.
The road was a ribbon of moonlight over the
purple moor.
And the highwayman came riding—riding—
riding—
The highwayman came riding, up to the old inn-
door.
“The Forty Singing Seamen” is equally
delightful.
Across the seas of Wonderland to Mogadore we
plodded,
Forty singing seamen in an old black barque . . .
But he took us through his palace and, my lads, as
I’m a sinner, ,
We walked into an opal like a sunset-colored
cloud—
“My dining-room,” he says, and, quick as light
we saw a dinner
Spread before us by the fingers of a hidden fairy
crowd ;
And the skipper, swaying gently
After dinner, murmurs faintly,
“I looks to-wards you, Prester John, you’ve
done us very proud ! ”.
Chorus : And we drank his health with honors,
for he done us very proud ! . . .
. . . But that forest fair defied us—
First a crimson leopard laughs at us most hor-
rible to see.
Then a sea-green lion came and sniffed and licked
his chops and eyed us,
While a red and yellow unicorn was dancing
round a tree !
We was trying to look thinner,
Which was hard, because our dinner
Must ha’ made us very tempting to a cat o’ high
degree!
Chorus : Must ha’ made us very tempting to the
whole menajeree ! . . .
Across the seas of Wonderland to London-town
we blundered,
Forty singing seamen as was puzzled for to
know
If the visions that we saw was caused by—here
again we pondered—
A tipple in a vision forty thousand years ago.
Could the grog we dreamt we swallowed
Make us dream of all that followed ?
We were only simple seamen, so of course we
didn’t know ! v
Chorus : We were simple singing seamen, so of
course we could not know !
W
It is a pity Noyes does not confine himself
to ballads. Yet here is a little thing worth
preserving:
EVE S APPLE.
When you leant thro’ the leaves with your slow
red smile and your ivory body bare,
Ah, what was the fruit you gathered that day,
white Eve with the dusky hair ?
For we took it and ate it together and laughed !
Your white teeth bit to the core.
There was little to leave for the doves to peck,
when our delicate feast was o’er.^
The ripe fruit breathed of kisses, you said, as your
breast’s white apples may ;
But your body was cold from the coils of the
snake when you came to my arms that day.
There was blood, red blood on our lips, white Eve,
as we nibbled away in the sun ;
But I knew that the fruit was my heart, white
Eve,
The red rent core of my heart, white Eve,
Which we gnawed and left for the rats, white
Eve, when our delicate feast was done.
\9>
Coming with a warm recommendation from
no less a critic than George Meredith, we
should expect something from The Collected
Poems of Dora Sigerson Shorter (Hodder and
Stoughton). Mrs. Shorter is indubitably a
balladist. She has the easy swing, the care-
less facility, the pleasant diffuseness of the
story-teller ; and her chief field is that most
fertile one of Irish legend. There is no
space for the quotation of her ballads,
though there are some charming ones upon
Banshees. If this paper had only a Banshee
page we would print them all, with a back-
yard for bunyips, and possibly a jujube ad.
from a professional taniwha. But, excluding
her Banshees, we may sample Mrs. Shorter
by this poem on “ A Meadow Tragedy ” :
Here’s a meadow full of sunshine,
Ripe grasses lush and high ;
There’s a reaper on the roadway,
And a lark hangs in the sky.
There’s a nest of love enclosing
Three little beaks that cry ;
The reaper’s in the meadow,
And a lark hangs in the sky.
Here’s a mead all full of summer
And tragedy goes by.
With a knife amongst the grasses,
And a song up in the sky.
And this simplicity of feeling is found
throughout all her poems. And she, though
feminine, has also the tragic note :
So for the luxury of the flesh, wrap it in fur of fox
that it be warm,
In the bear’s eoat sheltering its nakedness from
storm.
Give wine for its hot veins, fame for its throne,
and laughter for its lips,
All ends in one eclipse,
Sunshine or snows.
We gain a grave, and afterwards —God knows.
\l/
Much more distinctive work are the New
Poems of Herbert Trench (Methuen).
Trench has thought, modernity and the
poet’s mind. His Apollo and the Seaman
is a work of imagination second in its kind
only to Coleridge’s aged and loquacious sailor-
man. And Trench has forced the compari-
son by printing stage directions in the mar-
gin of his poem. It begins, stimulatingly,
thus :
Apollo through the woods came down
Furred like a merchant fine,
And sate with a Sailor at an Inn
Sharing a jug of wine. . . .
“ And why are you cast down, sailor
And why are you cast down ?
With lapfuls of the guineas light
Came you not back to town ?” . . .
The sailor tells him his dreadful news •
“ I heard them calling in the streets
That the ship I serve upon—
The great ship Immortality—
Was gone down like the sun.” ...
“ What was her build, that boat of yours,
So proud upon the sea ?
What was her make of hull and deck,
What suit of sails had she ? ”
“ O, her stretch of sail so white, so white,
By no man’s hand unfurled,
Was Heaven ! ”
“ And the decks you kept so bright ? ”
“ Were like this bustling world.”
“ And the hold and cockpit out of sight,
Pitch dark and ill to smell,
Full of the friends of your delight ? ”
“ That was the pit of Hell !
“ How think of her, gone down, gone down !
How think of her decayed !
Or that the maker of that ship
Could let his creature fade ! ”
\»/
Apollo explains that the ship was wrecked
by the
Mutiny of those friends of yours,
In irons down below... .
Blindly they stretched their scarry hands,
Their piteous hands, to me :
“ Since birds we cannot hear, nor sight,
Be thou our sanctuary !
Open again the narrow gate—
Let us no longer be ! ” . . .
And remembering then their mad outburst
Of quaint hope and despair
Who deemed each puny life should last
When nothing else escapes.
And the nations and the planets melt
Like breakers on the capes,
From laughter, from tears unquenchable,
Scarce able to forbear,
I smote the great hull to a ghost
And the mighty masts to air. ...
So the last great ship of man is lost:
Derelict—drifting out of hail—
Lost Immortality.
But Apollo has a new ship ready for us.
Earth is the ship ! Thou shalt have time
To find the Earth thy friend ! . . .
How like an eagle on the abyss,
With outspread wings serene,
She circles [—thought rolls under her
And the flash from the unseen.
Here’s to her mission, winged rock,
Bluff-bowed and heavy keel’d,
Through the night-watches swinging on
Still under orders sealed !
No crystal gives a peep, my son,
Of her errand far and surgy ; . . .
Nor, for the future, shall you reap
Much benefit of clergy.
Yet the seaman is not satisfied.
I am a man and not a race.
What matters, if self ends ?
And Apollo confesses his own impotence, his
kinship with his creature :
I too, like thee, have striven.
I too am launched from the profound
And past; I too am driven
In turn upon the stream of storms
From fountains beyond Heaven ;
And to me, too, light is mystery
And the greater life half-given.
And at the last the seaman is reconciled by
the sight of his sou playing with paper ships
by the gutter.
And whether it were ship or no,
The Earth seemed good to him....
Buffeting on from age to age,
It still was under way.
A great poem. The Voice from the Column
finely pictures Nelson warning the English—
So are the nations changed
Since the day of Trafalgar !
What we have been, they are.
And (beat low, muffled drum !)
What they were, we have become !
Indeed, the book has much worthy work.
The Stanzas to Tolstoy are packed with
thought, fiery with the glow of imagination.
And his Daughters of Joy treats a very old
theme in a very modern way. For a final
stanza let us close with this :
In summer-time when Mary bathes
And floats along as in a sky,
G might I be the stream that swathes
Her beauty with infinity!
Herbert Trench is one of the most hopeful
portents in present-day England. His work,
rough-liewn and crude in construction, is, like
Meredith’s, clogged with thought. In every
poem he has something to say, not some-
thing, as with Stephen Phillips, to describe.
He is forty-three years of age, and so far has
apparently produced only one other book of
verse and a translation of Merejkowsky’s
Death of the Gods.
The Moke
The Origin and Influence of the Thorough-
bred Horse, by Win. Ridgeway, is a brave
attempt to solve the problem of the origin of
the moke. And Australia, with its worship
of nag, ought to be interested. The author's
researches clearly establish the fact that the
horse, though indigenous in Upper Asia, was
not a native of Arabia. “ The reader will
probably be startled by this last statement,
inasmuch as it has hitherto been generally
held that Arabia is the original, and not
merely the adoptive, mother of the famous
steedsthat bear tiername.” But the testimony
of the great geographers Eratosthenes and
Strabo would of itself be sufficient to put it
beyond all doubt that the Arabs did not
breed, or even possess, horses until after the
beginning of the Christian era. Strabo says
of Arabia : “It is inhabited by Arab tent-
dwellers, who breed camels. With the
exception of horses, mules and swine, there
are numerous herds of cattle, and there are
all sorts of birds except geese and domestic
fowl. ” Speaking of the most fruitful portion
—that inhabited by the Nabataeans —he
says : “It produced sheep with white wool,
and oxen of a large size ” ; but adds emphati-
cally that the “ land does not produce horses,
whose place is served by camels.” The
Nabataeans were the Arabs who occupied
the ancient Edom, or Idumea, the region
lying between Syria and South Arabia. This
documentary evidence is fully confirmed by
the monumental. “ For when ASmilus
Seaurus, in B.C. 60, received the submis-
sion of the Nabataean King Aretas, he issued
coins bearing; on the reverse an Arab leading
his one-humped camel. The Arabian King
is thus portrayed, not as a cavalier,, but as
a camel rider.” Ridgeway also points out :
“ the evidence of the Semites themselves, set
forth in the Old Testament, puts it beyond
doubt that neither the tribes of Arabia
Proper nor those of Mesopotamia possessed
horses until a comparatively late period.
Even Job himself did not own a single horse.”
But he must have seen one, for God re-
marked to him that “ the glory of his nostrils
is terrible,” and also observed that the horse
“ saith among the trumpets, Ha, ha ! ” Cab-
horses nowadays have forgotten how to laugh.
Arab tradition also bears out the contention
that the original Arabian thoroughbred was
a camel. It was subsequent to the birth of
Christ that the Arab discarded his single-
humped camel and bestrode and bred the
moke.

The Maoriland volume of Newnes’ series
of “ Nations at Home ” has been entrusted
to a well-known Wellington journalist, R.
A. Loughnan. New Zealand at Home is a
chatty and discursive description of the
Dominion where the earthquakes come
from. To a stranger the book will doubtless
prove useful; but it cannot be compared
with W. Pember Reeves’ masterly Long
White Cloud. It is a pity that the author
had no opportunity of correcting his proofs,
for the Maori names under the illustrations
are in some cases mangled beyond recog-
nition.
Auckland Graphic recently held a plebiscite
of its readers for the best six M. L. poets (alive,
dead, or better dead), with the following
result :1, Bracken ; 2, Domett; 3, Jessie
Mackay ; 4, Adams ; 5, J. C. Andersen ; 6,
W. P. Reeves.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the Bree.
And it’s O ! but .her lips were red,
And Ah ! but her flesh was white ;
And I would ’twere I in her stead
At peace by the Bree to-night.
Where I saw them first together
My man and she alone ;
When the river slipped a-feather
By our old-time trysting stone.
“ Greeting ! ” His lips were set
To hers as never to mine ;
His eyes with bliss gleamed wet
As the waters, all a-shine.
For it’s O ! but her blood ran red
Till her face was white to see.
As I turned from the corpse and fled
That night by the lilting Bree.
S.A.
LILLIAS GORDON.
Nirvana.
Nirvana is not nothingness, no, not at all, to me;
I place it in a summer isle, embraced by azure
sea.
Nor must I follow Buddha’s faith if I would have
a chance
To reach his highest recompense, the Great De-
liverance.
I find that in my snug retreat, where worldly
hustlings cease;
Whose ways are ways of pleasantness, where every
path is peace,
A drowsy, bowered hermitage, whose vistas never
tire;
A realm of Lotopliagi, the Land of Heart’s De-
sire.
The South Sea sings its cradle-song my Shore of
Sleep around,
And warm winds, droning dreamily, this chorus
seem to sound :
“There’s nothing half so sweet in life, in sooth,
as our caress;
It’s perfume brings oblivion’s serene forgetfulness.
Come, rest you, all you weary ones ! oh, slumber,
all you sad !
So shall you quit disquietude, and, waiting, shall
be glad.”
For it is fixed that he who gains the gift of
Nature’s kiss
Knows everlasting peacefulness, and peacefulness
is bliss.
And bliss is but Nirvana, briefly put, in English
phrase,
So I may find my Paradise before I eh'd my days.
I need not chance Elysium, my Paradise is where
The surf foams white on coral walls that fence a
garden fair
And fruitful as that floral dream which Milton’s
stanzas limn.
Where Man the First knew happiness ere Woman
came to him.
Its spell attends a some-day’s glad translation to
bestow
Appeasement on a yearning soul that loved it
long ago.
And, though its charted name and site I don’t
precisely state,
Lest others rush my- Eden-nook until its charms
abate,
There is no ringed monopoly in rare and radiant
goals,
Though seeing eyes must seek for them, and men
are mostly moles;
For somewhere sure waits Paradise on earth for
every man,
If only he can win to it; ah, if he only can !
Nirvana is not nothingness, nor need one die to
find
The best of all beatitudes, tranquillity of mind.
N.S.W.
TALAFEKAU.
The Temple and the Shape.
They carved it out of marble
When the world was in its youth ;
They painted it with golden dyes,
And named the Shape “ A Truth.”
They stood it in a temple—
A wide and spacious hall—
A sweet and gracious work of art
Upon a pedestal.
The rolling ages knew it,
They felt its wizard charm ;
And men and maidens sought its aid
In hours of soul-alarm.
It held the bondman patient,
It dried the eyes of Grief;
It promised out beyond the grave
Reward and rich relief.
A thousand preachers praised it,
A thousand poets sang ;
And through the night, and through the day,
Its name imperial rang.
From land to land it governed,
From cape to sullen cape ;
’Twas like to God in God’s own home —
The Temple and the Shape.
Yet came the Sons of Knowledge,
The searchers keen and pale,
Who break the rocks, and weigh the stars.
And rend the hiding veil.
They entered at the Temple,
With eager heart and eye,
And there about the statue’s base
They read its name—“ A Lie.”
And when the Shape lay tumbled
From its high pedestal,
The earth still ran an even course,
Nor did the heavens fall.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (■with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “'The Editor. Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ojiicials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 13, 1908.
STATE ENTERPRISE OR CHAOS.
The N.S.W. Farmers’ and Settlers’ Association
lately sat in anxious conference in Sydney, and it
said a good many things about the beauties of
Private Enterprise, even while passing several
dozen resolutions in which it clamored wildly for
State or Public Enterprise to come to its assist-
ance and giveit a hand. One of the great difficulties
of the period is the difficulty of holding back
Private Enterprise by the coat tail as it sprints
multitudinously up the Ministerial stairs to ask
the State to interfere, and most times the coat-
tail slips, and Private Enterprise gets away, and
docs ask the State to interfere. It is largely
by reason of the incessant shout of Private
Enterprise for State Interference that State
Enterprise is spreading so rapidly, and
the people who complain about its expan-
sion are merely barking against the inevitable.
The State has been constructing things ever since
there was a State. The State has been interfering
ever since there was a State. The beginnings of
State Construction were also the beginnings of
civilisation, and presumably the end of State Con-
struction will be the end of civilisation. And,
as a general thing, the country which has most
State Enterprise is also the most civilised coun-
try. It is a little late in the day for a Farmers’
arid Settlers’ Association to shout against Collect-
ivism, which is State Enterprise, which in its turn
is the’whole embodiment of human progress.
In very early days the State—what there vyas of
it then —seems to have had a faitly m I iivate
Enterprise which was almost pathetic. It was
probably the need for roads which first set
State Enterprise going. Private Enterprise
either wouldn’t make roads at all or else one
bit of Private Enterprise wouldn’t take the
trouble to see that its fragment of road
joined on to the fragment that was built by the
next bit of Private Enterprise. In fact, in very
early and individualistic days one Private Enter-
prise couldn’t safely go to see another about a
road or anything else without great danger of
being killed. In Scotland, until a comparatively
recent date, the State authority was amazingly
weak, and Private Enterprise was supreme.
Consequently it isn’t much more than 100 years
since Scotland had roads at all. And when the
roads were made, Private Enterprise didn’t even
know which side of the track to walk on till Public
Interference told it to keep to the right. In the
olden, golden times of Private Enterprise war was
conducted by anybody who could buy the neces-
sary weapons and find the necessary followers,
and he took who had the chance, and he kept who
had the luck and the opportunity. War was pure
individualism, and every man was his own soldier
and his own policeman and his own pirate ; and
it wasn’t till State Interference made military
and naval and police matters a Government
monopoly that a farmer and settler could call
his wife or his cow or himself his own,
or could put his head out after dark—or even
before dark—without imminent risk that there
might fall upon it a club, wielded by parties who
are still at large. Genuine Private Enterprise
lingered long in Australia under the name of bush-
ranging (the bushranger didn’t ask the State for a
subsidy, so he was the genuine article—almost the
only genuine article) ; and it did many things it
shouldn’t have been allowed to do—notably it
laid out a good deal of early Sydney, which, for
lack of early and vigorous State Interference,
requires reconstruction worse than any other city
in the continent. It is amazing how little Private
Enterprise knew when left to itself. It didn’t
even know that it was unhealthy to throw out the
slops in front of the door, to have no drains and
no regular water supply, and to sleep ten people
and sundry pigs in a room. State Interference
washed and drained and scrubbed Private Enter-
prise, and taught it not to marry two wives, and
generally set it on the way it should go.
*•**■*
In early times weights, measures and money
were, to a great extent, left to Private Enter-
prise, and that article was as incapable and
incoherent as usual. The Tory press of to-day is
greatly addicted to recommending “the influence
of public opinion ”in preference to Parliamentary
Interference, but the force of public opinion
couldn’t even prevent the yard being about
in one part of England and nearly sft. in another,
and all manner of other lengths in between, till a
strong tyrant interfered and fixed it at ofiin.,
where it stdl remains. In the matter of weights
and measures, unfortunately, the State in Eng-
land didn’t interfere in time nor with proper
ferocity, and Private Enterprise and public
opinion wallowed along in their usual dismasted
way. As a consequence, instead of having only
one measure each of length, weight, area
and cubic capacity, there are in England
the legal stone, the customary stone, the
fodder (which is one weight at London
and another at Newcastle), the long ton
and the short ton, the clove, tod, wey,
pack, last, aum, pottle, strike, coomb, the perch
(of 30£ yards), the hide, the Lancashire acre,
Cheshire acre, and Heaven knows how many
dozens besides in the way of outlandish and com-
plicated and little-known measurements. The
English language, too, was left to Private Enter-
prise, with the awful result that there are five
vowels to represent nearly 20 vowel sounds, and
there are about 10 missing consonants (that is to
say, there are consonant sounds in the language
that are not in the alphabet), and the pronuncia-
tion has gone so utterly to the dogs of chaos that
no one can tell by the spelling of a word how it
sounds—in fact, if a man lived to a good old age,
and did nothing all his life save study the Eng-
lish language, the chances are that he would mis-
pronounce the name of his medicine while on
his death-bed, and mix up an irregular verb
in his last moments. But State Interference at
least saved the calendar from destruction. It
found Christmas getting further on into January
every year, and the State ran after Christmas,
and brought it back, and nailed it down, and tied
it up, so it, at all events, has been rescued from
the confused and unfixed habit of mind to which
Private Enterprise is so much addicted.
* * * ■*
It was long before decent roads were supplied
that the need for some kind of postal communica-
tion began to be felt. Private Enterprise never
got very far beyond writing the letter (if it could
write ; mostly it could only make its cross) and
giving it to a crusader to put in his helmet for
delivery at some unspecified time, or sending a
partially-naked person to run on foot, holding the
document in a cleft stick. The State interfered,
and created the post office, the money order
system, and the savings bank; also in most
countries it runs the telegraphs, and in many it
conducts the telephones. It regulated banking,
which had become a very chaotic institution, and
in some, cases it runs banks itself. When speedy
means of communication were wanted it either
built railways itself or explained to Private Enter-
prise how to do it; in England it took
over 40 Acts of Parliament to fully ex-
plain to Private Enterprise how to do
it. And in about nine countries out of ten
where the building and management of railways
was left to Private Enterprise—after State Inter-
ference had explained how to do it—the State
gave Private Enterprise, either in money or land,
a great part of the means of doing it. Once or
twice, however, the State didn’t explain—or
wasn’t strong enough to explain adequately—to
Private Enterprise how to do it; a notable case
was in early Colorado (TJ.S.A.) where the Denver
and Rio Cfrande Railway Co. and the Atchison,
Topeka and Santa Fe concern tore up each other’s
lines, hired small armies, fought battles, and fired
cannon at each other’s railway stations. Then
when harbors were wanted the State supplied
them ; and when canals were needed it mostly
supplied them also ; even such a mouldy thing as
the Egyptian State made the Suez Canal
possible and did the bulk of the hard work,
and State Interference is now creating the
Panama Canal and shovelling away the bones
that Private Enterprise has left in the way.
And when Private Enterprise, having been
supplied with harbors and canals, didn’t know
how deeply it was safe to load the vessels that
used them, the State invented a Plimsoll mark
for its enlightenment. It even had to pick the
weevils out of the sailor-man’s biscuit, and per-
sonally smell his salt junk to see if it was good,
because Private Enterprise was unfit for either
job. And, after leaving education to Private
Enterprise for hundreds and hundreds of years,
the State, in disgust, took charge, and started to
explain personally to the rising generation the
difference between B and a bull’s foot—a point on
which its ignorance, up to then, had been dis-
graceful as well as incredible.
The highways and byways of State Enterprise
are truly innumerable. It created English manu-
factures and shipping by a multiplicity of laws
which are mostly forgotten now, but which
did good service in their time. These laws, in an
amazingly few years, raised England from being an
aboriginal or King Billy kind of land which ex-
ported wool and bought manufactured goods from
Flanders, and which had its commerce conducted
by Flemings, and its banking by Italians, and
called its money “easterling” because even that
came from Germany, to the position of a State
which made things for itself, and looked all
the industrial world in the eye unafraid.
State Interference created the English horse;
that degenerate animal, left for many years
to Private Enterprise, was fast becoming
a sort of terrier, when the State ordered a
slaughter of undersized and diseased stallions and
useless brood mares, and imported improved stock,
and brutally curtailed the liberty of the subject
in the matter of breeding from inferior animals.
It taught the builder how to construct houses that
wouldn’t fall down ; every up-to-date community
lias Building Acts to prevent the sky-scraper
constructed by that notorious artist called
Jerky from falling headlong into the
street. It has built almost everything in
the world that is or has been conspicuously worth
looking at among the doings of man —the Pyra-
mids, the Pantheon, the Taj Mahal, the Kutab
Minar, Solomon’s Temple, the Tomb of Humayan,
the Parthenon, St. Peter’s, the hanging gardens
of Babylon, the pharos of P. Philaoelphus, St.
Mark’s, and other works innumerable. In between
times the State nurses the abandoned infant, cleans
pavements, runs fire, life and accident insurance the
more cheaply than Private Enterprise knows
how, carries you two miles in a tram car for a
penny, and takes care of the lunatic. Private
Enterprise used simply to let the village idiot
roam the streets and gibber at people, or else it
kept him in a wooden cage opening off the street,
as if he were a “ pretty cocky.” The State drags
the Private Enterprise churches apart by the
tail when they want to fix their teeth in
each other’s ear. And in its spare time,
and in different places, it acts as trustee and
executor, makes tobacco and cigars, manufac-
tures opium, boils sugar, hammers iron, drives
coaches, makes bread, makes and sells liquor,
sells cheap coal, conducts ferries, restores the
apparently drowned, makes clothes, runs after and
kills rabbits, supplies the lazy and incompetent
Private Enterprise person with water for irriga-
tion, rescues his partially-ruined banks from
destruction, lends him money for deep sinking in
mines, lends him money at a low rate on his farm,
lends him the State bull for breeding purposes,
and does other beneficent acts which Private
Enterprise seems unequal to in its own bloated
and arrogant and individual capacity. It even
carries Farmers and Settlers and their families to
Conference at half rates, they being too mean to
pay full rates, low as these are.
State Enterprise and State Interference, in
short, comprise the whole history of civilisation,
and the alleged Private Enterprise person who
objects to them is simply a person who objects to
civilisation. He is an Anarchist. When the
silly Farmers’ and Settlers’ Association de-
clares itself a staunch believer in Indi-
vidualism as against the Collectivist ideas of
the Labor party, it really proposes to undo civili-
sation —to get back to the time when a man (if he
could write, which was seldom) communicated
with another by sending his serf to run over bogs
and through forests with the letter stuck in a cleft
stick or hidden in his waist rag. Of course, the
Farmers and Settlers don’t really mean that—
they only think they do. What they would
probably say if they knew better how to express
themselves is that the existing State Socialism
and State Enterprise and State Interference
should be maintained, but that the peg should
be put in right here, and that State Enterprise
should spread no further. And after they had
said that they would most likely find that they
didn’t exactly mean it either. Yet it expresses the
idea of the present day self-styled individualist
about as well as anythingcando. Unfortunately for
the alleged individualist the peg won’t stay in after
it is put in. It gets washed out. Things in this
world won’t remain stationary ; even the Chinese
intellect moves. If they don’t progress they must
retrograde, and when State Enterprise begins to
retrograde it means that the country starts on
the back track towards the old condition of things
in which there were no roads, no railways, no
drains, no telegraphs, and each man was his
own army and navy and policeman. Every-
thing that makes civilisation began with State
Enterprise. The State either did things or made it
possible for private people to do them. The
beginning of things was chaos and disorder;
the progress of things was the growth of
the community principle, which made order
possible; and as the community principle must
either grow or decay, the end of things will
apparently be either complete State Socialism or
chaos again. If the community idea keeps
growing, the end must be Socialism. If it
begins to decay, the first sign will be that the
Bruce Smith or other individualist of the
moment will walk along the middle of the street
at night in order to avoid sudden attack ; then
presently there will be no street; and soon after
that there will be no Smith. The Farmers and
Settlers may object to Socialism as much as they
please. But as there is no more to be gained by
denouncing it than by speaking in disparagement
of the equator, they would be wiser to leave the
subject alone. Nothing is gained by throwing
clods at the inevitable, audit often happens that
while the farmer is away clodding the inevitable,
the cow at home has got through the fence and
fatally blown herself out on green sorghum.
THE “INTERNATIONAL FREETRADE
CONGRESS.”
In the course of his presidential address, Lord Wklby ad-
mitted that the prospect of Freetrade throughout the
world was somewhat cloudy. Protection was, he said,
advancing in very aggressive fashion, but Freetraders
were confident that the cause which promoted liberty at
home and friendship and peace abroad must ultimately
triumph.
Winston Churchill (President of the Board of Trade)
insisted on the intimate relation between Freetrade
principles and good international relations.
The keynote to all the speeches was the intimate con-
nection between Freetrade and peace.
SUFFRAGETTES EXPELLED.
Suffragettes frequently interrupted the meeting, especi-
ally while Mr. Churchill was speaking. They were ex-
pelled.
LONDON, Wednesday.—-The Manchester Master Cot-
ton-spinners propose to reduce wages five per cent.
A total of about 150,000 operatives will be affected if the
proposals are made effective. —Cctblegr evens front Jjoncion.
Very appropriately, the Congress is sitting in the
citadel of Freetrade.* Indeed, the British fact is in itself
an unanswerable defence of Freetrade. It is the one
great Freetrade European country. “How,” cries the
Protectionist, “ can little England be right and big
Continental Europe wrong?” This is as much as to say
that truth must always be the possession of the majority.
Truth, it inav be frankly conoedec»> must be with the
majority if all the Continental Protectionist countries
are superior in wealth and the conditions of social happi-
ness to Freetrade Great Britain. The logical requirement
of this Protectionist contention is that Great Britain
should be at the bottom of the comparative list. But she
is at the top. Bang goes the contention. England ought
to be ruined, but she is the greatest of empire builders,
holds the supremacy of the sea, and is the leading creditor
nation. . . . The keynote of all the speeches, says our
cable correspondent, was the intimate connection between
Freetrade and peace. —Sydney Telegraph.
* * *
When tlieForeign-trader has nothing better to say,
he generally asks, as the Sydney Telegraph does,
whether Britain, the great example of Freetrade,
is poorer or richer than the Protectionist nations
of continental Europe. It is a foolish query, for
there are two Britains in the ease. One is the
island of England and Scotland, which faced the
new fiscal epoch with its industries so mag-
nificently established, after a long course of
Protection, that it owned nearly one-third of the
world’s manufactures, fully one-third of the
world’s steam-power, about a third of the world’s
wealth, about a quarter of its commerce, and
nearly a third of its shipping. The other case is
that of Ireland, which, after 600 years of war and
oppression, had very few industries indeed.
Britain started out with the Freetrade super-
stition in its hand merely to defend the splendid
position it had already gained, and it
wasn’t driven to do even that in any serious
fashion till within the last 30 odd years, for
France only definitely abandoned the Freetrade
derangement about 1872, and Germany about 1879,
and the United States didn’t take to Protection
in any scientific fashion till the late ’sixties, or
take to it in real earnest till 1890. Till these
dates there was no very serious attempt to challenge
Britain’s supremacy. And even then it naturally
took a long time to materially reduce the stupendous
lead which Bull’s Own Country had gained under
Protection. The worst which has happened
up to date is that Britain has been so far over-
taken by its Protectionist rivals that it now
occupies only third place.as to wealth and manu-
factures ; that its agriculture has perished to such
an extent that it can now only feed itself for
about two months in the year; that quite a
number of its counties are marked on the map as
practically uninhabited while the congestion in
towns is stupendous ; and that it sends out every
year over 300,000 emigrants of its own people,
mostly bound for Protectionist countries. Barring
these matters, and the fact that about a quarter
of the population never has quite enough to
eat, and that the country is already losing its
faith in the Cobden gospel, there is nothing much
wi'ong with Great Britain after about 30 years of
competition, under Freetrade conditions, with two
great Protectionist rivals.
* * * *
But Ireland faced the beginnings of Freetrade
under wholly different conditions. With Ireland
it wasn’t a case of merely defending the strongest
manufacturing, commercial and financial position
the world had ever seen. It was rather a question
of trying to do what fool-advisers like the .Sydney
Telegraph urge Australia to attempt —to build
up new industries and new wealth and new great-
ness on Freetrade principles. Ireland is the true
criterion of the blessings of Freetrade as they
apply to us. It started about 1850 from scratch
or thereabouts in an industrial sense, just as
Germany did about 1879, only the two countries
started with different fiscal policies. The results
are that Ireland has lost half its population, that
a nation has been almost obliterated, and that the
green and fertile isle became, almost from the day
when Freetrade began to be imposed upon it, the
one white man’s country on earth that was unfit for
a white man to live in. And Germany, starting
from almost the same point, became, inside 30
years, Britain’s dangerous rival in almost every
branch of industry. Comparing Ireland and Ger-
many, the dispassionate observer begins to have
grievous doubts about the shoddy gospel.
The off-hand shout of such blind guides asSydney
Telegraph is to the general effect that if
a mere 30 years or so of competition on a
Freetrade-ver,-ms-Protection basis hasn’t utterly
ruined England, therefore Freetrade is proved
beyond all question to be the one. true faith.
The contention is too thin, for great countries
don’t collapse in a day. For very many years—
the years in which Britain’s supremacy over the
nations of continental Europe was built up—
Britain was the great high-tariff country amidst a
multitude of low-tariff rivals. Portugal tried
Freetrade of a kind, and went to the Devil, and
has remained at the Devil ever since. France
tried Freetrade, and the misery that resulted
brought about the great Revolution. It is custo-
mary to ascribe the Revolution to legal oppression
and feudalism, but with all these drawbacks Pro-
tectionist France under Colbert was rich and
contented, and Freetrade France under Turgot
and Necker was so hungry that it could have
almost eaten its king raw after it had beheaded
him. Germany also experimented with low
tariffs, and became a land of beggary. Then
Napoleon I. fell on all these poor, miserable, low-
tariff nations, and about finished their ruin, and
Protectionist Britain emerged from the Napoleonic
war owning almost all the wealth, manufactures
and commerce of Europe. Even the United States
got the low-tariff madness for many years, and
while it was a question of high-tariff England
competing against its low-tariff foreign competi-
tors, England’s supremacy was beyond all ques-
tion. But though the work of many generations
hasn’t been wholly undone in about 30 years, it
doesn’t follow that Freetrade has proved its case.
There are still a good many years to come.
And Britain hasn’t by any means trusted to
Freetrade to keep it afloat. During the Freetrade
period it has annexed by force and high-handed
violence the richest diamond field in the
world, just to give the Cobden policy a lift.
It has seized in the same way the richest
goldfield in the world. It annexed the world’s
ruby supply and a huge proportion of the
world’s oil supply when it took Burmah. It
took d uring theF reetrade period British East Africa
(180,000 square miles). Central Africa (42,200
square miles), Cyprus (3584 square miles), Fiji
(7435 square miles), Lagos (3420 square miles),
Nigeria (over 300,000 square miles), North Borneo
(31,000 square miles), Somaliland (63,000 square
miles), Bechuanaland (275,000 square miles), the
Orange Free State (50,000 square miles), Rhodesia
(144,000 square miles), the Transvaal (117,000
square miles), and Zanzibar (1020 square
miles); also it casually roped in Egypt and
Nubia, and a heap of other places. A country
which was eating up the earth in that fashion
could hardly avoid making some money, for the
time at least. And when nearly one-third of the
human race is not only ruled from but is
mostly compelled to adopt the Freetrade or low
revenue-tariff gospel and keep its ports open to
British goods, Freetrade in England gets an arti-
ficial lease of life which has nothing to do with
its own merits.
Probably the most humorous aspect of the situ-
tion, however, is the assurance that Freetrade and
peace are linked together. Britain had been in a
state of reasonable tranquillity fur many years


before it abandoned Protection—the marine shindy
at Navarino, the first Chinese war, the taking of
Sir Charles McCarthy’s skull to Accra, and
some troubles in India and Afghanistan were
almost the only blots on 40 years of unusual
calm. People were actually talking about
war having come to an end. But in the
Freetrade period things woke up to some
extent. There was a desperate war with Russia,
also an Indian Mutiny, a war with China, and a
war with Persia. A European war, which might
have been as great an affair as the Napoleonic
struggle, was only averted because Britain broke
its solemn treaty and left Denmark to its fate.
There was war with Abyssinia, war with Ashantee,
Afghan war, Zulu war, Soudan war, Egyptian war,
Transvaal war, an invasion of Mexico, a war
with Burmah, a Kaffir war, a war with Thibet,
and many sundries. Quite a number of these
wars arose through the lust for conquest—a lust
that was partly forced upon Britain because,
having thrown its own markets open to every-
body, it took to hunting all over the earth for new
markets outside. In fact, Freetrade Britain has
on its hands, as a general thing, about six wars
for one that the most quarrelsome Protectionist
country can show. And Freetrade Britain is to-
day the world’s awful example in the way of
bloated armaments, for it spends more money on
military and naval preparations than any other
country on earth. It has to do that for a number
of reasons. In its anxiety not to tax imported
goods it has heaped on direct taxation till direct tax-
ation and cheap imports have almost killed its agri-
culture, and being in consequence the only country
that could be starved out by a two months’ block-
ade, it has to maintain a stupendous navy, not
only to protect its own shores, but also to guard
all the sea routes by which its imported food
comes to hand. Then it must needs defend its
Crown colonies, which supply markets for
so . •puuh of its goods. A Protectionist
Britain could insist on the free admission of
British goods to France, Germany, Austria,.
Russia and the United States as the price of the
free admission of the goods of these countries to
British territories. But Britain, being obsessed
by the Freetrade superstition, reckons that that
sort of bargaining is beneath the dignity of the
great Cobden principle. So its alternative is to
go forth and conquer hundreds of thousands of
square miles of country occupied by scores of
millions of niggers, so that it may sell goods to
these niggers. Then to hold all that coun-
try and all the niggers, it requires the
great fleet already mentioned, and as a
result Britain has the biggest bill for armaments
and the largest list of wars going. Theoreti-
cally, Freetrade may be the doctrine of peace,
but in practice it seems to lead to a fair number
of troubles.
* * * *
The period of large and easy conquest—the
period when Britain was acting the part of Alex-
ander the Great (and the Drunk)—seems nearly
over now. There hardly remains a bit of the
globe that can be annexed without fighting a
Great Power to the bitter end, therefore the Great
Burst has apparently seen its best days. The British
Freetrade craze had amazing luck. It started
when England was, through long Protection and
good fortune in war, the greatest and richest
Power on earth. It was immediately followed by
the discovery in British territory—in Maoriland
and Australia—of some of the greatest gold de-
posits the world has ever heard of. It was
followed later on by the discovery, in the
territories of two weak little States lying
just alongside British territory and handy
for annexation, of the boss gold and diamond
fields of the world. Also, it was followed by a
wonderful movement in the way of invention,
which quadrupled, or more than quadrupled, the
manufacturing power of the human race, and a
good share of this invention was of British origin.
Likewise—largely through the gold discoveries;
which set adventure loose again in a way that had
been unknown since Elizabeth’s spacious days—
there came over the British people a large, reck-
less tendency to invasion and annexation
which in a few years ate up about 5
per cent. of the globe, and all that
globe was worth money. Thanks to these ad ven-
titious aids nothing worse has happened to Britain
during some 30 years of competition on Freetrade
principles .against vigorous Protectionist rivals
than that it has dropped from first to third place
in wealth and industrial importance. It
still keeps • the lead in shipping and
finance, so the result might have been worse.
Yet it might have been a great deal better. But
Australia hasn’t enjoyed two or three centuries of
high Protection; and ic isn’t the supreme
manufacturing and financial and commercial
nation of the world; and it doesn’t possess
a population nor an army nor a navy that
would enable it to go out and conquer in a
few years about 5 per cent, of the globe. There-
fore, it can’t afford to follow in England’s
footsteps, and it doesn’t want to follow in the
footsteps of Ireland. Consequently, it isn’t tak-
ing any Freetrade—which seems to be curiously
tangled up with the throwing out of women who
only want a vote, and the habit of cutting down
the wages of 150,000 cotton spinners by Is. in
the £.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
How to Make Great Wealth out of Nothing.
Premier Price came back from England with a
depressing tale in his month. He told the S.A.
Parliament that, so far as he can see, Australia
will never get out of the clutches of the London
Stock Exchange group, which controls “colonial ”
loans, until a Commonwealth Bank is established
in London to deal in Australian stocks. If that
is the case, it seems fairly probable that Australia
will never get out of the hands of the blessed
“group” at all. South Australia now owes
£4,000,000 less in London than it did two or three
years ago, and it fancied that its position on the
London market would thereby be strengthened.
It isn’t. The State has a £3,000,000 loan falling
due, and it proposed to pay off £1,000,000 with its
own money, and to borrow the rest. It had a
vague idea that it had the right to arrange
the new loan anywhere it pleased, and it
proceeded to negotiate with Lloyds’ Bank, a
stupendous concern with nearly £7,000,000 of
capital and reserves, and over twenty million
pounds of uncalled capital. An institution like
that, which is about eight times the size of the
Bank of N.S.W., looked strong enough for any-
thing, but the sharebroking firm of Nivison,
which represents “ the group that controls colonial
loans,” heard of the pending transaction, and on
behalf of the Stock Exchange trades-union or con-
spiracy, it ordered Lloyds’ to drop the business.
Lloyds’ dropped it, and South Australia learned
the dismal fact that, even though the loan asked
for is a mere renewal of an old I 011, and the State
which asks for the money is vastly more solvent
than it has been for a long time, it must
accept the cash thankfully at any old rate
that one or two London firms like to de-
mand, ‘and if these firms get into a huff
over something and won’t accept an abject
apology, the money won’t be forthcoming at all,
and then, unless the coin can be raised in Aus-
tralia, the State must be declared bankrupt.
Premier Price thinks that a Commonwealth Bank
in London would meet the case, but it is difficult
to see that it could do anything where Lloyds’
failed. Australia, in short, has been kicked hard
by the “ group,” and warned that, unless it is
perfectly humble and obedient in the future, it
may be kicked fatally next time. Under these
circumstances it should be worth while for the
Australian Government to think over the possi-
bility of the adoption of the Canadian banking
system as a partial alleviation of the trouble.
Under that system the banks would be required
to hold at least 40 per cent, of their
cash reserves in Federal Government paper
money—in other words, they would be required to
accept Commonwealth paper money to the value of
about £10,000,000 and give gold in exchange. As a
guarantee of the absolute security of this paper
money, the Australian Treasury could hold (say)
£2,000,000 in gold and £8.000,000 in State securi-
ties. In other words, the Treasury could hold 20s.
in the £ of security against its paper issue, and
as the interest on its State securities accumulated,
and was invested in more State securities, the
guarantee would increase—in 12 years there would
be £2.000,000 cash and over £12,000,000 of State
securities to guarantee £10,000,000 of paper,
which would represent 28s. in the £. and in 26
years the guarantee would equal 40s. in
the £. With this fund the Australian
Government could renew, without application
to the London market, all the State loans
which fall due in London during the next
two years, and thereafter it could at least, out of
its revenue from interest, take up a portion of
each State loan that fell due. That would relieve
the immediate pressure, and in the interval thus
gained it might be possible to find some way of
dispensing with the arrogant British stock ex-
change trades-union which assumed such a high-
handed and heavy-booted attitude in its dealings
with S.A. The proposed method is one
whereby great wealth would be made out of
nothing. Forty per cent, of the money
which lies idle and unproductive in the banks
would be replaced by paper secured by assets
worth 20s. in the£, and these assets would steadily
increase. till the paper money would be covered
many times over. The paper would, for local
purposes, be as useful as gold. But if any bank,
through the diminution of its cash reserves, found
that it was holding more than 40 per cent, in Go-
vernment paper, it could obtain coin for the surplus
at the Treasury, so the profit-earning capacity of the
banks wouldn’t be diminished one whit. Yet the
country would acquire, out of nothing, an annual
income at the rate of £280,000 in the first year, and
about £560,000 in the 20th year, and £840,000 in
the 32nd year—in fact, it would create a big sink-
ing fund out of nothing, and start to pay off its
public debt out of nothing. It seems a pity to
fool away such a chance to make a lot of money
out of nothing.
Abdul the Damned.
The recent upheaval in Turkey is another evi-
dence of a fact which has been noted before, that
the best-laid schemes often go amiss. Abdul-
Hamid (Abdul the Damned) had a scheme—dark,
intricate, inscrutable, boiler-plated, riveted,
copper-bottomed, weather-proof, and apparently
infallible. When Abdul-Aziz died violently, and
Murad V. was violently shifted off the premises,
Abdul-Hamid was raised to the throne mainly by
reason of his obvious piety. He was the. mildest
and most prayerful and most humane prince in
Europe or Asia. In his early days, when he was
steering his way amid the dangers of the court of
his uncle, Abdul-Aziz, the Man Who Stayed Out
Late At Night, Hamid never jostled a caterpillar
without apologising. But when he climbed
to the Imperial dignity a change set in.
There was a great reform movement on at
the time, and Hamid temporised by creating
a Parliament. But a remarkable mortality
set in among the conspicuous reform leaders, and
when the inquest on the last of them was finished,
and the jury had reported that the cause of his
death was the act of God, complicated by measles,
the Parliament was adjourned for good, and
Abdul-Hamid became a new man. His predeces-
sors had been rather a short-lived race, and Hamid
didn’t want to be short-lived. It wasn’t his habit.
So he perfected a huge and amazingly successful
but badly-paid spy system. His spy or secret
police force was, reputedly, like the sands of the
seashore for number. Its members, as a rule,
didn’t know each other, though their officers knew
them. The officers in many cases didn’t know
each other, though Hamid knew them. Hamid
was. . alleged to know everybody in a
general way. Id was said that in Hamid’s
empire three men might sometimes conspire
together with impunity, but if they took
in a fourth he was sure to be a palace spy. Then the
three disappeared. The spies were sometimes paid
and sometimes not. When a secret service agent’s
wages were in arrear he usually said nothing ; if
he said anything, the man he said it to might be
another agent, and he might disapper for speaking
evil of his august but slow-paying master. Still
the seeretWvice cost a lot, and partly for that
reason and partly for other reasons the pay of the
army was always in arrear, and even captains and
colonels were poor and shabby. Still the fear that
they might also take the disappearance disease
was strong upon them. One crowd that Hamid
is reputed to have paid regularly, however, was a
bodyguard of Albanians or some such ruffians, and
they were faithful to him for various reasons, one
being the knowledge that if he died they would
probably be wiped out in the subsequent con-
fusion. In Abdul Hamid’s reign there were mas-
sacres, brigandage and all manner of horrors, and
over and over again the Great Powers interfered ;
but Abdul smiled upon them, and negotiated
with them separately, and sowed dissension
among them, and promised everything, and fooled
everybody, and did nothing, arid sent the Powers
& way on an empty stomach. For 32 years the
Sultan lied and schemed in Constantinople, never
daring to go far away from his capital lest the
State machine might break down in his absence.
He did almost everything in the way of govern-
ment himself because he trusted nobody ; and the
result was that lie toiled far longer hours than a
bricklayer; and yet everything was done badly.
So terrified was he of delegating his authority,
that if a fire broke out in Constantinople it was—
or used to be—necessary to send to the palace for
permission to turn out the fire brigade. Hamid
is said to have once sacked a Minister of State
because he went out for half-an-hour’s walk
and didn’t fully report the matter to his
Imperial master next day. The secret police
agent reported it, of course. It was a won-
derful system, but when 10,000 ill-paid,
ill-fed, disgusted soldiers rose up on an
impulse and said “ Damn the Sultan,” and 20,000
more, hearing them, rose up and said “Damn the
Sultan” also, Abdul Hamid struck something
against which his secret service was powerless.
When the first sudden, unanimous “Damn” was
uttered, the shabby, ill-paid, neglected officers
joined the movement, the European army got out
of hand, the Asiatic army refused to fight against
the European army, the mob lynched the chief of
the secret police, and things burst up generally.
So Abdul the Damned hurriedly climbed down,
and Turkey is supposed now to have a Constitu-
tion and a Parliament, and to be a free country,
and quite Europeanised and up to date. Put
people who remember 1878, and the last Parlia-
ment, and the curious mortality among the last
lot of reform leaders, have their doubts.
The Bootblack and the Professor.
Prof. Marshall-Hall lately told the Melbourne
Argus how lie discovered a fine, aesthetic senti-
ment in the soul of a little Cockney bootblack who
insisted on giving to the musician’s boots the
most exquisitely finished Piccadilly polish of
which art and blacking were capable. The Prof,
would have been satisfied with a less faultless pro-
duction ; but the small artist bootblack cared less
for the opinion of his patron than for his own
idealistic cravings after the Beautiful. The
Dignity of Labor, thereupon argues the Prof.,
should stand above considerations of the market
place, display a fine scorn for hours or wages,
Factories Acts, and such like, and be its own
reward. It is a noble creed. In the Greece of
Pericles, the Rome of Michael Angelo, and
the England of Chaucer, everybody, more or less —
butcher and baker and candlestick-maker— was an
artist, a producer of beautiful things. In old
Japan, before the introduction of battleships and
breweries and picture postcards, every artisan was
an artist; he had plenty of time and no
visible wages, and he adorned whatever he
touched. The only interesting things in
Japan-bronze, lacquer, pottery, cloisonne—are
old things produced before the Joss of
Cheapness became the idol of the world.
In those idealistic times everything one touched
or looked on—clothes, carpets, dishes, stained
glass windows, even a Lord Mayor’s banquet (if
there was any Lord Mayor)—was made a thing of
beauty and a joy till it broke. Now, alas ! the
Guilds, historical and utopian, have passed
away, and Ugliness has become supreme.
Marshall-Hall laments that the worker has lost
pride in his craft, and blames the Labor party for
the evil results. And therein he barks up the
wrong tree. The dire result is due, not to the
sweated artisan, but mainly to Fat, of whom the
Prof, is such a fervid admirer. The Working
Man’s College, where many young artisans, after a
hard day’s work, study at night in order to be-
come experts in their various crafts, affords a
good example of the pride still felt by some in
fine workmanship. But what pleasure can a
working man feel in his trade when it has been
debased by Fat, with his insatiable hunger for
profits and dividends ? It is true that Cheapness
has displaced Quality, but Fat is the High Priest
of Cheapness. The worker has to supply the
demands of his employer, or get the thing you
put wheat in. If he objects, as. the Prof, would
have him—“ Say, boss, I want more time over
this job ; it might suit you, but it doesn’t satisfy
my artistic soul”—he would be fired out because
he couldn’t produce sufficient shoddy per hour.
So the Professor is wrong. The humble artisan
who has been robbed of the joy and pride of work
is not to blame. And not altogether is even Fat
to blame. Poor Fat is but the pearl of the com-
mercially diseased oyster. He is the child of the
age—and the Argus. We are all to blame, all
of us who favor things cheap and ugly, and
reckon “ Moses Bringing Down the Tables of the
Law ” better value for the money than the “Bent
Tree” of Corot. Shoddy has made Philistines
of us all. Nevertheless, all honor to the Prof.’s
little artist bootblack, who, being such an artist,
and no lover of shoddy, and with no capitalistic
vices, is probably now, not as the Prof, imagines, a
bloated employer, but a sandwich man, carry-
ing through the Strand a hideous placard, adver-
tising a foolish actor in an impossible play—the
easy descent of the artist soul in this sordid and
commercial age !

Government by Griffith.
That Australian Chief Justice Griffith is a
singularly able lawyer no one of any judgment
presumes to doubt. That on any question of pure
law his opinion is of the first value in the Common-
wealth is again indisputable. Yet, allowing those
premises, there is still need to question whether
Chief Justice Griffith (who, as a politician,
“boxed the compass’’from extreme Radicalism
to extreme Toryism, and seemed to find the
latter the most congenial direction for his thoughts)
is the best of men to lay down the
precedents on those fine questions of Constitutional
law now arising, which are quite as much political
as they are legal in character. In the matter of
the “ New Protection,” as The Bulletin pointed
out a few issues ago, it was possible for an honest
and skilled lawyer of Tory views to decide in quite
a different fashion from an honest and skilled
lawyer democratic views. Mr. Griffith's
views are Tory, so the New Protection was con-
stitutionally banned. In the matter of the
Union label, decided last week, the situation
was repeated, with the modification that it
was just barely possible for a Tory lawyer to give
a color of legality to his political prejudices
against such “Labor legislation.” Mr. Griffith
is such a splendidly skilful lawyer that he was
capable of the task, and the union label legisla-
tion of the Australian Parliament was declared
“ unconstitutional.” Except to the legal mind,
which can lose all track of common-sense in a
maze of subtleties, the decision seems an extra-
ordinary foolishness. The law, as passed, pro-
vided that a trades-union could register a
label as its trade-mark, its sign that
the goods so labelled were made by its
members; and that that label would be
thereupon protected against piracy. There was
no hint that anyone should be forced to use the
label, no prohibition against the Employers’
Federation, which dearly loves a blackleg, also
registering a blackleg label and securing protec-
tion for it. The law simply said that a trades-
union might get legal protection for the innocent
and desirable purpose of letting the public dis-
tinguish the products of its members. (The
British law which won’t allow “Made in Ger-
many ” goods to masquerade as British is on the
same lines, though it goes much further.) Since
the Federal Constitution gives the Australian Par-
liament full power over all trade-marks matters,’
the subject was clearly within its authority. But
Mr. Griffith says no. In giving the Federal
Parliament jurisdiction over trade-marks, the
Australian people did not mean, he maintains, to
really give it that jurisdiction unfettered. They
meant only to give it jurisdiction over such trade-
marks as he would call trade-marks ; and his
definition does hot agree with the dictionary
definition, nor with the British law definition, nor
with the United States law definition (the
State of California has union-label legisla-
tion, directed against the union-breaking tac-
tics of the Tobacco Trust, and this legislation
has never been upset by the Tobacco Trust, which
is not slow in seeking injunctions and prohibitions
of the Federal judiciary when there is a ghost of
a chance of success). The position now is
that the Federal Legislature may pass Acts,
but Mr. Griffith can upset them by
veto, and does so pretty consistently.
There is really no need to bring any other
personality into the matter. True, there are
five High Court Judges. Two of them, Mr.
Isaa.CS and Mr., Higgins —both eminent law-
yers who in politics were democrats—invari-
ably disagree with Mr. Griffith’s vetoes
upon democratic legislation. The other two,
Mr. Barton and Mr. O’Connor, invariably concur
with Mr. Griffith. Everywhere that Mr. Grif-
fith goes they are sure to follow. They seem to
be obsessed by his masterful mind, tio the posi-
tion comes to government of the Common-
wealth by Mr. Griffith. Thk Bulletin never
saw much cause for joy in the government of
Queensland by Mr. Griffith, and it doesn’t
welcome him as the autocrat of Australia.
Mercenaries or Citizen Soldiers.
Colonel Foster, R.E., grows lyric on the theme
of Defence and the Australian soldier, most
Saturdays, in the Sydney Telegraph. But to
Australians, it all appears very much beside the
point. He regards the local warrior as an
extremely useful warrior—for England. Colonel
Foster is a stern foe of the Deakin Government’s
scheme of a citizen army. Such a force would be
no good to England. He holds that the Aus-
tralians of the present generation should not
think of self-defence in their time, but should
devote their military energies to equipping them-
selves to act, should need arise, as Britain’s
mercenaries in foreign parts. All his enthusiasm
is reserved for the possibility of Australia being
able, not merely to “equip and despatch men
willing and able to fight” on Britain’s behalf, but
for the further possibility of their “reaching the
seat of war, so organised as to be complete, with
staff supply and transport services and field
ambulances.” “ If,” he concludes, “the unavoid-
able wastage in War were readily repaired by
reinforcements in men and horses from their own
country, their usefulness to the Imperial army
would be doubled.” And so on. The trouble
about all this is that, though it inspires
Colonel Foster and most other English officers,
it doesn’t inspire the average Australian to any
great extent. The present-day Australian feels
that his* first duty is to his native land; a share
in the job of buttressing Great Britain
comes, in his estimation, a long way after.
The Commonwealth is a big and desir-
able place—so big and so desirable that its
inhabitants feel the defence force Colonel Foster
approves is nowhere near big enough to meet the
exigencies of the situation. With careful train-
ing, the existing troops would make, doubtless,
valuable enough British auxiliaries. But they
aren’t half numerous enough to defend Australia.
It is impossible to convince one sane Common-
wealth resident in a hundred that the tin-can
battleships which constitute Australia’s naval
defence arm are adequate to keep this country in-
violate in time of stress. As a fact, political
and Admiralty heads in the United Kingdom
hardly trouble nowadays to keep up the fiction
that the local squadron is capable of warding off
serious foreign aggression from Australian shores.
The attitude of the Sea Lords is that they can’t
afford to hire their best to the Commonwealth for
its defence (the great modern vessels and guns are
too badly needed in British waters), and that if
Australia wants front-rank fighting machines it
will have to show as much patriotism as the little
South American tates, and build them for
its own protection. Tbe whole trend of the
modern British defence policy, both naval
and military, is to concentrate strength in
and around England. Naval manoeuvres are
based on North Sea schemes in accordance
with that idea. England is reducing its overseas
armies (e.y., in South Africa) because it is coming
to be recognised by the War Office authorities
that Imperial defence ought to begin at home.
Haldane’s Territorial Army, which is earmarked
“ To be used for British Defence only,” is a plain
manifestation of the same trend. Even so Tory a
publication as the Saturday Review admits
(27/0/’O8) the advisability of the “ introduction of
compulsion” into that scheme, and that is the
expressed view of Roberts and the other world-
famous soldiers of the British National Defence
League. In a word, the United Kingdom is doing
as hard as it can most of the things Colonel
Foster warns Australia against. If the possi-
bility of the invasion of the United King-
dom, which is rimmed round by the cream
of the finest navy on earth, is worth guard-

The Old Delusion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All European nations, except Russia, were represented
at the Freetrade Congress held under the auspices of the
Cobden Club.
Lord Welby, who presided, admitted that the prospects
of Freetrade throughout the world were somewhat
cloudy.— Daily paper.
The Old Delusion on the hearthstone sits,
Trying with palsied hands its shins to rub,
While round it stand the intellects and wits
Of that good Brotherhood —the Cobden Club.
Cold blows the Fiscal wind, beyond the walls
The wolves of Fact howl madly at the door,
And from the ancient’s eye a teardrop falls,
And damps with gratitude the Brothers’ floor.
Out in the great, uncharitable world,
All doors are closed against the Old Mistake ;
Only in here can he lie snugly curled
Upon the lukewarm hearth for old time’s sake.
jTho’ in Time’s grip his weary bones are wrung
With aches and agues, still he’s full of sin
E’en while for him the Mass is being sung,
Behold him to the larder creeping in !
He robs the Brotherhood of half their store
And brings starvation to the gloomy cells ;
Yet they the world’s hostility deplore,
Thinking him honest as the vesper bells.
The Old Delusion on the hearthstone sits
Trying with thievish hands its shins to rub,
While round it stand the intellects and wits
Of that slow Brotherhood—the Cobden Club.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

To the query “ Why is the American
Fleet?” may be made the reply that he is
mentally speedy because his climate stimulates to
high-geared mental action. But the Australian
needs to quicken his wheels of thought with well-
regulated oilings of D.C.L., the Whisky of Wise
progress, if he wishes to hasten the advent of an
Australian Fleet in Being.*
