' The coin of the cocky flows in good seasons to
the pockets of Australian implement makers, partly for
patriotic reasons, but chiefly because the local makers
keep to the front with labor-saving- devices. A stump
jumping disc cultivator is now on tap, invented by J. B.
Garde and made by Sunshine McKay. The mallee gentry
who are too tired to tug out the stumps use this new
critter to dig the cleared spaces, and it climbs over the
intervening palisades without breaking its knees.*
has no navy worth mentioning, should claim at
least an equal amount of attention from Aus-
tralians along the same rational lines.
The Genteel Clerk and his Humble Lot.
Shocked to the marrow by the fact that ‘‘ a
certain person endeavored to get him to join a
Clerks’ Union,” and by the further fact that the
said Clerks’ Union held a meeting recently at the
Trades Hall, Sydney, and had the vulgar audacity
to draw up a living wage schedule, etc., one who
signed himself “ A Clerk” wrote half a column to
the Sydney Telegraph (6/8/’08) in protest. To
that writer the association of his class with organ-
ised labor appears in the light of a contamination.
He is horrified at the discovery that the rising
generation of Australians in the avenue of em-
ployment along which he toils is jettisoning the
old tradition of humble abasement in the presence
of its employer, and of abject reliance on the
latter’s “kindness.” He beseeches clerks not
‘ ‘to get themselves involved with Labor unions, the
Labor party, the Labor Council, and Socialists.”
Reading his screed, one conceives him shuddering
violently while he wrote, at the tragic thought
that employers might come to regard clerks with
contempt, and question their collective right to
the style of “gent,” as a result of this mad and
radical step on the part of some of their number.
He himself, he explains, is well-satisfied with his
lot. He doesn’t mention his age or his qualifica-
tion, but, whatever they are, he gets a whole
£3 10s. a week for them. (He is probably well
over 40, and in the possession of a family.) The
side-advantages of his job are stupendous. A few
of them, as recorded by himself :
(1) i If by any chance I should lose my usual train to Syd"
ney in the morning and am late, I hear no word of com-
plaint, and, of course, I cheerfully endeavor to make up
for my lateness.
(2) I get one hour for lunch, and can, if need be, take a
little more.
(3) Treated by our worthy employers almost as equals.
(4) l always get a cheery recognition from them in the
morning.
(5) I frequently take work home voluntarily, and this,
under the circumstances, I am delighted to do. And
when, as sometimes happens, it is necessary forme to
exert myself more than usual so as to meet the pressing
desires for expedition of the persons who do business with
my employers, I do so cheerfully.
(6) On one occasion I sprained an ankle, and, naturally,
having such exemplary employers, I was anxious to get
back to the office at the earliest possible moment, and re-
turned before my foot was capable of properly doing its
work. When I turned up at the office one of the prin-
cipals saw my limping gait, and very kindly told me to go
home again.
In the first place, this crawlsome citizen—doubt-
less by some well-defined process of cause and
effect—is in a financial position that not one
clerk in twenty may ever hope to attain. The
average clerkly wage is below that of a tram-con-
ductor, and away underneath that of a de-
cent artisan. It takes an educated man of
mature years, ripe experience, and a high
order of intelligence to earn at the clerking
game anything over the pittance the Telegraph
correspondent reaps weekly. The number that
don’t, and under present conditions may never
hope to, get anything like that sum, crowd all
Johnson’s trams and trains early in the morning
and late at night every weekday of the year. A
proportion of them are parasites living inglori-
ously, perforce, on their relatives till the time
comes when they shall earn a screw big enough to
keep body and soul moored together unaided—
and that time is a matter of years in the coming,
often enough. In every case a clerk has to expect
that he will be called on to do “back-work” or
“overtime.” In the warehousing business he is
paid extra for “overtime” (the Factories Act
has brought this Socialistic curse on the Anti-Sosh
clerk); in businesses which don’t come under
the Factories Act, the clerk just does his
overtime as cheerily as he may for nothing,
plus Is. or Is. 6d. “tea” money. The holidays
of the mere or ordinary clerk are few. . The Bul-
letin is acquainted with an ex-clerk who toiled
for seven years in a bicycle concern and scored
never a holiday, barring public ones, during all
that time. That the clerk is worse paid and
worse treated proportionately to his worth than
any other worker in the Commonwealth is a
truism. His congenital snobbishness, his fatuous
hankering to be regarded as a “ gentleman,”
as something of a clay that is different
from the dust of his ancestor who wore
the bowyangs unashamedly, is responsible—
that, and the presence in his midst of calamities
such as the £3 10s.-a-week Telegraph scribe
abovementioned. He pays the price of shabby-
gentility, and lives always on the steep black
edge of Want in return for the privilege of wear-
ing a dark coat and. a small black bag, and being
in a position to talk disparagingly of unions. To
the unelerkly intellect the reward doesn’t seem
worth the price paid for it. And it is refreshing
to note that a big section of the clerkly intellect
in the Commonwealth is beginning to look at
things that way too.
The Party of Snuffle.
The N.S.W. Government, which has intervals of
hard common-sense, last week brought down a
Bill to provide that when vagrants who fall into
the hands of the police are found to suffer from
certain dangerous and easily-communicable
diseases they should be kept in custody until
cured, quite irrespective of the nominal length of
their sentences. It is a merciful proposal, and the
people treated under it would owe a debt of grati-
tude to the State which gave them another chance
of a clean and wholesome life. And it is a wise
proposal, for the danger to the public health from
the dissemination of contagious diseases can hardly
be over-estimated ; according to one knowledgable
doctor of The Bulletin’s acquaintance, quite
half the illness of the community is due to that
singlecause. Yet, merciful and wise as the measure
is, it has raised a protest from the party of Cant
and Snuffle. “Mr. G. E. Ardill, Hon. Secretary
Public Morals Association,” in a long letter to the
press, calls upon “every worker in the cause of
moral reform ” to fight the proposal to the bitter end.
How moral reform is going to be advanced by
allowing sick people to remain sick and to infect
other people with sickness, Mr. Ardill doesn’t
explain. Cant and Snuffle seldom do explain.
But the idea which governs the protest is plain—
that if unfortunates fall ill through what
Mr. Ardill calls “ vice,” but which a more
humane judge would probably define as the
result of the sweating, the cruelty, and the
greed of our modern social system, they should
be allowed to remain ill, and make as many others
ill as possible. The Bulletin hopes that Mr.
Wade will not be frightened from his purpose by
the protests of the Party of Snuffle. Better rather
to extend the measure, and provide that every
poor sufferer from any form of communicable dis-
ease who comes under the knowledge of the police
should be segregated until cured, and that the
State should attend to the cure.

“Briefless” makes a few remarks :
Wade’s Industrial Disputes Act is, according to N.S.W.
Full Court, a young bear with a peck of troubles
before it. In a dispute over the appointment of a Board
in a minor matter affecting the Newcastle collieries, the
coal owners moved the Full Court for a writ of prohibi-
tion or something. Now Wade’s Act distinctly says
(section 52): —
“Any decision of the Industrial Court shall be final,
and shall not be removable to any other Court by certi-
orari or otherwise ; and no award, order, or proceeding of
the Court shall be vitiated by reason only of any infor-
mality or want of form, or be liable to be challenged,
appealed against, reviewed, quashed, or called in question
by any Court of Judicature on any account whatsoever,
and the validity of any decision shall not be challenged
by prohibition or otherwise.”
When the Chief Justice read this section, the horsehair
on his wig curled in amazement. Having dismissed the
appeal of the coal-owners, which, he carefully remarked,
was on a distinct point of law quite apart from Section
52, he made a few remarks about Section 52. It appears
that Governor Rawson assented to Wade’s Act, and the
Legislature in Section 52 has taken away a prerogative
of the Crown. Now the Crown, which is Ned, must con-
sent before his prerogatives are interfered with, and
apparently Rawson’s job doesn’t include the right of
abandoning the prerogatives of the Crown, or filching
any rig-ht Ned has under the Constitution. So the whole of
the Industrial Disputes Act, according to Justices Darley
and Pring, is ultra vires. This opinion is rough on Mr.
Wade’s law, and unpleasant in many ways.

“ Old Soldier ” : Henry Lucy, in a recent letter
to the S. M. Herald, has expressed the terror
with which Germany is regarded by some classes
in Britain. Lucy alludes to someone who seems to
be White, the defender of Ladysmith, in this
fashion :
He takes the gloomiest view of the military position of
the country, more especially with respect to artillery.
He admits that Mr. Haldane’s scheme is excellent in
theory, and, if fully developed, would be effective. Mean-
while, the country is at the mercy of Germany. When
the hour strikes, he says, Germany will pick a quarrel
with France, in five days will be at the gates, of Paris, will
demand the French navy as the price of peace, and with
its own fleets will be more than a match for England.
White must have taken leave of his senses if he
talked like that. In 1870 it took the Germans
two months to get to the gates of Paris, and then
they had another four months’ work before they
could make peace on much cheaper terms than
those suggested. The army opposed to them was
inferior in everything, and the French fortresses
were old. ill-armed and badly provisioned. The
French frontier towards Germany is now pro-
tected by a first line of four immense entrenched
camps, enclosed by strong, permanent, heavily-
armed forts, which would have to be taken
one by one. When, if ever, this first line
is broken through, there are two others behind it
to be forced before “ the gates of Paris,” or rather
the outer line of forts that surround the city, will
be met. Some of these forts are as much as eight
miles from the city, and their circuit averages over
20 miles across. Paris, even according to German
authorities, could now be neither bombarded nor
blockaded. Things are not too good with the
British army or the French navy, but that German
army which is to reach Paris in five days is too
gorgeous a dream.
♦
The other day an officer of the Adelaide Board of
Health bailed up a milk cart that was on its way
to the Destitute Asylum—the hard, repulsive
name which allegedly philanthropic S. Aus.
applies to its home for aged indigents—and,
taking a sample of the liquid, found that it con-
tained over 38 per cent, of added water. That
meant that for every five gallons of milk there was
a trifle over three gallons of water. The Board of
Health looked hard at this shocking attempt
to rob the old people of very nearly half of
almost the only food some of them can
take, and then passed the scandal on to
the City Council’s solicitors. The solici-
tors, having overhauled the matter, reported
that it wouldn’t be any use prosecuting, because
the alleged milk was supplied under con-
tract, and the contract mentioned what penalties
should be collected if the stuff supplied did not
come up to standard. Presumably, since the soli-
citors say so, that is the law ; but if it is the law,
the sooner it isn’t the better. If the Destitute
Asylum bosses, or the bosses of any other lodging-
house, choose to agree with a milk-purveying
shark that he shall pay 2d. fine if he supplies
three parts water and five parts milk, there seems
no good reason why the law should look at the
2d., and say “That settles it,” and crawl
back to its hole. If two men fight, having pre-
viously agreed that whoever kills the other one shall
pay the widow 4d. and call the thing square, the
law isn’t in the habit of crawling helplessly into
its kennel in the event of the 4d. falling due.
And the milk case is exactly the same sort of case,
only the murder is a bit more gradual and a lot
more contemptible.

Charles Ross, who slipped over at Bacchus
Marsh (Vic.) a day or two ago, deserves a line.
He was an engineer, and (so the record goes) drove
the first rotary engine on a steamboat on the
Clyde, Scotland, more than a half-a-century back.
In ? 52 became to Victoria, and took a leading
part in the construction of the Moorabool and
Melton viaducts on the Tait railways to Geelong
and Ballarat respectively. After that he doesn’t
appear to have done anything remarkable, so this
par will have to switch on to his mother, who was
alleged to have perished at the advanced age of
1052 years. She had the further distinction
of being the daughter of the carpenter who made
Nelson’s coffin. Considering the radiance of that
last assertion, the statement that Charles Ross
was 84 when the inevitable grasped him seems
hardly worth mentioning.

“The Truth,” Clyde Fitch’s comedy,
which was staged at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, last
Saturday night, is right up-to-date in the matter
of form. There is an absence of the dreary, old
potted epigram, and a naturalness of situation and
dialogue about it that charm us moderns, sur-
feited with the artificial stage-conventions of the
’eighties and early ’nineties. Still more charac-
teristic of our latitudinarian times is the kindly
tolerance of the playwright’s attitude towards
life. When Daudet’s “LaMenteuse” was done
into English, the lying Margot had to be made a
very unpleasant person indeed. Her allurements
were purely physical. She had told untruths to
her good, kind husband, and, finally, had been
unfaithful to him. In the end, Margot slew her-
self, and every right-thinking theatre-goer of
the mid-and-latter-
Victorian period
said “ Serve
her right.”
Clyde
Fitch
changes
all that.
He
shows a
woman,
young,
beautiful,
married to
a man who
adores her.
The husband
is well-off, res-
pected, and the
possessor of many
friends. The lucky
wife is not tired
of her paragon.
On the contrary,
she loves him as
much as she is
capable of loving
anyone. Yet she
lies to him—wan-
tonly and otherwise
—without stint, and compromises herself with
the husband of one of their mutual friends.
There is really no excuse for her. A Therese
de Megee condones her offences because she
loves with wild passion. But Becky Warder
is of the great army of superficial women; she
has no passion in her composition. It is a
notable tribute to Clyde Fitch or to Margaret
Anglin, or to our own understanding charitable-
ness, that we come away from “The Truth”
well satisfied that no less than justice has been
done when Becky, after a few hours’ worry, the
fruit of her manifold naughtiness, is wrapped in
her husband’s forgiving arms, and thereafter led
back to the comfortable home in which we first
met her.

do with superb assurance and ease. In a role charged
with emotion audiences get carried out of them-
selves very often (where the player has genius),
and aren’t able to watch the machinery working.
In “The Truth,” the onlooker may observe the
portrayerof Becky, the unsympathetic, with a more
or less coldly analytical eye. She uses no gesture
or intonation that hasn’t been brought to the pitch
of absolute justness by hours of thought on the
part of one with an intimate knowledge of
Beckies.
* * * *
Henry Kolker does good work as Warder,
the distracted husband. It is hard to conceive
anything more sincere and convincing than his
handling of the scene where he convicts Becky of
duplicity. The part of Roland —Becky’s deplor-
able father—is a fine one for Titheradge, and that
sound actor takes his opportunity with both
hands. He makes the raffish, mendacious old
villain live before our eyes in all his unpleasant-
ness, and yet in some intangible way we are given
to understand that Roland isn’t such a hopelessly
bad sort as not to merit some of the affection
Becky accords him. It is a notable performance.
Susie Vaughan plays Mrs. Crespigny, a boarding-
housekeeper, excellently, and Miss Alma
Vaughan is good as the jealous wife.

“Endorsing” was the chorus of the
Kilties’ Band welcome when it reached
Sydney by the Marama on Monday. Mr.
G. H. Reid made a speech at them, over
them, and about them at the Town Hall,
and Mr. O’Sullivan endorsed all he had said
about the hardy Scotch character and the
musicianly nature of Sandy Macbagpipe and the
constancy of his passion for Mrs. Bagpipes. He was
proud to welcome such a fine squad of musicians,
and was glad the two giant drum-majors
weren’t Cliffs, and that he hadn’t to scale them, or
words to that effect. Mr. ,J. S. Larke, Canadian
Commissioner, was the next endorsee. He en-
dorsed all the able and flattering remarks of the
last speaker, and gave this country a good
character for kindness to long-haired Russian
pianists and short-kilted landsmen. Conductor
Allen Cook, in reply, was only checked by a
mountainous quantity of modesty from endorsing
everything everyone had said. In the evening
the Highland Society threw up its bonnet in
welcome. Mr. F. Shipman announces the first
concert for August 15 at the Town Hall.

That the drawing power of Marcus Clarke’s
“For the Term of His Natural Life” is still
mighty was demonstrated by the fact that Mac-
Mahon and Carrol had to turn good hard cash
away in heaps from the doors of the Queen’s Hall,
Sydney, where they are rendering that story by
means of the cinematograph. The story has
been, of course, severely edited, but the boiling-
down was evidently done by a man who knew his
business, for the continuity of the yarn is quite
unbroken. The pictures are good, especially the
seascapes, such as the burning of the Hydaspes, and
the voyage of Rufus Dawes, Lieutenant Freer and
Sylvia in the fearful and wonderful boat made by
Dawes. The films are all excellently done, and
have the added attraction of being wholly of Aus-
tralian origin. They prove that as moving
picture builders Australians are not eclipsed by
the best French and Yankee filmers. It is a
stirring show. The only fault the writer has to
find with it is the interpolation of an illustrated
song in the gaps. The illustrated song is out of
date, and should be dropped overboard.
* -* *
In the complete absence of any news to
the contrary, it is assumed that “Miss Hook of
Holland ” will last at Sydney Criterion till the
great United States Drunk is over and the Fleet
has passed away. There is no need for bait in this
cash. The ungarnished Hook is sufficient attrac-
tion.

At Sydney Palace, West’s Pictures com-
prise the best set of films shown by that manage-
ment up to date—which is saying a great deal in
few words. There is a Shakspearan revival, and
Hamlet, Prince of Denmark, sees the most
realistic ghost that ever urged a devoted son to
hear a tale of horror. The “properties” in the
cinematograph rendering of the play are on a
scale of splendor that would have made Sarda-
napalus green with envy, if that worthy monarch
had any envy in his composition. “The Silver
King ” is also shown, and the crowd rises up
and demands encores with wild shouts. That is
another advantage of the moving picture. There
are no encores. The film, having filmed, goes not

Spencer’s Theatrescope, at Sydney Lyceum,
continues to supply instruction and other sub-
stances. A humorous film is the Duel with
Dynamite. On a Parisian boulevarde an ex-
quisitely tailor-costumed young woman is led to
an open-air cafe by her admirer. He goes
within, presumably to order the drinks, and a
perky young Parisian in a fearsome check suit
fixes his monocle on the fair lady. He ogles. She
takes no notice. He seizes a chair and progresses
towards her by a series of frog-like leaps, taking
his chair with him. He tries to introduce him-
self, but is repulsed. Then the stalwart cavalier
emerges, and there is much ruffling of feathers,
which finishes with an interchange of cards.
Next morning one party arrives at the killing
ground in a motor-car, the other in a carriage.
Rapiers are handed out, but no damage is done.
Broadswords are next tried, and each man gets
nicked in the neck, but honor is unsatisfied.
Pistols are used, but nobody is killed.
Then the onlookers, weary of the business, fit a
motor-car petrol tank on each man’s back, fill it
with high explosives, and hand each man a torch,
After that they depart hurriedly while the duel-
ists chase one another, each striving to light the
fuse that sticks out from the other man’s back.
Both get alight, and soar sky ward in pieces with
a eatherine-wheel motion. Eventually they come
down just in front of the motor car and carriage,
and shake hands, and all is peace.
* * * ■*
Since Austin Rudd last sang at Sydney
Tivoli, seven years have winged heavily into the
Past and roosted mournfully in the fowlhouse of
Time. It doesn’t seem as long as that, but a
daily paper makes the assertion in all seriousness,
and when the daily paper says a thing solemnly
Doubt is crowded out. So this scribe reluctantly
concludes that the seven years have really been
devoured by the locust and that he is growing
old. Rudd seems to be much the same as when
last he handed out his imported brand of humor,
and his stage-red nose has lost none of its
former brilliancy. The Miles Stavordale banjo
and harp magicians and Mignonette Kokin
are the other two first rank turns of the bill. Of
the second division, Regan and Ryan, Baby
Watson, Fanny Powers, and the Gibson Girls are
the most deserving of the gods’ shrill whistle of
approbation. Galetti’s baboons arouse great en-
thusiasm by pounding on the door of a closed pub
in a very life-like manner. Mankind keenly
the sight of one of the lower animals
imitating him in one of his own most animal
moments.
** * #
The Herbert Flemming co., supported
not so well as it deserved by the public (for
which Flemming lectured the public with pained
surprise when farewelling), has finished its season
at Adelaide Royal, with successive performances
of “The Morals of Marcus,” “Simple Simon,”
and “Dr. Wake’s Patient.” As a play, “Marcus”
easily eclipses the other two. It even preserves
much of the literary charm of the novel. It is
superbly played by Miss Beatrice Day, Miss
Guildford-Quin, and Homewood. This co. is
very strong. Three different men have played the
lead, and there are about three more who are fully
capable of it. “ Mrs. Wiggs ” has now moved in.
The players prove to be better-looking than
the singularly unattractive posters make them
out. The play is richer in “characters”
than in plot; but observation of human
nature is oddly mixed with sheer carica-
ture. The Tivoli, with good juggling and acro-
batic turns, has introduced some needed variety
into its Variety programme, and West’s pictures
at the Town Hall have been showing the Svdney
tramway trouble and other comic and "tragic
happenings.
* * * *
“Simple Simon,” by Murray Carson and
Nora Keith, making its first Australian appear-
ance at Adelaide Royal, proves to be one of the
thinnest play-fabrics imported for a long time.
It must have been the name-part, the character
of the strong, quiet man, that commended it to
Flemming. But the authors (who write like
clever amateurs) have not been able to show the
strength they intend. Simon is readily deceived
by a girl, he deliberately leaves no address while
away on a three-months’ honeymoon, and when
his gigantic business goes to smash as a result, he
only saves himself by a loan from the Jews at the
last moment. In fact, the save is effected between
the eleventh and twelfth strokes of noon ; half-a-
second later, and the chance would be gone.
Simon’s one strong act is his decision—on finding
that the girl who is engaged to him has told a
very natural fib about never having been
engaged before—to still marry her, to enjoy the
long honeymoon aforesaid, and then to blast her
young life by calling her a liar and leaving her.
The idea is as stagey as the long soliloquy in
which it is announced. The whole play needs a
resolute critic with a large, blue pencil (or a large,
blue axe), there being quite 50 per cent, too much
talk everywhere; further, Flemming’s own de-
lightfully easy, natural manner is developing into
an over-deliberation that tends to make things
drag. The best thing in the piece is the character
of the former lover, a well-meaning sportsman
without any backbone—the kind who can only
keep straight with a good woman’s hand to sup-
port him. Homewood’s playing of this part
makes the occasion a personal triumph for him.
Miss Day—to whom nothing seems to come amiss
—is artistic as the careless heroine ; and Miss
Mortyne and others give a more than adequate
support.
## * *
The rush of business to the King’s Theatre,
Melbourne, since “The Squatter’s Daughter”
was revived,-should suggest to Bent the advisa-
bility of appointing, on behalf of the Vic. Educa-
tion Department, a commission of inquiry into
the matter. Does the scenery, or the title, or the
bushrangeous character of Bailey and Duggan’s
great Australian circus-drama chiefly account for
its shocking popularity ? Would it be more popu-
lar if Ben Hall were to kill Geo. Cross and marry
the heroine in the last act? Or, on the other
hand, would five extra rows of reserved
stalls be urgently demanded if the tem-
pestuous bush girl (Miss Guildford) were to
carry a bag of snakes around and incite the largest
specimen to bite Ben Hall to slow music ? Death
by snakebite has not yet been introduced into this
country’s classic drama, neither has a log-chop-
ping contest, nor a milking tournament, as far as
the present scribe can remember. But they are
possibilities of the near future. Messrs. Bailey
and Duggan, it is said, have completed another
drama, to be entitled “The Man from Snowy
River,” and this will bristle with startling
national effects. Meanwhile “ The Squatter’s
Daughter ” continues to educate the public taste,
and matinees for the convenience of ladies and
children are happening on Wednesdays.
A revival of “Peter Pan,” at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), confirms the first impression it
made upon receptive minds. It has its weak
moments, and its moments which are almost
great in their expression of poetical sentiment.
If the performance in some respects falls more
short of absolute and impossible perfection than
could be wished, it is quite superlatively good as
regards the Pirate Captain and much of the
artistic burlesque which radiates from his com-
manding presence. Moreover this is late in the
day to find fault with “ Peter Pan ” in
detail, whereas no time is too late for remark-
ing that such delicately-fanciful productions
are things to be spoken of with gratitude—
with more gratitude than they are likely to get.
The large number of Melbourne people who
stayed away from Her Majesty’s on Saturday
night have mostly come to the conclusion that
one helping of J. M. Barrie’s “creation” was all
they wanted. And yet this is essentially the
kind of novelty which any playgoer should sample
twice, to see whether it amounts to an acquired
taste. There is only one more week for inquiry
into this matter. Saturday after next Miss
Anglin and Melbourne’s dear old friend Tither-
adge will appear in “The Thief.”

Melbourne Princess’s is getting a week’s
rest between the departure of “ Mrs. Wiggs ” and
the arrival of “The Prince Chap.” Last Bulletin'
stated that H. R. Roberts and his own particular
comedy drama would come to light on Saturday,
but the exact date of the revelation was left open
to dispute. This Saturday (15th) is the day
appointed, and the time is 8 p.m., by which
hour the ladies in the audience are respectfully
desired to have their hats off.
** * *
Afe Melbourne Royal, “Two Little Sailor
Boys ” continue to love one another, and the cry
of “Muvver!” is as superabundant as before.
Indeed, “Muvver!” punctuates the tale like the
eerie “Hallelujahs!” of a Salv’army festival.
Thus every properly brought-up person yearns
fervidly for the gore of Mrs. Lola Grey, who
carries no muvverly instincts in the far interior of
her embonpoint. Lola is uniquely heterodox in
that respect. She repels the reckless affection of
her own pallid kid, and even attempts to bury his
supplicating bleat in the cold waters of a property
stream. Such unnaturalness merely intensifies
the primeval desire aforesaid ; nevertheless, indig-
nation is tempered by the thought that her
blood would damage her clothes. A pity.
Such garb as she affects deserves not destruction,
but an elegy. It is expensive, almost Mord
Hildyardian, and seems the visible embodiment of
Miss Lilian Myers’ fat, fruity elocution. For, just
so much as that lady is inclined to make every
word a meal, so is she suspected of making every
frock an anthem. But there are other things
than clothes in the latest production at the Royal.
For instance, there is a burglar who belts his
adopted son for insubordination, and in this scene
Miss Maisie Maxwell’s performance makes ample
amends for her unnatural devotion to muvver.
** * *
The Grand Prix Motor Race is this week’s
flicker de resistance at West’s Picture show.
Photographed in Paris on July 7, the film reached
Melbourne on August 10, which was so fast a per-
formance that the management rushed it into
print and called it “A Record Kinomatogram,”
A motor race on the screen is peculiarly interest-
ing on account of the question it raises as to
whether the cars beat the photographic apparatus,
or the apparatus travelled faster than the winning
mote.
** * *
The Hambourg concerts collected the Mel-
bourne musical world in an uproarious, shouting
crowd, with Jovian Marshall-Hall in the lead. On
Tuesday night the pocket-Rubinstein showed off his
60-h.p. speed gear while rattling through the
“ Moonlight Sonata.” A Chopin item was mostly
unmixed delight (Hambourg has genuine feeling
for Chopin), until a polonaise went to his head
and drove him into a wild gallop. A barcarolle
of Rubinstein’s dripped off his fingers like gentlest
raindrops. “ A Midsummer Night’s Dream ”
(Mendelssohn-Liszt) was the real cross between the
gentle and thefiery that the two composers present.
Thursday’s concert opened with variations on
“ The Harmonious Blacksmith.” That pandering
to popular palates over, Mark got going on the
“Greek Sonata”—that icy classic of classical
Beethoven—and did not let up until he nearly
raised the roof with his audience’s enthusiasm.
Truly it was a fine thing. Some Grieg, a rippling
of Schumann and a crashing singing Liszt
polonaise padded out this programme satis-
factorily. Mark is, above all things, a master of
effect. If he were a violinist he’d play Paga-
ninni mostly—just as if he were an orchestra he
would love to let himself go in vivid scarlet
Dvorak.
** * *
Tom Burrows, the machine-like person
who waves two lumps of wood round his head for
half-a-week at a time, went to Her Majesty’s
Theatre, Ballarat (Vic.), last week, and started,
in the small, dark hours of Monday morning, to
break his previous dreary record of 65 hours
20 minutes. During the whole of the day only a
handful of people were curious enough to pay
an entrance-fee for the privilege of seeing
meat shaking firewood, and in the evening
the populace stayed away so brilliantly that the
misguided athlete was left flourishing his timbers
mostly at the doorkeeper. At 11 o’clock the
management, having counted the takings and
found they totalled £3, realised that Ballarat
didn’t care if Burrows swung his clubs for 65
months, and after parleying with the human wind-
mill through a haze or veil of whirling tree he was
persuaded to stop. Then the battered atmo-
sphere regained its senses and the lights were put
out.

One Tod Lowen, who is understood to write
the words of his own songs, chanted those original
compositions from the stage of Melbourne Opera
House on Saturday—and was forgiven. Lowen’s
humor is not highly intellectual, but its puerility
leans to quaintness. Also it avoids unfavorable
comparison with the more robust absurdities of
Bluett, who has just returned to his old haunts.
Meymott is. burgling his wife’s jewels for some
more last nights, and the Arabs are as remarkable
as usual.
PETER PAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Peter Pan and Tinker Bell
Came from where the fairies dwell—
Thus it was these things befell.
Peter Pan an elfin wight,
Tinker Bell a ball of light.
O’er the mountains, gay with heather,
Flew one day to town together.
“Yonder,” chirped a giddy starling,
“ Is the home of Wendy Darling.”
Wendy lay in bed a-sleeping :
Peter waked her with his weeping.
Bright as dew on sunflecked meadow
Peter’s tear drops shone.
All because he’d dropped his shadow,
Wendy stitched it on.
“ I’m the boy,” said Peter Pan,
“ Who refused to be a man.
Fly with me, and bring your brothers,
-To the Boys who’ve got no mothers
In the land afar—
Land of Never, Never, Never :
No one there is old or clever;
Fly with me and live for ever
Where the children are.”
Fast as Fancy’s wings could carry,
To the Never Land of Barrie,
Which is far beyond Belief
(Million miles from Pain and Grief),
Off they flew r , and lived awhile
Guarded by a crocodile.
In whose tender thorax sticking
A ten-year clock was loudly ticking.
(N.B. —The bard invites a smile
By terming it a clockodile.)
Gaunt, hungry wolves gave over snarling
When told it worried Wendy Darling;
Grim Pirates who had threatened slaughter,
Met sudden deaths by sword or water ;
And roaring lions turned and ran
When gazed upon by Peter Pan.
Things in the Never Land went well—
Except with jealous Tinker Bell.
The parents she had left behind
Passed out of Wendy’s fickle mind,
She having found maternal joys
In “ mothering ” a mob of boys.
Then came to Peter Pan, alack !
The thought that he must take her back—
Back to the weary world of lies,
Where little boys grow old and wise.
And now, together with her brothers,
And all the twins who wanted mothers,
She lives in town, where, sad to say.
She’s doomed to wear her life away.
But when the birds and blossoms sing
Their song of love in vernal Spring,
The woman who is on the wane
Becomes a careless child again,
And flies away to Peter Pan,
The boy who would not be a man..
For flesh must die and turn to mould,
But Love refuses to grow old.
AT POVERTY POINT.
John Archibald Crain (otherwise “Jack” Crain), a
writer of many theatrical reminiscences and other
"o°d matter in Tub Bulletin, passed out last
week. He was a Caledonian Irishman, his mother
hailing- from Glasgow—she was of old Covenanter
■descwit and his father from Victor Haley’s countv of
Armagh. He was learning the civil engineering profes-
sion in the Clyde-side city when he made the luckless dis-
covery that he had a fine tenor voice, and thereafter he
abandoned all hope of a steady and remunerative pro-
fession, and became a Bohemian on the face of the earth.
Instead of investing his little pile in a whelk-stall or a
smithy, or some such remunerative biz, he went to Milan
and learned all in the way of singing that it had
to teach. Riccardi and Steffani were his fellow-
students in the old Italian city. When he was
finished with Milan, Crain went back to England to lift
his voice for a cash consideration, but he had
grown used to a better climate, and presently he fled in
dismay to Auckland (M.L.). Here he struck Riccardi
and Charles Harding, and out of the meeting <r re w
Riccardi’s Comic Opera Co., and from that evolved
Harding and Crain’s Opera Co. Among the many things
■done by “Jack" in that connection the fact that he
created (and created exceedingly well) the part of Ralph
Rackstraw in “Pinafore” is best remembered. He lost
his money in the firm ; it sang well and acted well and
did its business management lamentably. He then went
to head a new venture in Tasmania, whence he was sum-
moned in haste to Sydney to support the great Oarlotta
vatti. He was a leading tenor in Sydney for a long time,
till a lung trouble robbed him of his voice. After that he
pegged in manfully at journalism. He was one
of the props of L’ltalo-Austra-liano, and in
between times he contributed paragraphs, theatri-
cal reminiscences and humorous items to The
Bulletin and other papers. For quite a number
() years his health was bad and his luck was none too
good, but he was always cheerful, and showed a brave
front to the world. A big, happy, lumbering wreck of a
man, walking with difficulty (he suffered from a complica-
tion of troubles in his later years), coughing with diffi-
culty, yet never taking a despondent view of the world—
that was “Jack "Crain as most people recollect him. He
was appointed editor of a new venture, White Australia,
but pneumonia struck him before the first number was
re j j’ ke * n Sydney Hospital—still cheerful,
and devising features for his new paper till the very last.
~ ” : H. Rickards had some impressive folk on
his Melbourne programme just lately. First, there was
Austin Rudd, a rather fat little man, who warbled of
forty young ladies in their—ahem 1 nighties’’—with an
expression of arch significance. He had also something
to say about bathing and beer, and broke the audience
up by his interpretation of those two nice, fresh subjects.
Then there were four damsels—the Four Soapsuds, or
Be th'hg—who endeavored to be genial against fearful
odds. Their passion in life is seemingly a lady named
Lou, who is urged to “love me, do!”—a sad yearning
uitty altogether, the ineffableness of which is emphasised
by giddy kicks audiencewards. Later on, they declared
that they were “ the coquettesof the Strand,” which quite
a number of people refused to believe without affidavits
to that effect. But the most original creature was one
Leonard Nelson. Leonard fled on to the premises in a
false beard, old pants vvith bowyangs, shirt and hat. His
hand clasped an antiquated micky. Quoth Leonard,
with conviction, “The dawn is breaking,’’and so that
there should be no doubt about such a reckless state-
ment, some unfortunate being had to drag a heap of iron-
mongery very noisily from one wing to the other, where
the noise ended with a crash. Said Leonard, with ter-
rible meaning, “ The dawn has broke,” or “ broken,”
whereat three boys in the gallery laughed audibly.
Leonard made other amazing declarations with similar
means of illustration, and generally achieved an artistic
triumph. There were other people on the bill, and some
of them treated the populace to a stream of equally
ferocious fatuity. One meets .with terrible experiences
at a theatre of variety.
“Akenehi”: The interviewers in London have been
telling the great donkey-eared British Public that
the Butts have been telling them, that the devoted
pair’s bank balance grew to the extent of £51,000
through the Australian tour. One can’t, of course,
say “Rats!” because “Rats!” is rude; but—well,
the Rumford bank balance must grow amazingly
during a sea voyage. The pair gave 75 concerts, or
thereabouts, in these sunshiny parts and in Maoriland ;
and 75 into £51,000 used to be £6BO and nothing over if
they teach arithmetic like they used to when I wore
ankle-strap shoes and val. lace on my pinnies. The
average price paid to hear the great Butt was certainly
not more than Bs. (amazing the number of people who
don’t pay anything at all that one finds in the places
marked “ This lot one guinea ”). Then, even for the gross
receipts to reach £51,000, the 75 concerts would have re-
quired an average paying attendance of 1600 or 1700 ; and
when you remember that the contralto boomed at Albury
and Sale and such places, vou know that average
is just impossible. Sydney Town Hall certainly
holds 3000 people; but, even if vou pack it, only
2200 people can get colds in Melbourne Town Hall at
once ; and from that eminence the seating accommoda-
tion of Australian halls topples down furiously. A liberal
estimate puts the average attendance at 800 for the 75
concerts, or 60,000 altogether ; and if, after paying rent,
lighting, and other expenses, the pair cleared even ss. a
head, the total would still be only £15,000. That’s what
must be wrong—the interviewers really didn’t think it
mattered whether they put the 1 first, or the 5. But it
will matter a lot to those managers who by-and-bye will
tr.V and snare some other great foreign artist to Aus-
tralia, and who will be met with, “Oh, nonsense ! The
Butts made £51,000 out there.” Whereupon the manager
will either have to regretfully decline business, or attempt
to squeeze out of Australians the same shockingly high
prices that have been charged on a few occasions lately.
“0.K.”: The Bulletin dropped into a natural con-
fusion of names when it referred to the conductor of
Williamson’s musical comedy orchestra as Harold Carr.
The tall young man in question is Howard Carr. Never-
theless, he has so often been addressed as Harold that
he accepts the misnomer with all the courtesy due to the
inevitable. There is a Harold Carr in the musical line.
The two men are not of one family, but their baptismal
names are near akin. Such names are rocked in couples
in the cradle of one’s memory, and one easily mixes them
up.

“The Truth” doesn’t contain a single epi-
gram. I would like to seek out the man Fitch, and wring
his hand in an ecstasy of gratitude. I am sick of
epigrams. They began to weary me slightly about ten
years ago. I am a gregarious soul who yearns to be
m accord with his fellow man, and hearing various
liars of my acquaintance protest stoutly that they
loved this form of parley, I struggled against my growing
hatred of it. In vain. With each new comedy I saw I
loathed the epigram and its badly-trained stable com-
panion, the paradox, more and more. I took to
frequenting (a) melodramas, (b) music halls, (c) prize
?%• Islmll never, forget the night I heard the villain
in The Horrible Wedding,” who was being urged tearfully
by an elderly moralist from Mangel-Wurzelshire to make
an honest woman of his most recent victim, reply cyni-
cally flicking the ash from his cigarette: “Marriage is
the last insult one offers to a woman one resnects ” Amid
the storm of hoots that followed I left. The thino- has
crept insidiously into the music-hall of late years' “I
cannot afford to tale in washing”; “I get drunk on
gm for the sake of the Higher Morality,” etc., etc Such
painful quips issue nightly from red-nosed, zebra-strined
outrages at every variety show. “Larry ” Foley ground
out a batch very little brighter than the above at the
recent reception to Bill Squires; and the first thing
Noah Brusso Burns said to the Sydney Telegraph
interviewer was that “ he had never hurt a man in his
life. The success of Fitch’s epigramless plav encourages
me. I think this form of humor is doomed. * b
The three Russian musicians, Jan, Leo, and Mischel
Cherniavaski, whom Edward Branscombe has engaged
for an Australian tour, will introduce themselves to this
country at Melbourne Town Hall on the 19th September
The date is a point in their favor. Musical ventures in Mel
bourne have for years been suffering from the weather
and if managers are well advised, they will in future
treat June, July, and August as a close season for Town
Hall entertainments. People in general are indisposed to
go forth on damp nights to catch cold in the draughtv
municipal barn and wilderness. ® J

HIS FAVORITE PART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His name is Harold Montague,
He is a leading juvenile ;
His photographs are all on view—
They reproduce his famous smile,
His well-known dimple, traced with care,
And his distinguished head of hair.
He is the rage in tea-rooms gay,
And milliners adore his looks ;
White-workers talk of him all day,
And nurses love him—so do cooks.
By hook or crook, by day or night,
This man is never out of sight.
Upon the stage, I must admit,
Hear Harold’s work is only slight.
He poses much, and sings a bit,
And hustles smartly for the “ light.”
Perhaps to make so much ado
In doing nought needs talent too.
But in the street, at parties, and
At dances he is quite unique.
The moments when he’s truly grand
Are those of which no critics speak.
His masterpiece of acting true
Is Mr. Harold Montague !
vie.

SOCIETY
PENILD'S
WINES
It is five years now since the Victorian
railway strike, but to this day the blackleg driver,
as a rule, drives his train along in dead silence,
and his union fireman shovels coal in dead silence,
and there is no speech between them. The curse of
being a “scab ” is a curse that will never be lifted.
For this reason, the Sydney tramway depart-
ment did its best for a while to put a blackleg driver
and a blackleg conductor together, even as two
lepers might be put on one island to prevent the
other leper feeling lonelj' or singular; but
now, according to all accounts, most of the
blackleg drivers have been reduced, by
reason of their incapacity, to conducting. Report
says that the official tests have been practically
suspended as regards the blacklegs ; when the
testing process was first started they went out
wholesale for inability to come up to the
standard, so the painful question was dropped or
postponed. Report also alleges that any new bags
that may be in use are carried by old conductors,
and that the new blackleg conductor always has
an old bag. Some people have an idea that a
conductor’s bag is his own property, and that the
new men brought nice fresh bags into the service ;
so the present method —that is how the popular
story has it —saves the new men from opprobrium,
and lets any opprobrium that may be floating
around fall on ; the old men. If these reports are
true they suggest a fair amount of ingenuity on the
part of somebody.

An aged petitioner in Melbourne Divorce
Court, last week, was remarkable for being the
brother-in-law of his own daughter-in-law. He
and son Job had married two sisters, but whereas
Job’s wife is still living with her husband, the
sister who paired off with Job’s father has proved
a faithless baggage. She lived with her old man
for a week or so, ’way up in Alexandra (Vic.), after
which he went away to get work and ruminate.
He was 64, and a weary toiler at that, when he
riveted his affections upon Mary, who was 19.
Probably his exile was saddened by reflections
upon the disparity in their ages. But after a
week’s absence he returned to his bride, and she
kicked him until he fled. Then she threw stones
at his retreating form. Subsequently she broke
him up with a poem of dismissal, beginning with
“Go and leave me, I don’t want you.” The
moral is very pointed. No gentleman on the
shady side of 60 should marry the youthful sister
of his son Job’s wife.
Chinese furniture-making bosses swagger
about confidently since the publication of John
Hoe’s declaration in S. M. Hkualix John Hoe
voiced Sydney Chinadom in his.shriek about the
high wages paid by the John community, and Chow
firms repeat his challenge to examine men and
books. That examination, they maintain, will
prove the Chow boss pays higher wages and works
his men shorter hours than does the white boss in
the furniture line. A similar challenge, thrown
out in Melbourne, led to the appearance of Quoon
Lee and Co. before a Bench of magistrates
lately, on a charge under the Factories
Act, and Barrister Purves, who represented
the Chow employers, blandly stated certain illumi-
nating facts. He admitted that the Act pre-
scribed 565. per week for workers, but held on
behalf of his clients that it didn’t say when that
amount was to be paid. In Quong Loon’s case it
seemed liable to become a good many years over-
due. On that basis the Chow furniture-maker
boss can very easily pay (or owe) higher wages
than the white man, and can produce books of a
certain sort to hack up the statement. It might
be worth while to test John Hoe’s brag about the
wages of Chowdom in Sydney.

The Sydney egg trade broke into public
notice through a deputation of poultry raisers who
waited on Minister Perry to complain of the way
their bran and pollard was being adulterated.
The conversation strayed on to eggs, and the de-
putation spoke violently about the 2,000,000 of
hen output that were allegedly brought over from
Adelaide last year and sold to “ the trade” at Id.
to 3d. a dozen. Of course, the long-distance egg
isn’t likely to be quite so fresh as the short-range
egg, and the threepence-a-dozen-egg is always
open to suspicion, and no one expects any eggist
to take a charitable view of the rival egg pub-
lished by his loathsome hentemporary. But even
making all allowances, the statement that the
disreputable elderly harridan of an egg is broken
and put through a fine sieve to prevent the
chicken from getting into the pastry is a stagger-
ing sort of a remark.

The imperturbable Chinee was again in
evidence on the N.D.L. steamer Prinz Walde-
mar at Sydney last week. Suspecting stow-
aways, the Customs people had ransacked the
vessel without success. But Donohue, the old dog
of the staff, wasn’t satisfied. He had had a “ tip,”
and knew it was a good one, though all his search-
ing had revealed nothing. So, at midnight, after
all the Chinese crew were asleep, Donohue and a
companion made their way unannounced into the
engine room. Result, nil. They probed about at
great length, and were just about giving it up
when attention was directed to what looked
like heaps of mere dust and grease on
top of the boilers. Under this were nine
Chows. They had lain motionless in their
filthy bed for at least 24 hours, and when dragged
out could hardly be recognised as human. One of
the reasons for the Chinaman’s success as a stow-
away is his capacity for remaining quite motion-
less for about a week at a stretch, and passing
himself off as coal or dirt, or some such old sub-
stance of no importance.

Vic. Insolvency Judge Moule lately
pointed out the straight and narrow path to that
State’s prospective and possible insolvents. When
Timothy O’Hanlon went bung, after 12 months
in a store at Lilydale, he owed some £270 ; and,
after selling his business, his assets totalled about
£lBO. Having paid £2O worth of local debts in full,
O'Hanlon picked up his household goods and his
family, and went to Melbourne to talk to his
Yarra-side creditors. But being on the way to see
one of them next morning, with £3B
in his pocket, he happened across
a friend wdio was simply dying to
take him out to the races and
put him on a good thing for
the Ascot Cup. Mr. O’Hanlon
went to the races, backed
the good thing, and re-
turned, home with £75 in his
pocket. That, as Judge
Moule remarked, settled him
completely. What were a
few pounds to him who
could make £2O or £3O a
day by backing “ good
things”? Making no fur-
ther attempt to see his
creditors, he boldly went to
Flemington, to Caulfield,
Epsom, Williamstown, and
other courses, until all the
money had disappeared. After
informing Timothy that he had
started business with insufficient
capital, had kept his books in a non-
descript fashion, and had withdrawn
capital for personal expenses with-
out making any attempt to replace
it. Judge Moule quoted sec. 141 of
the Insolvency Act, which deals
with people who gamble with the
only means they have for paying their
creditors. And then, having quoted his
text, his Honor preached a brief sermon to
the effect that Timothy must go without a certi-
ficate, but could take two months’ gaol instead.
When Judah goes in for philanthropy, it
sometimes grows very reckless, but seldom over-
looks its Nose. Nose is in everything it takes up.
And the Jewish Aid Society of Melbourne is a
typically Nosey institution. It isn’t an aid
society in the Gentile sense. It gives nothing,
unless it is sick or drunk ; but it £ends. And it
doesn’t Send blankets, soap, or picture-postcards.
It Sends money to the more indigent Noses. It
has been doing that for 20 years very obscurely
and without any blare, and alleges that 1354 cases
have been dealt with to the tune of £lB,OOO. It is
a fairly substantial tune, and almost amounts to
an anthem, and it would be a whole choral society,
perhaps, if any information were supplied about
the interest. It can hardly be that a Jewish Aid
Society lends money without interest.
HOW FAR?
[For Tub Bulletin.]
The Legislative Investigation Committee, sitting at
Alabama, elicited some remarkable evidence respecting
the State convict leasing system practised by the Southern
States. It was shown that at one State prison farm con-
victs were lined up and sold like mules to the highest
bidder. One convict was leased out in exchange for
seven negroes because he possessed clerical ability. A
white boy, who was leased, was whipped to death for
spilling coffee on a dog.— United States cablegram.
The calm stars shine upon the lifeless clay,
And glazing eyes that mutely plead to God
For vengeance for a young life done away
Because, o’er duty, sense had dared to nod !
From his weak frame the ruthless lash has shorn
The bonds that held his spirit down to earth ;
And now he lies in death not more forlorn ' ,
Than he had lived forlornly,since his birth.!
The news is flashed by cable from afar !
He spilt some coffee on a dog, this slave,
And now he’s dead ! That’s all! His lucky’ star
At long last beariis upon his new-dug grave !
No brutal blow can maim him any more !
He lies there huddled —lifeless as a log !
Oh. yes, he’s dead, my worthy 7 masters, for
He chanced to spill some coffee on a dog !
The calm stars shine ; we muddle smugly 7 on,
And, from these levels where to-day we are,
Gaze cold contempt upon dark ages gone,
And say 7 we have progressed ! Good Lord, how far ?
MAORILAND. SIXC. S. CLARK.

In Fiji they have a leaven of gentle Indian
in the population. (There are about 17,001)
Indians to 97,000 Fijians and whites.) The
following from the Western Pacific Herald
(10/7/’08) shows how the Indian compares in the
matter of crime with the rest of the population
The July criminal sessions will commence before his
Honor thediief Justice on 21st instant. The following
is the calendar Ghul Mahomed (Indian), attempted
murder, Ba ; Azad Khan (Indian), escaping from' gaol,
Ba; Gungar (Indian), murder, Macuata; Khubi
(Indian), murder, Rewa; Alivoreti Vodre (Fijian),
attempted suicide, Kadavu ; A. Q. McGowan, offence
under Bankruptcy Act, Suva ; Uraia Tabuarua (Fijian),
murder, Suva; Ramanath (Indian), escape from lawful
custody, Suva; Mahabir Singh and Naraijan Singh
(Indians), setting fire to a dwelling-house, some persons
being therein ; Budhu and three others (Indians), Ba,
robbery with violence ; Sultan Singh and three others
(Indians), burglary, Ba ; Aladat and Nathan (Indians),
murder, Navua : Ruveni Rokoita (Fijian), carnal know-
ledge, Rewa ; Ramcharan (Indian), Navur, murder;
Tulsi Ram (Indian), murder, Ba.
Total, 19 Indians, three Fijians, and one white.
And the Hindu record is six murders, four bur-
glaries, four cases of robbery with violence, two
cases of arson, two of gaol-breaking, and one
attempted murder. A curiously violent person
our brownish fellow-subject seems to be. He is a
mere handful of the community, yet he seems to
provide 83 per cent, of the crime.
THE DANCE AND THE DEVIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Alderman Forsyth : I have no objection to the resi-
dents having amusement, but dancing I do object to.
Alderman Gonsalves : I think dancing is the best and
most moral sport there is on the face of God’s green
earth, and the man that does not indulge in dancing must
have something physically wrong with him, and ought to
see a doctor at once. —Yaucluse (Sydney) council debate.
When toothless Age goes skipping by
And smirks and bows before each tree,
Turn not a swift, suspicious eye
Nor think it is insanity ;
A wise man as he older grows
Insists he’s sound as any bell;
He treats with scorn all medicoes
And leaps about to show he’s well.
O ! drag the student from his book,
And chase professors from their chairs ;
Rejoice when sounds betray the cook
Is waltzing with the maid downstairs.
Urge all the Force to dance along
And agitate their mighty feet,
And with the lady who’s gone wrong
Do gay schottisches on the beat.
What though the Devil loves a waltz,
And threads the lancers’ merry maze?
I'd rather have him with his faults
Than those sad folk who spend their days
Bemoaning us. So, make your bow !
Come, seize the joy Life has in store !
I love to hear the joyous row
Of his gay hoofs upon the floor.
N.S.W.

There is pathos in everything, even in
George Robert Beere’s rambling address from the
dock at Melbourne General Sessions, after he had
been convicted of obtaining postcards by false
pretences. For example -
I was to have been married to a girl who came in for
£2OO, and she would have given me whatever money I
wanted. If the Court passes sentence upon me her life
will be ruined as well as mine. I could never marry her.
The wedding bells would not ring out. They will rather
ring out a death knell to our hopes. The clergyman had
been engaged, and only the ceremony remained to be
performed to make two loving hearts one. But now our
joy has been turned to sorrow, and all for the small sum
of 6s. 6d. For twelve months past I have been teaching
in a Sunday-school. ... I ask your Honor to temper
justice with mercy. I have spent mv life in rescuing
others, and now I shall be sent to prison, and I suppose
that the hope I had of seeing the American Fleet will be
dashed to the ground.
Whereupon Judge Box smote him with a sentence
of 12 months hard labor.
Within the pale of Melbourne Gaol,
For fifty weeks or more,
George Robert Beere must shed the tear
Of bitterness galore.
With sympathy for such as he
The toughest bosoms beat.
Heigho ! if he were only free
To go and see the Fleet 1
Devoid of hope, he’s picking rope,
Or mooching round the yard ;
Securely caged, he is engaged
In doing “ 12 months hard.”
But when he falls asleep at night
His dreams are long and sweet;
Then comes a vision of delight—
A vision of the Fleet.
Can burly Bent withhold consent
To Brother Beere’s release ?
A little while from durance vile
Let him depart in peace.
The learned Judge will not begrudge
The prisoner this treat—
One week, one day, to go away,
And gaze upon the Fleet.

While Parson Nas h has become a fashionable
fad in Melbourne, Parson Gladstone, of the same
church, has lapsed into obscurity. Rash became
a burning sensation because of an alleged Past;
while Gladstone sputtered feebly like a wet
match, chiefly because he could or would not
seduce the filthy lucre of the Very Best People.
Whatever Nash did, it could not have been of less
heinousness than the thing 1 charg’ed to Gladstone;
but if press-booming and the screeching of pro-
and anti-Nashites are criteria, Nash is a
saint of unearthly spotlessness, while Glad-
stone, by contrast, is quite unimportant.
Little is said of Gladstone, who, the other
day, bloomed. into momentary publicity in
connection with his new Reformed Church of
England. The howl is all of Nash. And all
because Nash came from the pretentious, genteel
subbu bof Hawthorn, where the vineyard is streaked
with golden girl and fat old idolatrous ladies ;
while poor old Gladstone belted Scripture into
the country village of Nathalia, where the vine-
yard is either mortgaged or overrun with Cow.
It is a biting commentary on the influence of
Sassiety in Church matters.

Sydney s 41 pocket-hanky municipalities
are ambitious to shine in the costume line. Ash-
field, a pretentious little western suburb, has
gone in for a mayoral costume. In most of the
places where the costume mania exists the cos-
tume maniacs aim at something fluffy and furry,
like a cat’s tail; but Ashfield has decided on
“ black silk, old-gold facings, scarlet lining, and a
lace collar.” Marrickville. which is in the district
that elects Johnson, M.H.R.
(and therefore almost any-
thing might be expected
of it), has seriously con-
sidered the question of
mayoral robes and
aldermen in evening
dress. A tiny little
village called
Bankstown, near
Sydney, has a cos-
tumed mayor,
whose robes are
almost as gorgeous
as those of the
Lord Mayor of
London, and in-
clude a three-
cornered hat —it
may even go one
corner better, and
be a rectangular
hat. The Lord
Mayor of Sydney
has a chain added
to the hairy robes
of office, and the
sight of “Tom”
Hughes, the pre-
sent Lord Mayor
(who is a fine,
strong, healthy man, God bless him !), on a hot
summer’s night, with the temperature at 100°,
sweltering in his furs, and the sweat falling on the
barrister’s wig of the Town Clerk in front of him,
would move a stone to pity. Before long, these
41 twopenny-halfpenny civic cabbage-patches
will be asking for lord mayoralties. And then
Sydney will have 41 lord mayors and 41 little
Sydneys, all full of robes and cat’s tail and
overdrafts.
* * * *
The Sydney Clerks’ Union shows that, after
all, there are clerks with a backbone, and that
the clerk with a backbone is an energetic person.
The Clerks’ Union is not many months old, and
hasn’t nearly the membership it ought to have,
but its fighting record is good. It started opera-
tions by a protest against the wages paid to shire
and municipal clerks, and the publicity given to
the protest caused some shires to alter their
sweating ways. It made representations which
apparently secured redress for some solid griev-
ances by which clerks in the Post Office suffered ;
at all events, the grievances were remedied. And
it made revelations about sweating, over-
time, and breaches of the Factory Act in some
city houses, over which trouble is still threaten-
ing. Moreover, the agitation has caused an un-
wonted briskness in the Factory Act officers,
and led to a considerable change in many com-
mercial houses. The Union has done other
things, and at the present time is worrying various
people about the coercion exercised over clerks,
and the continual overtime worked in the Savings
Bank.
The captain and crew of the French barque
Max begin to be aweary of trapesing round the
Seven Seas to no purpose. The Max was despatched
from Havre to Hobart in Nov., 1907, for orders.
When she arrived at Hobart the orders sent her
on to ’Frisco for more orders. The more orders told
her to get back to Hobart for some further orders.
She called in at Sydney on the way for the further
orders the other day ; and later, while rounding
Green Cape, the elements took a hand and drove
her back to the Beautiful Harbor again. There
the skipper stopped and cabled his owners to get
him a charter or come and chase orders themselves.
It is one of the quaint facts of Private Enterprise
that it just loves to hump things to and fro, and
when it can’t get any goods to hump it just carts
itself back and forth.
One Melbourne citizen is saying most in-
sulting things about the local. Board of Works.
He and his wife were sleeping peacefully after a
placid Sunday’s r.est, when the water main out-
side burst, and.a cartload of. blue metal, cinders,
mud, and Yan Yean projected itself through the
window. They were a surprised pair for a few
minutes, but they managed to escape drowning ;
and while the wife sat on the cold back fence in her
Disher Bill, the housefather ran around, discovered
the turncock, and got the avalanche shut off. In the
morning he found his wife very ill, his furniture
and household accoutrements ruined, and his house
a water-logged wreck ; so he interviewed the
Board re compensation. But that Timber
remarked that under its Act it wasn’t
liable for any such damage unless negligence
could be shown, and as the citizen couldn’t prove
negligence the Board courteously wished him
good morning. The occurrence illustrates one
of the abiding joys of Melbourne life. The Board
of Shirks never dreams of i-enewing its pipes ex-
cept to insert a larger one where needed, or unless
one bursts—and then it only puts one length in.
Also most of the pipes are only a foot or two
underground, and when they crack, With several
thousand tons of water tearing things up, there is
a great sloppy mess. And some of the
pipes are reputedly about 50 years old,
so they ofterl crack. Afterwards, when any
aggrieved and muddy citizen talks about damages
and compensation, the Board is the essence of
courtesy, and carries on as though the citizen had
just acquired a fortune, or married a new wife, or
something.

Hindmarsh, one of the Adelaide suburbs
where they tan hides and make bricks and bad
smells, has been haying its back premises viewed
by Board of Health Officer Dr. Coombe, who also
looked in at a few butcheries. On the outside,
especially if you view them from the front,
Adelaide and suburbs look as clean as a bone after
11 lawyers have picked it ; but it isn’t always
safe to inquire within. Anybody, for instance,
can be a butcher; and the butcher around
Adelaide has his own slaughter-yard, which is
mostly stuck away in some remote place, too
objectionable to be used for anything else. ' Pok-
ing his nose into the Hindmarsh shambles, Dr.
Coombe found them “generally dirty, untidy, and
filthy, and the floors were covered with blood,
offal, etc., etc. The hooks on which the meat
was hung were dirty, rusty, covered with stale
blood and fly dirt.” And as for the shops :
Some plucks of sheep hanging in the shop were diseased
and unfit for human consumption.
Here some meat exhibited was really unfit for human
consumption.
Some of the meat exhibited was poor and of doubtful
class.
Some of the meat unfit for human consumption.
The health officer drops now and then into par-
ticulars ; but when it has been told that the hook
that the meat hangs on is filthy, and the floor that
the meat is dragged over is filthy, and the meat
that hangs on the filthy hook over the filthy floor
is diseased and unfit for human consumption,
Hindmarsh has probably heard enough to drive it
to a diet of potatoes. If potatoes are out of the
question, then it might almost be an improvement
to try boiled chaff.
* * * *
“ Mate,” re Courts of Marine Inquiry :
The gentlemen who are decorated with trimmings from
a defunct gee-gee are too fond of dropping heavily on the
unfortunate shipmaster who has made a mistake. They
sit in comfortable armchairs and have things explained
to them at great length for a great heap of hours. Then
they reserve judgment and think the matter out at
greater length, always in comfort. They come back and
announce that, there was a grave error of judgment, and
the master’s ticket is suspended for 12 months. He, most
likely, had to act first and do his thinking afterwards.
If a Judge had to listen to two lawyers—for opposite
sides—at the same time, and give his judgment before
they’d finished talking—knowing that his billet depended
on the judgment being correct—he might have a better
understanding of the conditions a shipmaster works
under.
When a case is reversed on appeal to a higher court,
nothing happens to the Judge who is proved to be
ignorant of his business. I propose that a Judge whose
judgment is reversed should have to pay the Costs of his
own incorrect decision and have his certificate suspended
for three months for bad navigation.
Gentlemen who wish to look smart, should
pay a visit to Wild’s hairdressing saloon, where six
first-class artists are engaged. The establish-
ment is under the Patronage of Lord Northcote.
Address, 30. Sydney Arcade (upstairs), entrance
from George or King-streets.*
A GERMY JINGLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
All telephones, whether in public or private, should be
periodically disinfected. There can scarcely be a more
suitable place for harboring organisms of all kinds than a
dark telephone call-box, with its stagnant air and dirty
floor, the dust of which is brought in from the street and
stirred up and inhaled by each fresh caller.— -News item.
The germs of life, the germs of death,
They everywhere abound
In atmosphere of human breath
And subtle waves of sound.
The germs of death, the germs of life,
Are all engaged in daily strife :
We cut them with a carving-knife
When dinner-time comes round.
We get them in a maiden’s kiss,
They float upon the breeze ;
They mingle in a serpent’s hiss,
And in a baby’s sneeze.
Those organisms, foul or clean,
Are ever present on the scene :
They save the King, or kill the Queen,
Who catches'a disease. /'
When sadness clouds our little lot,
Or pleasure fills our cup,
Because we’d rather live than not .
We breakfast, dine and sup.
To all who feed their flesh and bones
This rhymster calls in warning tones—
Go, disinfect your telephones,
Or Death will ring you up.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The State Treasury figures for July, the first
month of the financial year, have a damp, sodden look.
Compared with those for July, 1907, they gloom in this
way :
July, 1907. July, 1908.
N.S. Wales ..£1,126,286 £1,069,317 Dec. £56,969
Victoria .. 765,181 605,230 Dec. 159,951
Queensland .. 360,347 407,304 Inc. 46,957
S. Australia .. Too slow to produce monthly figures.
Westralia .. 190,005 161,619 Dec. 28,386
Tasmania .. 70,227 55,218 Dec. 12,009
The", comparison, however, makes things look really worse
than they are. Twelve months ago, in view of the ap-
proaching Lyne tariff, merchants loaded up, and there
was a flood of Customs receipts. The flood has long since
ebbed. The Commonwealth Customs revenue was, there-
fore, for last month only £1,018,158, as against £1,239,999
for July, 1907. The payments to the States show a still
larger shrinkage on account of the Commonwealth having
partially got out of its bad habit of starving itself in
order to provide the State Treasurers with surpluses.
Victoria received £152,963 less in July, 1908, than in
July, 1907, and N.S.W. £50,033 less. Prosperous Queens-
land, on the other hand, received £28,468 more.
The expenditure figures are, as usual, meagre :
July, 1907. July, 1908.
N. S. Wales .. £1,118,775 £1,026,242 Dec. £92,533
Queensland .. 190,715 255,723 Inc. 65,008
Westralia .. 201,910 209,379 Inc. 7,469
Queensland, however, doesn’t charge up its interest till
the end of the half-year. Westralia had a deficit of
£47,759 for the month—and Westralia is the State
whose Premier hifaluted the other day about the
enormous number of tons of gold and millions of
pounds of dividends that foreign cos. were carting
awa}\ N. S. Wales, having sacked a Treasurer because
of his frightful behavior in borrowing £2,500,000 a year,
has given up its drunken ways, and Reformed, and
now borrows at the rate of £2,300,000 a year. The
loan money bill for July was £190,047. It is much
like an old boozer taking the platform at a Y.M.C.A.
temperance meeting, and thanking the Lord that he is
a changed man, because nowadays he blows the froth
off before he swallows the same old amount of beer.

A Trust, representing the municipalities, built the tram
lines, and gave this co. a lease of them till July 1,1916-
eight years from now. The co. had to find the rolling-
stock, keep the lines in repair, pay interest on the Trust’s
borrowings, provide a sinking fund for the extinction of
the debt, and finally hand back the lines in good working
order. The sinking-fund contribution was graduated ;
till 1905 it was £33,000 a year, and since then it has been
£45,000 a year. The amount borrowed by the Trust was
£1,650,000 ; but, as the rate of interest was per cent.,
the loans were issued at a total premium of £55,794. The
co.’s own capital was £250,000 ; but a further £230,000 of
profit was capitalised. In the foregoing table the cal-
culations are made on the basis of the £250,000 actually
paid. Actually, therefore, the business has given the
following return during the past two years :
1907. 1908.
Profit as shown above .. .. £137,661 £148,315
Sinking fund for Trust .. .. 45,000 45,000
Trust’s debent, interest .. .. 74,250 74,250
, Total £256,911 £267,565
LesS int. on co.’s investments .. 25,598 26,190
Net revenue of undertaking .. £231,313 £241,375
The capital put in by the Trust was, as shown just now,
£1,705,794. The co. has apparently about £405,000 in
the business. Lumping the two together, representing
the whole capital of the undertaking, it will be found
that the return is about £ll Bs. 6d. per cent. That is to
say, Melbourne is charged for its tram-rides enough to
show a return of £ll Bs. 6d. per cent., while, according
to the last published report of the N.S.W. Railway Com-
missioners, the Ma State was only required to pay enough
to show a return of £4 .18s. Bd. per cent, on the Govern-
ment trams of N.S.W.
The reserves are made up as follows :
1906. 1907. 1908.
Capitalised profits .. £230,000 £230,000 £230,000
Renewal reserve .. 269,841 266,374 249,571
For return of capital .. 109,902 143,749 178,780
Dividend reserve .. 12,000 12,000 12,000
Investment depreciation 20,000 20,000 20,000
■Other depreciation .. 80,000 115,000 150,000
■Undivided profit .. 25,711 25,992 25,079
Total reserves .. £747,454 £813,115 £865,430
■Some of these reserves are not of the best character.
The renewal reserve, for instance, has been cut into
•during the past year. In replacements apparently
£24,117 was spent; of that, £7314, however, was charged
‘direct to working expenses, the balance coming out of
itbe^reserve. The lines, etc., have to be handed over in
igood condition; therefore, there may be a further
■call upon this reserve during the next eight years.
'Then there is the depreciation reserve. The co. has
ithese possessions (figures for 1901 and 1907 are tacked on
tfor comparison): —
June,’ol. June,’o7. June,’oB.
Liquid assets .. £400,303 £900,773 £887,489
Freeholds .. .. 253,455 249,146 245,786
Other property .. 154,436 144,653 152,547
£808,194 £1,294,572 £1,285,822
Less debts .. 67,347 243,757 182,515
Surplus .. £740,847 £1,050,815 £1,103,307
There is, in addition, a wasting asset, “ Leasehold proper-
ties, £12,123,” which is being steadily written down. Add
that to the surplus ; total, £1,115,430, which is the
amount of the actually-paid capital (£250,000) plus the
reserves shown above (£865,430). Now, as to the deprecia-
tion reserve, it can only be required for the two items,
“ Freeholds ” and “Other property’’—the latter mostly
rolling stock. The Trust, at the end of the eight years,
may not want the rolling stock and a number of the co. s
properties ; in that case, some of them would have to be
quitted at a sacrifice. No doubt if the Trust could have
command of the lines for 12 months previous to July 1,
1916, a whole lot of the stock would be obsolete, because
the Trust would probably have sense enough during that
period to electrify the system. The trouble, however, is
that the Trust won’t be able to come in until the co. goes
out; and, unless some way out of that trouble can he
found, it will have to carry on the business with the co.’s
rolling stock while the inevitable change is being made
from cable to electric traction. Consequently if it has to
take over the co.’s plant and rolling stock, the co. probably
won’t lose much on those assets.
The capital of £480,000 (as watered) is in 960,000
shares of 10s. each. These are now quoted at 38s. 9d.,
which is a tremendous move from the 11s. 9d. of 10 years
ago. The 38s. 9d. gives the business a market value of
£1,860,000. Taking the freeholds and “ other property ”
to be worth what they are said to be worth in the
balance-sheet, then, as shown above, the co.’s net
possessions are now worth £1,103,307, apart from the
trifle of leaseholds. In the past seven years this total has
been increased by £362,460 ; but in the next eight years
it will have to increase by no less than £740,847 to bring
the assets up to the present market value of the under-
taking. It is quite true that the profits have increased
rapidly during the past few years; but it is doubtful
whether the increase will be wholly maintained. Also it
has to be remembered that the sinking fund is now
£12,000 a year more than it was prior to 1905. For many
years Melbourne Trams have been regarded as a gilt-
edged investment. The time has come, however, when,
owing to the approach of 1916 and all its possibilities,
good and bad, they must be regarded as speculative. On
38s. 9d. per share the increased dividend for last year
shows a return of £5 3s. per cent. ; and that isn’t so large
as to make tip for any doubt that may exist as to the
return of 38s. 9d. per share in 1916.

The reserves consist of an insurance and repairs sus-
pense account (£47,474), and a fund for equalising divi-
dends (£10,028). Into the former fund £3526 has been
dropped during the past six months, while, in order to
make up the usual 8 per cent, dividend, the directors
have had to draw £2511 out of the other fund. Presum-
ing that the £3526 was a fair addition to the fund to pro-
vide for repairs and renewals — i. e., was a fair provision
to cover depreciation—then the profit for the six months
was only enough to pay 5f per cent., instead of 8 per
cent. The term was not normal, though, owing to the
labor trouble in March and April. That was followed by
a further increase in costs, which the co. is countering
with an increase in freights, etc. Industrial condi-
tions on this line are, however, still disturbed.
The co., as pointed out just now, added £3526 to repairs
and renewals fund. That is a net addition—presumably
the co. also paid out of the fund whatever was spent on
repairs, etc., during the term. It is impossible to tell
from the published accounts whether any other profits
are seized upon and applied to write down ship property
direct. The co. has of recent years been constantly buy-
ing and selling vessels; so the variations in the ship-
property item tell nothing, as a rule. Once or twice,
however, the item has been reduced, apparently being
written down out of revenue ; in other words (say) £5OOO
of cash or liquid assets has displaced the same book
value of ship-property. In the absence of a profit
and loss account it can’t be said to what
extent this practice of writing-down is pursued.
The business has increased very much during the last few
years. The ship property item has moved up like this—
June, 1900, £85,745 ; June, 1903, £137,251 ; June, 1908,
£233,362, besides £14,904 paid on account of a new vessel.
For many years, down to 1905, the capital stood at
£131,250; it is now £218,750, in 250,000 shares on which
2s. 6d. is uncalled. The balance of the capital for the
new ships has been raised by the sale of wharf
property (resumed by the State) and the realisation of
other assets. The present balance-sheet, therefore, differs
very much from those of the years previous to, say, 1903 :
and the history of the co. is not much guide to the future.
On the other hand, North Coast settlement has been very
rapid, though the permanence of some of it is still in
doubt. Moreover, rail competition is one of the certain-
ties of the future ; and that is pretty sure to mean that
somewhat less energy will be spent by the State in keep-
ing open some of the alleged “harbors” of the North
Coast.
