This is one of the trustee cos. which try to inspire
confidence by telling you nothing about themselves—or
almost nothing. There are some people, no doubt, who
are impressed by the wisdom of the owl; but it is doubt-
ful whether many are sufficiently impressed to say to the
owl, no matter bow silent he may be—“ When lam dead
you shall take my place so far as my business is con-
cerned.” As to the investment value of the shares in this
co., the same thing applies as applies to several other
trustee concerns—one doesn’t know exactly what the
profits are. Trustee cos. draw two lots of commission—
one on the income ; the other on the capital value of the
estate. It is with regard to the latter that all sorts of
different plans are adopted. Some cos., directly an estate
comes in for management, credit themselves with the
commission on the capital value; others spread
out that commission over the years through
which it is expected that the winding up of the estate
will drag ; others take credit for the commission as the
estate is realised —if 20 per cent, of the estate is got rid
of in one year, they pass 20 per cent, of the corpus com-
mission into profits. On account of these varying
methods it is very little use taking any notice of the
profits alleged to have been made by a trustee co. in any
one or two years. This co.’s capital is £25,000 ; and it
has a trifle more than that represented by cash and
public stocks, leaving the reserves to be represented by
the Society’s premises (£15,962) and sundry debts (out-
standing commissions, etc.). In the foregoing table £354,
appropriated as a bonus to the staff, has been deducted
from the past half-year’s revenue. For the past twelve
months the profits show a return of £6 19s. 6d. per cent,
on capital and reserves combined —which is only very
moderate for a trustee co.

1907 .. 2593 Nil 2500 3,504
1908.. 3288 3 per cent. = 2026 2000 2,766
That begins to look better ; but if, as alleged, Japanese-
made machinery is now being unloaded in Queensland, it
is quite possible that even the Lyne tariff will have to be
put, up in order to give these Queensland manufacturers
reasonable white-labor Protection. Under the old State
tariff the machinery which this co. makes had mostly a
25 per cent. duty. Then the Ma State Freetrade con-
tingent whittled away at Kingston’s proposals, and only
about half that Protection was secured under the first
Commonwealth tariff. After a great battle, the industry
gets the benefit of a number of 25 per cent, duties, though
there is still an appalling list of exemptions. Never-
theless, the change has given new hope to the co.,
which, under the piebald tariff, was threatened
with almost certain extinction. It is too early even now
to say whether the tariff is sufficient to allow the co. to
make a reasonable six or seven per cent. One great
trouble with nearly all these works is that, on account of
the uncertainty of the future, they can’t command the
means to put in complete, up-to-date plant. If Australia
really means Protection it would pay it handsomely to
express itself definitely—“ Here is 20 N percent. to go on
with. But if the cheap Jap (or somebody else) beats
you on that basis, then you shall have some more.”
If Australia made a definite promise like that, or elected
a Parliament which talked like that, capitalists would
go ahead, works would be brought right up to
date, and very soon costs would be reduced.
But Australia won’t talk like that; and, until it does,
one always must look at the future of these industrial
concerns with a doubt, which is discreditable and damag-
ing to the Commonwealth. Still, as remarked above,
there is hope. The published accounts don’t show' the
extent of the business —wages paid, etc.—but these
figures indicate an improvement :
1902. 1906. 1907. 1908.
Capital £64,227 £64,302 £64,302 £64,302
Debentures .. .. 21,150 10,575 Nil Nil
Other debts .. .. 8,308 1,755 6,587 11,639
Freehold, plant, etc... 68,083 58,682 53,419 51,879
Stock, etc 22,074 12,211 13,782 15,791
Cash, etc 14,071 12,302 10,343 15,712
The increase of the “other debts” is mostly due to the
need to finance apparently larger contracts in hand ; the
estimated value of these, over and above the ‘progress
payments, is included in Cash, etc. The co. could very
well do with more capital ; but it has very' little hope of
getting-it on reasonable terms until Australia says more
plainly that it is going to protect this urg-ently necessary
engineering industry, even if a 100 per cent, duty is
called for.

This private line serves a section of the Riverina, and
connects with the Victorian State system at Moama,
W'hich is across the river from Echuca. The 1903 drought
hit the district hard ; but after that there was a recovery,
and, though things haven’t been too flourishing the last
six or nine months, they have been w'orse in Victoria.
There has consequentlybeen a steady drift of Victorian
farmers into N.S.W., especially into this and other
southern parts. The bulk of the traffic is, however,
pastoral, wherefore it occurs in the second half of the
year—out in the south-w'est the jumbuck is already, in
August, being robbed of his winter overcoat. Confining
the compari -on to the “ off ” seasons, the past half-year’s
figures are the best this century—and for the few' years
also of the last century for which The Bulletin has the
co.’s records. Some details :

In the late ’eighties the co. paid 10 per cent.; then mort-
gages, rabbits and other diseases gradually ate the
dividends down to 5 percent., at which they stood for
some years, until drought dried them up to the 2J per
cent, shown in the table. With closer settlement the
prospects of the co. steadily improve ; and the dividend
prospects also improve on account of the steady reduc-
tion of the debenture debt. In 1902 it was £30,000 ; it is
now back to £23,500, and the half-yearly interest pay-
ment has melted down from £6OO to £440. No expendi-
ture has been charged up to capital account for many
years ; all renewals' together with additions, if any, to
stock, plant, etc., have been paid out of revenue. There
are 25,000 £5 shares paid to £4.

This co. didn’t get a good start. It was born in 1891, and
the next few years were very severe on financial infants,
and especially on infants which expected to live
largely on sheep. After two or three years it
managed to pay a dividend, but it was only
able to repeat the performance twice afterwards.
It laid itself out to freeze and export meat, and mort-
gaged itself to provide the accommodation and the means
to carry on the trade, and then it found that the big
drought hadn’t left it any work to do. While things
W'ere slowly mending in that respect, it built up a wool
business ; and, though 1902-3 were a severe set-back, it
managed to pull through, and hold itself ready for the
very big increase in the frozen meat business which has
come with the last four good years. In three years past
it has handled about a million sheep for export, 316,732 of
them during the past 12 months. What the effect of that
has been on the local price of meat nobody can tell for
certain ; but it probably amounts to two or three
shillings a head on the average—that is to say, Sydney
meat-eaters would pay N. S. Wales meat-growers two or
three shillings less per head of sheep if it were not for
the onerations of this co. The Sydney meat-eater and
the N.S.W. meat-grow'er are therefore very liable to
regard this co. with different feelings. Digging further
into the published accounts, these comparisons can be
got:—
1902. 1906. 1907. 190 S.

tures? etc. .. £102,217 £62,527 £52,454 £74.771,

etc. ... .. 125,700 135,494 135,941 136,310
Advances .. .- 87,412 69,643 77,450 72,432
Stocks .. .. 3,111 2,831 7,980 10,754
The profits have been used to reduce the mortgage and
debenture debt. The principal of the freeholds is the big
store that stands alongside Admiralty House, in a place
where no sheep-store should be. It is probable that
undisclosed profits have been used to write down some of
the assets; a few years ago it was said that £7500 had
been applied to this purpose, and the profit and loss
account lumps the profit “after providing for. . .'bad
and doubtful debts and depreciation.” The balance-sheet
still shows a goodwill item, £24,516, which ought to be
written off, and which probably will be now that at last
the reserves on the other side exceed that amount. In
the table at the top of this, that item has of course not
been reckoned as an asset.

This co. has a stiff competitor in the suppliers of electric
lifts, and it makes very little progress in the matter of
either gross revenue or net profits. For the half-year to
June, 1903, the gross revenue was £12,501 ; June, 1907,
£12,461; June, 1908, £12,294. Meantime, in five years
the wages, general charges, etc., have crept up from £5916
to £6166. In its published accounts theco. shows reserves
amounting to £26,685 after providing for the dividend.
But on the other side there is a paper or goodwill item,
“ Parliamentary rights and concessions, £15,000.” Even
the remaining: £11,685 is not the best of reserves, because all
the plant, property (including leasehold), etc., are in at
cost; consequently a part of the reserves at any rate re-
presents actual depreciation. For the same reason the
profits are no doubt over-stated, since nothing is deducted
from them to provide for the renewals which will become
necessary by-and-bye. Current renewals are probably
being paid for and charged up to working expenses ; but
current revenue will not cover the big - renewals of
plant, etc. The additions to the reserves are being put
into plant; but they' are not bringing in anything extra.
Five years ago the fixed investment (mains, freeholds,
etc.) cost £101,745, and the gross revenue was £12,501;
the item has now grown to £111,247, yet the gross
revenue is still no more than £12,294. The co.’s Parlia-
mentary Bill provides that any - divisible profits over 10
per cent, shall be cut up equally between the co. and the
City Council. Possibly this may explain why the gross
revenue does not grow—the co. may prefer to make con-
cessions in order to assure its connection rather than
imperil the connection by making extra profits, of which
it would only get one-half. But there is no proof of that
in the published figures.

This co. is the offspring of the Sydney and Suburban
Hydraulic Power Co. ; and, though the City Council
started out to dispute it at one time, apparently the pro-
vision limiting the parent’s dividends to 10 percent.,
unless the council shares in the excess, does not apply to
the child. Anyhow, the council did not pursue the claim.
The reserves have been put back into the business ; but
there is no hint as to whether they have to cover depreci-
ation, or whether depreciation is allowed for out of undis-
closed profits. The capital is in 30,000 shares of £l, paid
to 15s. The business depends to a considerable extent on
the Hydraulic Power Co. ; and if the latter had more
trouble to earn its 10 per cent., it might occasionally
drive a harder bargain with the subsidiary co. That
remains one of the possibilities ahead of this co.

The advances made by this highly-respectable old Uncle
Monty are an interesting study. At the middle of 1901
things were about normal, and the advances totalled
£73,450. By the end of droughty 1903 things were bad ;
so Monty had lent his nephews £88,400. Then followed
some years of boom and of cheap money, and by June,
1907, only £80,428 was outstanding. Just then acci-
dents began to happen. The wretched share market, in
which it hadn’t seemed possible to go wrong for 18
months, turned dog on its trustful punters. Time bar-
gains began to be oppressive. And then the punters had
to remember their old relative once more. So the advances
have proceeded like this : June, 1907, £80,428 ; Dec., 1907,
£84,839 ; June, 1908, £93,170. The co. used to accept
deposits; but apparently it found that an overdraft is
handier, and to oblige the new rush of customers a small
credit balance has been turned in the six months into a
debit of £BO4O. Of the reserves £3102 is a fund to allow for
shrinkage of assets ; and with over £93,000 lent on a
tremendous variety of securities, there must be amaz-
ingly good management if there isn’t an occasional over-
valuation. Therein lies the biggest risk from the invest-
ment point of view. One can’t check the value of the
assets in any way ; yet if it should turn out that they
have been overvalued, then of course the profits have
been overstated. Still it doesn’t seem in this case a very
grave risk, because in the five and a half years since the
10 per cent. divs. have been paid, £12,134 has been added
to the reserves ; and that allows a comfortable margin
for quite a lot of unsuspected moth and rust and decay'.
The co. is in 75,000 shares of £l, 50,000 of them fully
paid, and 25,000 paid to 10s. ; but on many of the latter
lot calls have been pre-paid.

This co. got into the habit of paying big dividends, and
knocks it off with great difficulty. In the early ’nineties
it was paying 17 and 18 per cent. ; then, step by' step,
it was driven back to 12 per cent, in 1896; and
it is now becoming impossible to justify that much.
Things would not look so bad if the co. were writing
down its mains and other works ; but apparently not a
penny is going that way. Certainly the co. is paying for
repairs out of revenue ; but that doesn’t cover general
wear and tear. Provision ought to be made for the bigger
renewals ; and that isn’t being done. Bendigo isn’t on
the up-grade either; so if this co. is managed with the
average amount of Bendigo prudence, a reduction in the
dividend is one of the things that the share-
holder needn’t be surprised to meet at any time.
So far as one can see at present, only two things can stop
the reduction—a great improvement in the Bendigo out-
look (and there is no sign of that) or a very' substantial
reduction in the cost of coal (and there are not very
strong indications of that either). As to the latter item,
£5452 of gas-money three years ago cost £1695 for coal;

Australasiari Office—
Equitable Building, MELBOURNE.
A water famine at Broken Hill has time and
again been predicted, but even .were the Stephens Creek
Reservoir to run dry, some of the companies would he
able to keep portion'of;the works running by using the
water pumped from the mines. The South Co. has already
made provision and installed an Aldrich Quintuplex
Electric Pump at the 950 ft. level. The North Company
is now intent on taking the same precautions, and has
ordered from W. & J. Lempriere, of Melbourne, an Aldrich
Quintuplex Pump, which ■will be placed at the 1000 ft.
level. The water from the mines would need treatment
before it would he fit for human consumption. Is the
Wade Government too engrossed with State Frights to
give some proper attention to State requirements, and
provide the Hill with means of receiving abundant and
permanent good water?*

* * * *
One of the things that are liable to happen to
an insurance office under the present system of piecemeal
State legislation is happening just now to the Citizens’. In
the revenue account which it issues with its annual report
it puts receipts under six headings, lumping commissions
and management exes in one line and surrenders and
bonuses in another. Everybody who reads the report
knows how much is paid in bonuses and how much in
surrenders ; also how much of the bonuses goes to policy-
holders (four-fifths) and how much to shareholders (one-
fifth). The Bulletin, in fact, in post-morteming the
annual report, always adds the shareholders’ bonus to the
costs of management in order to show the expense rate.
Under the Vic. Companies Act the co. has to send in a
return according to a prescribed form ; so surrenders have
to be shown distinct from bonus. It is simply a difference
in grouping : there are no figures in the Victorian return
(except the proportion of commission and management
expenses) that couldn’t be easily ascertained from the
report issued in N. S. Wales ; yet because the two forms
differ, the office is being jumped on with tremendous
energy and accused of issuing a misleading document,
and the two documents are put side by side to show that
both can’t be right. Yet, of course, they ban be, and in
fact are.
At the half-yearly meeting of the Adelaide
Steam Tug Co. (B. 6/8/’08) it was decided to pay a bonus of
£2BOO (5 per cent.) in addition to the ordinary dividend at
the rate of 10 per cent. The total distribution for the
half-year was therefore £5600 (at the rate of 20 per cent,
per annum), leaving the reserves at £15,668. The extra
bonus was paid out of the insurance fund, which the
directors reported had grown larger than was necessary
for their purpose.
« * * *
Broken Hill South issues the expected cheerless
report. The net profit for the half-year was £15,497, in-
cluding £lO,OOO from zinc tailings—a shocking slump
from the £94,000 net which the co. made in the corres-
ponding half of 1907. That, of course, was during the
metal boom. Twelve months ago, trying to drive home
the fact that there was a boom in progress, and that
profits were therefore inflated and weren’t safe things to
look at, The Bulletin pointed out that, with lead at £l3
and silver at 2s. 6d., the co.’s profits on the half-year’s
output would have been only £13,529. It looked an
absurd figure in those days, no doubt; and yet it was a
bit over the mark, judging' by the past half-year’s work.
* * * *
Apart from lead-market considerations, and if
appearances are not deceptive, the Broken Hill Junction
North has passed through its darkest hour. The long
and expensive struggle with the mill seems to be at an
end ; and if the shaft only holds out, there is now no
reason why a good tonnage of ore should not be main-
tained. The No. 10 level never looked better than it does
at present,’and at the bottom level a fine body of ore is
being opened up. A rhodonite bar, which caused some
anxiety when the level was first worked, has now been
found to have a fine body of ore riding over it. From the
bottom level the ore body is going down with a strong
western underlay, and partly on that account it is
probable that the shaft will be deepened and another
level opened up. Additional tables are also to be placed
in the mill, and various other improvements of an enter-
prising character are engaging attention.
An attempt has been made by the Junction
North to lay hands on the derelict Broken Hill Junction,
but the shareholders sniffed disdainfully at the first
bait offered—2o,ooo shares in a co. of 200,000. And yet
they might do worse. As a mining proposition, the
Junction North could not be expected to seriously con-
sider its neighbor just now, although there is a good deal
of ore in the north end of the Junction which could be
economically dealt with through the Junction North
shaft. Leaving mining out of the question, the water in
tbe mine, is an asset, and in suggesting a deal the Junction
North no doubt had that fact in view.
* » * *
While it is quietly chewing over the Junction
North’s offer to absorb it, the Junction is going through
with its reconstruction scheme. Adelaide and Broken Hill
shareholders compromised, and so settled their quarrel;
the first payment is to be sixpence per share, as Adelaide
wanted, and subsequent payments are to be 3d. every
other month, as Broken Hill wanted. And finally (though
that probably won’t help much) Broken Hill is to have a
local director or two. The co. still has about £2600 in
hand, and claims to have 149,000 tons of ore in sight ; but
it is hard to get at, and most of it is bad to treat after it
is got.
* * * *
A very little moisture gave an impetus to the
continuous movement in the Sulphide Corporation’s
Central mine, Broken Hill; or, at any rate, the impetus
arrived at the same time as the moisture. The special
movement amounted to nothing serious, but serves as a
reminder that the stated ore reserves in both Central and
Block 10, next door, ought to be heavily discounted, as it
is extremely unlikely that it will ever be possible to get
out anything like all the ore in those mines.
* * * *
Later developments at the new bottom level of
the Harden (N.S.W.) are much better. A little driving
has been done both east and west on the ore ; and
though in the drive east the stuff was still poor (10s. per
ton), westward the face is in ore 2ft. wide, worth 158 s.
per ton. A pump is now being put in at this level ; and
till the lode is drained a little, other work there is hung
up. Meantime the plant, working on the ore from the
upper levels, makes a steady profit of about £l5OO a
month—last month’s total was £1505, after paying all
development costs.
* * * *
Kyloe (N.S.W.), which was over-capitalised
already, having had to pass through too many hands in
the voyage from the prospector to the public, has been
obliged to issue 20,000 pref. shares. Under ordinary circum-
stances a copper mine with a small and not very regular vein
wouldn’t have much hope of raising capital just now ;
but Kyloe developments have been satisfactory, and
Adelaide, which holds most of the mine, readily took the
pref. issue.
■*•#*■*
Shareholders in Cobar Gold Mines (the old
Fort Bourke) must be beginning to feel uncomfortable.
At the close of the financial year 1904 (September 30) it
was reported that the reserves of free-milling ore were
about exhausted ; but after that a little of the same stuff
was found below 216 ft. level, and so crushing on a reduced
scale was continued for a while. At September, 1904,
the actual cash in hand was £63,365, and the other liquid
assets four or five thousand in excess of liabilities. That
was regarded as a fund to provide plant to treat the sul-
phide ore ; and shareholders, who had never got a divi-
dend, were told that the sulphide ore really constituted
the mine. Between 216 ft. and 316 ft. Chairman M'Donaid
reported in 1905 that there were 262,000 tons of ore available,
about half of it low-grade. Since then the co. has had in-
numerable reports made, and practically nothing else has
been done, except that a little experimental Elmore plant
has been put up. Yet the cash had been sweated down to
£45,864 by the end of September, 1907, and the sweating'
process has, no doubt, been pretty severe during the past
nine months. In London both the Elmore and the
Cattermole oil processes have been tested, and, as a result,
Johnson and Sons, the well-known metallurgists, recom-
mended an oil process ; that was on July 16, 1907. That
recommendation has been carried out, and the result has
been poor ; whether the Elmore process or the preliminary
crushing is at fault is a matter of opinion. Praotically,
therefore, the co. isn’t any more advanced than it was
four years ago, notwithstanding that meantime over
£20,000 of cash and liquid assets have gone; and the
prospects of providing a sufficient plant with the pro-
ceeds of the oxidised ore are becoming bluer. It is
another example of the conspicuous failure of the Eng-
lish management of Australian mines.
J, O. Armstrong and Dr. Jack, of course, report
that Lloyd’s (N.S. W.) is good enough to put more money
into. They put the ore blocked out at 120,000 tons,
worth 4 per cent, copper ; and, though that is a good bit
to go on with, they recommend first the pushing out of
three levels further east (Nos. 3, 7 and 10), those being
now beyond the last fault. If the result is good, as they
expect (and as managing director Corbould has always
expected) it will be, then the plant must be remodelled
and £20,000 spent on a tramway to bring in firewood.
This last was really the snag against which the mine
pulled up. Assuming the eastern exploration is satisfac-
tory, the mine will call for £46,400 of new capital: £lO,OOO
to put out the three trial drives and then develop the
mine down to the No. 13, £20,000 for the tramway, and
£16,400 for the removal and rearrangement of the plant.
With that done they figure on putting copper F. 0.8.
Sydney at £46 per ton.
Sydney shareholders have renewed hopes of Mt.
David (N.S. W.), as the last crushing of 460 tons returned
2760 z. and 530 z. from concentrates. After paying reali-
sation charges on the latter, the return should equal
from £2 10s. to £2 15s. per ton. The Huntingdon mill
only added 12oz. to the yield, which seems to spell disap-
pointment, but the object was to quarry and treat a lot of
low grade material at the lowest possible cost, so poor re-
turns from this section of the plant are to be expected. In
extending No. 2 level of the winch winze the mine is
developing a new make of payable stone. The new shaft,
when down to the required depth, will have a better
chance of opening- up the mine and increasing the output.
The small output of considerably under 6000 tons a year
is at present the stumbling block from an investor’s point
of view. On such a limited tonnage a continuous run on
good stone is required to pay even a modest sixpenny
dividend in 12 months. Expenses are not likely to be
reduced for a time.
> * *
Hillgrove (N.S. W.) is suffering dull times again.
Scheelite and antimony are too low to give much em-
ployment, and the New Hillgrove Prop, has had to take
a pull On its heavy capital expenditure. The recent
“ burst” in No. 17, Baker’s Creek, and the lower grade of
ore treated also help the glum feeling. With the New Hill-
grove Prop, the difficulty all along has been to make
small mine resources stretch to the absurd length of the
share register, and the debt of £21,000 will only tend to
aggravate this difficulty. Baker’s Creek has a bit of
money in the bank, and is thus in a better position than
the big English concern. Long crosscuts have, however,
to be put out from the shaft in order to open up every
new level. Still, these help to insure the workings, which
will soon reach 2000 ft., from serious disaster through
“ bursts.”
* * * *
“Substantial people” have come forward to
guarantee the new issue of Tower Hill shares (W.A.). The
scrip has a liability of Is. per share spread over a fairly
long period.
* * * *
Kinsella (W.A.) has been sampled by the Fin-
gall Co., and found wanting. Local papers say that pro-
specting at depth is to be undertaken ; but that is only
hearsay. The property was bought to provide a needful
source of water supply for the Fingall, and will probably
never be used for anything else.
* * * *
London Statist, of June 27, gave the Whim
Well crowd (W.A.) a bad time, and other British papers
are joining in the hunt. Mendacious newspaper puffing,
the fact of £47 per ton of copper produced being re-
quired to make ends meet, irrespective of London admin-
istration, interest on floating debt, etc., and finally the
absurdity of the inspired hint that a dividend is coming
along-, are dealt with. Then attention is drawn to the
promotion of the co. But at that point the directors
became annoyed, and are talking law.
* * * *
The Northern Mines, at Lawlers (W.A.), now
treats nearly 9000 tons a month, at a total cost of 14s. 3d,
per ton, and this despite over 500 miles of railage, nearly
100 miles of cartage (this puts up the cost of supplies),
and seven miles of ore-trucking to the mill. Only up-to-
date plant and the expenditure of capital running into
more than six figures enable the co. to do this ; but the
achievement is agood one, all the same.
* * * *
Vivien (W.A.) just manages to keep a little
ahead of it on ore worth about 7dwt. The report for 1907
shows that the year’s working resulted in a profit of
£ll3, which, with £733 brought forward, made a total of
£847. The main feature of the development work during
the period was the extension and connection of the No. 2
and 3 levels. A new slimes plant is now at work and
helping to swell the monthly return. Ore reserves are
two years ahead of the mill, but dividends are not yet
visible.
* * * *
The Callion directors (W.A.) have sent one of
their number to Adelaide to argue about £6OO which is in
dispute between the new management and the old. The
Board, now a Westralian one, threatens to go to law over
the matter.
* * * *
W. J. Loring is returning by the Mongolia, but
will stay aweek or two in the West before proceeding to
8., M. and Oo.’s Australian headquarters in Melbourne.
* * * *
Chaffers (W.A.) lately had a set-back, partly
because it has thrown up the copper show which it was
testing in the Nor’-West; and people begin to suspect
that most of its resources will be frittered away, with
nothing to show for them. This Nor’-West claim had an
outcrop which assayed up to 42 per cent, copper; but
there wasn’t enough of it to be worth bothering about.
Meantime, two tribute parties are developing the new
lode in the old Chaffers property, and the development
is very satisfactory. Two loz. crushings have been
taken out, and there is a good body of 26dwt. ore in one
face. The 500,000 shares in the co. are, however, a
tremendous swag to carry.
* * * «
The Atlas Petroleum Co. has issued a pros-
pectus in Westralia. The promoters propose to form a
co. with 22,500 £1 shares, half of which are contributing,
to bore for petroleum somewhere between Bunbury and
Cape Leeuwin. The old yarn about “ large quantities of
asphaltum being thrown up on the adjacent beach” is
revived ; much space is absorbed by figures showing the
world’s past production of oil, and an extended reference
is made to the drill-hole put down to a depth of 1710 ft.
at Warren River some time ago, and which,
according to the prospectus, “ proved incontestably
that a petroleum horizon had already been cut
and opened up by erosive action of the river.”
Seeing, however, that the asphaltum may have
come from anywhere, and that the petroleum produced
to date in other countries is neither here nor there, and
that the bore alluded to only passed through common
sea-sand, it is hard to understand what the authors are
driving' at. The scheme is not one to be treated seriously.
• * * *
It is the hand of managing-director P. W.
Tewksbury that signs the first report of the Tewksbury
Amalgamated, but it is the voice of J. C. Watson, one of
his two co-directors, that sounds through it. One isn’t
at all surprised, then, to find that this directors’ report,
with the voice of the ex-Labor leader in it, isn’t quite
like most other directors’ reports. One
frank and honest paragraph is devoted to redemption of
capital. “The mine and plant, which principally repre-
sent shareholders’ capital . . . continually depreciate in
value as worked. It is, therefore, evident that part of
the gold won by that work, to at least the amount which
the assets have depreciated in value, also repre-
sents the capital invested. ... It is therefore pro-
posed henceforward to ear-mark one-third of the
amount distributed to shareholders as redemption of
capital, and the balance will be paid as dividends.” This
plan can be followed with a dredging proposition, for,
though there are risks, still, given proper prospecting,
the future can be fairly well foretold. It won’t make a
bit of difference in the amount to reach the shareholders’
pockets, but it will be a constant reminder that the more
they get the less there is left, and the longer they work
the nearer they get to the time when their claim will be
no more than a mud heap. And that ought to stop the
punter from paying an absurd price for shares later on,
when the mud-heap stage has been very nearly reached.
* V * *
The Tewksbury Amalgamated, which is a con-
solidation of several small Bright (Vic.) dredging pro-
perties, is working five dredges, from which it gathered
up last half-year £10,277 profit. That, however, was
before writing off depreciation. This was on a capital of
£66,500. gome remarkably cheap work has been done ;
the old Tewksbury Freehold No. 2 dredge’s working
expenses were only 1'435d. per cubic yard, or £5 I9s. 7d.
per 1000 cubic yards. Other dredges worked by this co.
are those of the old Tewksbury, Eurobin, and Freeburgh
cos. On the results so far and the prospecting, the direc-
tors reckon to pay back capital and something like
£130,000 besides.
* * + »
Vic. dredging and sluicing claims are bustling ;
the recent rains in the N.E. have filled the dams and
started the creeks running. Donald Fletcher has applied
for a lease on the Yackandandah Creek, at the junction
with Clear Creek. But Pearson Tewksbury’s application
for 2000 acres on the Ovens River below Wangaratta is
making most stir. If this is granted it will be the first
plant to work so low down the river. But the farmers
are getting ready to say a word or two to the warden.
The Kiewa River leases have not yet been granted ;
Warden Rowan is to visit the ground and give a decision
shortly. The landholders are kicking hard.
* * * *
Two lights of the Melbourne Stock Exchange
journeyed to the Insolvency Court last week to testify
that the practice of the members of that Exchange was
not to spec shares to clients. They were not called,
otherwise cross-examination might have disclosed that
they could not testify very much from their own
personal knowledge as to what is done. Whether
the practice comes within the category of the
“dishonorable conduct” included in the Stock Ex.
rules as a reason for expulsion, may depend on
how the dealing works out. If the specker can deliver, no
one suffers, provided he can pay his difference should the
market go the wrong way. If it does not, and he becomes
unfinancial, the dishonorable conduct rule does not mate-
rially affect the position, because the defaulter is shunted
anyway,
* * * *
Mackay’s Quartz, Gaffney’s (Vie.), carries the
usual sixpenny worth of froth. It isn’t named after one
Mackay, the legal manager, in whose office it is, but is
fathered by some hoary-headed prospector who punched
it well, and then left. The co. has indulged in theluxury
of a 6in. winch, a modest overdraft, and a long tunnel.
Now it wants a lode. When that is got it will want a fair
shoot of stone, and then it will meet the wants of some of
the knowing ones, who require a neat market for a hungry
mob of speckers.
*■ * * *■
Melbourne is told that it has hit a three-star
mine in the Diamond Creek, about nine miles from
Heidelberg. The show is an old-timer, with a still older
dyke to work, carrying a vein of quartz alongside. The
Argus lately alleged that there wasn’t a dead-end show-
ing. That may be true; but is there a decent
reserve of stone? No. 2 level north carried
13 inches of stone ; there is Bin. of the same
stuff south, while a reef where cut at 525 ft. is also
Bin. The average of gold is nearly 30dwt. Costs are
relatively high with such a narrow formation ; and six
months’ hard work would tear out most of the quartz
proved. The co. doesn’t do things that way, though.
It clings to a five-head battery, and runs through only
200 tons a month. Shareholders aren’t greedy, as while
they took £2625 for divs., they put £lOOO aside for emer-
gencies. A little more of this and a little more good
stone, and they will deserve a niche as mining saints.
* * * *
Golden Dyke, Wandong (Vic.), pops out a
balance-sheet at six-monthly intervals to show that it
pays directors’ fees, and has credit enough to get a bank
overdraft. What ties it up is the hunt after quartz, the
shortness of the shoots when got, and the heavy per-
centage of antimony. Directors say “the whole of the
machinery is in good working order.” If that is their
best, it is time the happy despatch was thought about.
* * * «
The Mitchell River Antimony Co. (N.Q.), which
was going to demonstrate a new caustic soda process for
the extraction of gold and antimony, is up against the
hard, cold fact that its money is exhausted, though the
process hasn’t been given even an hour’s test. The
McArthurs, of Glasgow, fathered the process, and were
heavily interested in Mitchell River; and they sent
out metallurgist Opie to give the thing a start. So' that
there shouldn’t be any friction, the co. also appointed
McArthurs’ representative as general manager. He did
some mining work, wasn’t satisfied with the results, and
left just when the Board expected the test to be made.
To get the plant on the ground and do the little prospect-
ing work, that has been done has cost close up to £20,000;
and, though the developments may not show as much
ore as looked probable from surface indications, there
was enough anyhow to test the process on. The two
Munros and other substantial North Queenslanders are
deep in the business, so it is safe to say that, now that
the thing has got this far, funds will be available to
take it the necessaiy few steps further.
* * * •*
The conditions under which the Gympie (Q)
Prospecting Association is to qualify for its slice of the
£50,000 Government grant are briefly these : The Govern-
ment undertakes to pay for six months the wages of 25
men at 255. per week, and a superintendent at £4, to
prospect places approved by the Committee and sanc-
tioned by the Government Geologist. No lease is to ex-
ceed five acres. In the event of any holding proving
sufficiently valuable to pay current wages and other
working expenses, Government assistance is to cease; also
titles to the land will be withheld until an agreement has
been signed by the Association undertaking to refund all
money advanced before any distribution of profits takes
place. The Association is a fairly strong body, repre-
sented by the warden and local inspector of mines, two
members each from the Miners’ Union and Mine Owners’
Association, and the chairman of the Gympie Chamber of
Commerce. Croydon is to be the next field dealt with in
the same way, and the State Geologist has just finished
an inspection.
* * * *
At the end of 50 years’ gold-mining, the output
of Queensland amounts to 15,611,7670 z., of which Charters
Towers has contributed 5,647,9380 z.. Gympie 2,375,3040 z.,
Rockhampton and Mt. Morgan 3,266,4990 z., and the
Palmer 1,323,7350 z. It is remarkable that a field which
has produced over a million ounces has been so neglected
as the Palmer has been.

W.R. : Hunt-street, Wrightville, N.S.W
R.D.T.: Can only refer you to the last notice of the
association in Bulletin of Sept. 26 last.
Mine-inspector Deeble’s annual report on the
Kalgoorlie field draws attention to the curious fact that,
though cos. frequently report that they lose practically
nothing- in the tailings, yet several of the mines are
installing retreatment plants to handle these tailings. The
inspector also points out that the competition among
managers to show low figures as to costs per ton may at
times operate against the interests of shareholders. The
extraction of the last dwt. of gold, worth 45., sometimes
costs an extra 3s. per ton, so there is a strong temptation
for the manager to let the shilling profit go rather than
show costs 3s. per ton higher than those of his neighbors.
And there is a lot of truth in this.

HE WAS ENGAGED JUST THEN.
Through trying to find a gas leakage at a Manly hotel
the barman was hurled 20ft., the parlor door was shat-
tered into fragments, and the hotel was set on fire. The
explosion was so terrific that it aroused Manly from its
winter sleep.— News Item.

“ Agricola” : Be “Gundy ” and foxes (B.
16/7/’08). His statement that the lady fox evi-
dences great cunning in concealing her young
shows that the said “Gundy” infests apart of
the country where the fox is still a novelty In
the Gundagai district (N.S.W.), where thousands
of foxes are slain, murdered, and assassinated
annually, it is the easiest thing out to discover the
cubs. Mostly the lying-in establishment con-
sists of a hollow log, surrounded by an ancient
and unattractive odor. Even before the cubs
are able to go out prospecting for themselves, a
well-defined pad may be seen leading to the door
of the Maternity Home. On the mat are de-
posited legs, heads, and other indigestible frag-
ments of bunnies, making it rather a conspicuous
place to both nose and eye. With a dog trained
to the work, scores of the cubs may be
captured with little trouble during August
and the two following months. In the
Batlow district (N.S.W.), a couple of years ago,
one man scored 98 cubs in a fortnight. He de-
pended on a fox-terrier to guide him to the
nursery, and then excavated them with his axe.
At that time the P. P. Board was giving a bonus
of Is. per scalp for fox cubs, but since that was
discontinued the infant mortality has slumped.
“ Kangaroosfcer ” : Ee long-distance rides.
When the French barque Montebello was
wrecked on the south coast of Kangaroo Island
(18/11/ 06), a settler’s son brought the news to
Kingscote, 79 miles away, well inside nine hours,
lie weighed over list., and rode a 13-hands pony
mare. Iwo rivers in flood had to be swum, and.
the track for 70 miles is very rough. A resident
here has an old pony mare—now pensioned off—
upon which he has repeatedly ridden 90 miles
between sunrise and sunset, on one occasion 97
miles, and he never rode under list.

“Percatates” writes
Permit me to have a “ cast ” or two at“H.G.,” who
talks wild bosh (B. 23/7/’08) concerning trout. In the
at cf tit e ’ t * le Sno 'YJ r P'> ver —the old-man trout stream of
Iv.S.W. has no native edible fish aboye the big falls near
Paupong ; and never had, unless we except the tiny in-
digenous mountain trout (so-called) and minnows, of the
sort found in the Blue Lake a-top of Kosciusko. In such
Monaro and other streams as already had indigenous fish
(bream and the like), and to which trout have been intro-
duced, there has been -no diminution of the indigenous
fish owing to trout ravages. The boot is on the other leg.
ouch ravaging as has occurred must be placed to the
credit of a local product—the perch. Trout have failed
hitherto in every eastern stream where perch were, the
latter being voracious eaters of trout fry. Trout, cod
and perch all live and have their being in different parts
of the ’Bidgee River, but generally they keep apart.
Trout cannot live for any length of time in the tepid
water the perch loves. When the trout becomes full-grown
he often wanders,into the domain of the perch and cod,
and remains uninjured for a while. At Bombalong, near
Michelago, I have caught trout up to the time of
the arrival of a thunderstorm ; then they “ went off the
rise. I changed the tackle, shifted to deep water, and
caught seven perch during the time that hail fell.
Generally speaking, the trout keep to the rough, cold
upper waters, which the cod and perch abhor.
There is nothing to show that Salmo Iridens, of Long
Plain and Brindabella, ever ate the family of portly old
Oligorus Macquariensis, who lives from Queanbeyan to
the sea, or harassed Mrs. Terapon and her fry, the silver
perch, of western waters. I would like to ‘know from
H.G. why are there nob “ tons ** of such native fish in
the waters where trout are not? The Upper Queanbeyan
and Badg-a (both Big- and Little) are full of trout; the
lower waters hold a few cod. But there is nothing like
the number there was in the old days. The sole reason for
the cod’s infrequency is the fact that thev aren’t pro-
tected. God are shot and netted bv cookies along the
river banks, regardless of seasons. Trout flourish because
it has been made an offence to treat them so. The
Fisheries Board is now rearing fry of cod and perch for
stocking suitable waters, and they are to be protected
reasonably. If “ H.G.” knew the elements of his subject
he would realise that it is lack of proper attention from
dynamiting vandals, and not the presence of trout—a
harmless fish in regard to other fish at the worst—which
has depleted N.S. W. streams of native fish.
“ Henry Phelthatt ” : lam glad “ Goril ”
dropped something pretty convincing on to
“ Maorilander.” That doubting individual must
have got his swine into the same obese condition
as the footballer he recently exported—so hog fat
that they wallow helplessly in their own lard, and
then knock up at the first opportunity. All wild
hogs aren’t that way—not by a heap. I pursued
the pork-beast once. I was mounted on an experi-
enced old piebald swine-chaser. We drew cover
in a thick pine scrub. My steed switched his tail
and propped his ears at the first dog-note. Next
second we were moving. To borrow from
.Nazzaro : “ The universe went roaring past, and
all I had to do was to keep the machine’s head
right,” hold tight, dodge the universe, and keep
in sight of the pig. He had about 25
lengths’ start of me at the outset. At
the end of a very fast mile I had reduced it to
15 lengths. The piebald never slackened, neither
did the pig. The excitement became intense.
Both were within striking distance, especially
the pig. I urged my black and white to his
utmost, nor stopped to look round until I had
put about five lengths (Bogan pig lengths) between
us. At 2} miles' I had not drawn any closer to
him, neither had I got much further away from
him, and the horse, the hog and the universe
were all roaring together. The pig was rolling
badly ; so was the piebald, when the welcome
sound of hoof-beats smote my ears. The desire to
abandon the chase suddenly became mutual.
The boar escaped in the thick lignum bush, and-
my steed and his rider had perforce to “ give him
best.” The rest of the party told me, and. I quite
believed it, that I had taken after the wrong
hog. All I’m sorry for is that it wasn’t “ Kauri-
lander ” and not me.

“Elmore”: Am as well aware as “The
Prooshan” (B. 23/7/’08) that the wombat wears
bristles, which “The Prooshan ” will appreciate
when his sense of humor sprouts again. But
womby’s rind is no fraud whatever his gall may
be. First struck wombat’s coat being utilised (in
the green state) by prospectors in NvE. Vic.,
back of Bogong high plains. Oil drums were out
of thequestion there then, and the men were hauling
stone up 60ft. with wombats’ skins sewn together.
They reckoned them indestructible. As mats
have often struck them. One in particular (home
tanned) has covered the step on the side door of a
Sunday-biz pub I know for seven years, and it is
still as good as new. This pub is in a busy mining
town, and the hobnails of a few hundred miners
will test anything. A stockman of my acquaint-
ance tanned several woms.’ under-vests with alum
and saltpetre, and got his saddles seated with
them. “ They’ll never wear out,” he says. As
for skinning, I’d sooner do one dozen wombats
than start on a hog. Yes, there’s much silver for
the man-who goes in for wombat peel.
* * * *
This is the true story of a scrap that didn’t.
They thought they had a champion at Redcat.
He was a fourteen-stoner, and his name was
Dooley. He made his bread honestly, plucking
up gumtrees by the roots, and carrying them down
to the sawmill by the river, one under each arm,
and he had whipped every man in the district
who thought he could fight. It occurred to Craig,
the publican, that a bit of money might be picked
up by promoting a scrap between Dooley and a S
professional heavyweight from Melbourne or
bydney. A heavyweight with a fair newspaper
reputation was secured, and the great mill was
advertised for show week. Craig was a
whale for advertisement, and it was he
who invented, organised, and stage-
managed the idea of a quarrel and a pre-
liminary bout in his bar. The publican
had risked about £3O in the enter-
prise, and wanted to keep up the popular
excitement, and it seemed to him that the
preliminary battle would occasion talk,
excite interest, and spread faith in the
genuineness of the coming encounter.
The battle in the bar was wisely thought
out and well worked up. Dooley was to
say something insulting to Dodger Walsh
(his opponent from town), and Dodger was
to hurl his temperance drink in Dooley’s
whiskers. Then Dooley was to get atWalsh,
and wallow all over him. Of course each
understood it was merely theatrical display, and
they were to be torn apart before mortal injuries
were inflicted. It worked beautifully up to a
point. Craig took care to have a great crowd in
his bar. The editor of the Red eat Banner was
present. Dooley called Dodger a person without
a father, Dodger threw his ginger-beer over
Dooley, and Dooley went at Dodger like a mob of
frantic cattle. It was at this point that Dooley’s
zeal outran discretion. He didn’t forget instruc-
tions, but in the fervor of the moment he over-
acted his part, and the bash he landed on Dodger
hurled that hapless pugilist into the next room,
clean through a pine partition. When the date
of the fight came round, Dodger was still in the
accident ward. He was a month in bed, and
Craig had to pay all exes. All Dooley said, as he
looked ruefully at the hospital containing his in-
capacitated opponent was: “Fancy him talcin’ it
like that! ”
“ Edyson ” : Mrs. Crowe, widow of the
lamented Tom Crowe, of Tin Hills (Vic.), issaid to be
about to take action for the recovery of substantial
damages from Walter Horace, settler, of the
same district, for breach of promise of mar-
riage. It appears that Mr. Horace admits the
promise and stands to the breach, but pleads
extenuating circumstances. The plea is put
in his own graphic language. “I’ve
knowed Mrs. Crowe was after me ever
since Tom pegged out, ’n’ I’ve done me
best t’ dodge her, but she was that
persistent I was often thinkin’ of
sellin’ out account iv her intentions,
but I couldn’t git no offer fer th’
selection, all th’ married men havin’
land iv their own, ’n’ all the single
ones bein’ more scared of Widow
Crowe than I was. If I fell ill she
came buzz-nackin’ round with me-
dacines, ’n’ poultrees, ’n’ broth, ’n*
it got that way ’cwasn’t
safe fer a man t’ have
anythin’ th’ matter with
him. This went on till
th’ day I was fellin’ a
stick up over th’ hill in th’
fur corner iv my land.
I’d gone t’ me billy t’ get
a drink when a gust o’ wind
brought down th’ stick un-
expected. It struck an old
dry butt, ’n’ came spinnin’
clean a-top o’ me. When
I come round I was pinned
t’ th’ ground with th’ fork
iv that ole butt’cross the
small o’ me back. I wasn’t
much hurt, beyond a
broken rib or
two, but th’
grip was that
tight I hadn’t
a wriggle left
in me. I was
there fer half
a day afore I
was found, ’n’
then it was Mrs.
• Crowe found
me. She didn’t
start off for ’elp at
once, but stood loolc-
in’ at me in a curious, speculative sort o’ way.
‘Fer God’s sake,’ I sez, ‘get me out 6’ this!’
* P’raps,’sez she. ‘Whatd’yer mean by “p’raps”?’
I sez. ‘Will veh marry me if I get yeh out,
Wally Horace ?’ she sez, straight. ‘No,’ I yells,
‘ I’ll see yeh ’anged first! ’ ‘ Then, good day t’
yeh, Mr. Horace,’ sez she. She come back about
sundown, when I was feelin’ near dead. ‘ How is
it now, Wally, my poor man?’sez she. ‘Will you
marry me now? ’ ‘I won’t,’ sez I. She said how
she thought it would be a wet, cold night, ’n’went
away. Just afore midnight she gave me
another look up. ‘ Will yeh marry me,
W.ally Horace ? ’ she sez. I felt that another hour
ud be th’ death o’ me, but I hung on. ‘Never,
never will I marry yeh. Mother Crowe,’ sez I.
She went off agen, ’n’ I saw nothin’ of her till ten
next mornin’. It was hellish hot, ’n’ th’ ants was
gettin’ at me. I was fair beat, ’n’ mad fer water.
Good mornin’, Wally,’ she sez, sweet’n’ cool;
‘ how d’ yeh feel about marriage this mornin’?’
‘Give me a drink!’ I sez. ‘Speakin’ iv mar-
riage ? ’ sez she. ‘ I’ll marry yeh !’ I gasped—l
was desperate. ‘ I’ll marry yeh—l will, s’elp
me!’ She made me write it on a bit o’ paper
with a pencil, 'n’ then she went fer Myall and
Tincliff, ’n’ I was cut out ’n’ sent t’ th’ ’orspital.
Now she wants me t’ stick t’ me promise, but th’
lawyer sez promises obtained under undoo pres-
sure don’t hold good, ’n’ if er five-ton gum butt
on top of a bloke ain’t undoo pressure, I give it
up, that’s all.”
A FINE POINT.
“ It’s an insult to a pig to call this pork!"
“No offence intended , sir!"
SHADOW OF DEATH HOTEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out on the Back Plains hot and dry,
Out where the mirage gleams,
Out where the red remorseless sky
A crimson ribbon seems.
Old and roomy it stands alone,
Looking across the plain—
Half-way house to the Great Unknown
From the cities of Greed and Gain.
All its windows ablaze with light
(Oh, the Wanderers know it well!)
A beacon fire ’mid the gloom of night
The Shadow of Death Hotel.
Many a straggler battling back
Towards the coast-line far away,
Somehow loses the usual track
In the maze of saltbush grey ;
Roaming on till the day is dead
And a million stars shine pale,
Hearing the night-birds overhead,
And the fierce-eyed dingo’s wail;
Till far in front the warm lights flare —
(So I’ve heard the Wanderers tell!)
That night he sups on the best of fare
At the Shadow of Death Hotel.
Many there are that rest awhile,
Then pack their “ drums ” and go
Back to the dust-clad rank and file,
With footsteps weary and slow.
Others there are who linger long
Heeding not loved ones far,
And they raise their voices in wild, mad song
With dead mates at the bar.
Their grand old songs ring ouc on high
(Old mates, how the echoes swell 1)
As the last brief hours of Life fly by
At the Shadow of Death Hotel.
Out where the Drought-Fiends ever tread
Dust Tracks on the sun-burnt plain ;
Out where the scrub is waste and dead,
With never a sign of rain ;
Out where the luring mirage gleams
Under a blood-red sky ;
Out in the land of dead men’s dreams,
Out where the whirlwinds fly.
Where the blighted land has a heart of stone,
Where the cross-road leads to Hell.
Half-way house to the Great Unknown
Is the Shadow of Death Hotel!
V'C.

“Sapphire”: I have met “The Proo-
shan’s” lizard (B. 30/7/’08) many times in New
England (N.S. W.), and also near the Wilson River.
Touch one with a stick, and off flies an inch of tail.
Forthwith the vagrant inch grows madly agitated.
It begins boring operations. It spins round like
a demented top. For about three minutes the
earth is violently disturbed in its vicinity ; and
then all is quiet, and the inch lies down and rests.
It seems to be dovetailed to the remainder of the
lizard, and bushmen assert stoutly that its late
owner will return, if it gets a chance, and couple
up again with the section that has been reft from
it. These lir'rds are known colloquially as
“ wood adders,” and one generally meets them on
dead ironbarks. They are “ pyzonous ” all right.
Six inches of adder and our pup had an argument
one grey, sad day. The adder fled after a while,
but it left its tail on “Tiny’s” nose. Tiny
“crossed the bar” moaning.

“Say, can I sell you a good rheumatic lotion!
“No, git it at the pub.”

“I’ve got a dandy hair wash.”
“So's the pub.”
“ You seem to buy everything at the pub.”
“Oh no! Only whisky.”
“Kapai Te Tuna”: “Rocket” (B.
9/7/’08) doubts my qualifications as eel-expert.
Well, here is my diploma: Have eeled for 30
years in every waterhole, creek, and river between
Bluff and Moeraki (M.L.). In that time I have
caughteels from sin. long to 261 b. weight, and by the
following methods—spearing, shooting, gaffing,
dynamiting, guzzling, hooking, “bobbing,” hypno-
tising, and with eel-pot. Have eaten them baked,
boiled, fried, stewed, stuck-up, hashed, fricasseed,
raw, and sun-dried. Have worn eelskin around
my throat as a cure for laryngitis, next the skin
as a cold prophylactic, and on my feet as a chil-
blain eradicator. Have used the “floats” as
finger-stalls, and the rendered fat as a hair tonic.
Can “Rocket” say as much? I am glad
“Rocket ” told the world about the four-pounder
he got in the waterhole, because I have often
wondered what became of the tuna that I dragged
along the road in the dim past, and threw in that
self-same hole.
“ Cross Nib’s ” tale :—Fags’ reputation
was that of a swift mover till the fever tapped
him. Then a nurse, a thermometer and slops
hobbled him for about a month. When the hos-
pital people passed him out into the wider atmo-
sphere he was convex as to the waist-band, and
otherwise demoralised. So he sought work. The
boss subjected him to interrogation which Fags
answered in non-committal manner. His former
occupations savored of variety ; they ranged from
dairy-hand to shearing-machine expert. After
mature consideration the boss conferred upon him
the office of wood and water joey. The first item
upon his list of duties was to become attached to
a cow, and tear from the same lacteal refreshment
for all and sundry. Bucket in hand he sallied forth
next morning. The cow was eyeing her progeny
with affectionate placidity
when he arrived at the
yard, but upon observing
the approaching Fags
she retreated in dis-
order to the scrub.
Waving the bucket
wildly, he follow-
ed in pursuit. An
hour latter the
cow appeared with
Fags profanely
perspiring in the
rear. Amidst a
shower of miscel-
laneous missiles,
including Fags’
battered str a w
hat, 'she rushed
into the stock-
yard. Fags shut
the gate, find sat
down wearily
upon the up-
turned tin uten-
sil, waiting for his
temperature to
fall. In the inter-
val he delivered a
passionate oration,
punctuated with
many florid ad-
jectives, in which
the job and the
cow were referred
to with peculiar
pertinacity. Then
as his volubility
gradually dimin-
ished, he began to
take an interest
in the day’s work,
After a few min-
utes’ strenuous
pushing, accom-
panied by tail-
twisting, he suc-
ceeded in getting the
animal into the bail.
Fags then hung on to
her glandular conformation.
intent on annexing the calf’s cordial.
For a while she exhibited no interest in the
massaging operations. Suddenly came protest.
Standing upon three legs, she stirred up the
atmosphere with her off hind foot; but Fags,
confident in the retarding influence supplied by
the leg-rope, tugged away briskly. He con-
tinued for a few minutes. Inop-
portunely the fleshy tripod slipped
upon some spilt milk and capsized.
Ah Mee, the cook, hearing a despair-
ing shriek from the vicinity of the
stockyard, arrived in hot haste.
With the aid of an old shaft he
endeavored to lever the bovine from
Fags, recumbent. Happily for that
worthy, the leg-rope became de-
tached, and the animal resumed its
feet. Fags, upon his release, grasped
the milking-stool, and most ungrate-
fully smote the Pagan upon the
brow. “ That’s one f’r y’r guessin’
gear, y’r curry-faced squib,” he
hissed. “I’ll teach y’r t’ shove th’
ruddy ruminator on top uv me, y’r
stale-fat fakir.” Ah Mee, fearing a
recurrence of the milking-furniture,
decamped to the kitchen, locked the
door, and therebehind lamented like
unto a chained canine when the orb
of night is effulgent. The boss, dis-
turbed by the discord, proceeded to
the culinary department and re-
quested information respecting the
cause of the trouble. He received it
via the keyhole. At midday Fags
was following a former vocation—to
wit, supporting the bagman’s bundle.
* * *
The present Victorian Go-
vernment’s genius for bungling mostly
everything it puts its hoof into was
exhibited a little more thanusualaday
or two ago. Pursuing its policy of
aping the large benevolent personage, it sent 16
of the unemployed down to Trafalgar. That
gorgeous creative mistake, the Moe Swamp, hangs
out in those parts ; and the 16 had to get to
work on it. Arrived at Trafalgar, there was
no official to pilot them to the scene of the
accident, he being somewhere else engaged
on something or other. So the men led
the simple life in the local railway shed, and
managed to subsist on victuals supplied on tick
by a friendly storekeeper. The following day
the official turned up from otherwhere, and forth-
with led them to where picturesqueness had gone
insolvent. Having set them to work, he got lost
in the horizon for another day, by which time
their food was showing a deficit. When he tran-
spired again, they mentioned the subject of their
yearning interiors, and he, after informing them
that the poultry, etc., would be forwarded in-
stanter, concealed himself in the distance with
great success once more. They waited and
waited, and eventually were forced to con-
clude that the live-stock had perished from its
own previous deadness. Nothing had arrived but a
bitter wind and heavy rain. Gradually they were
reduced to stale bread and spuds. Gradually,
too, even these things became absent, and, as the
expert aforesaid was in the same condition, seven
of the workers made for Melbourne—three per
train, and four per hoof—leaving the other nine to
finish the banquet, and to start on the consump-
tion of the swamp when the festivities were over.
And as the swamp isn’t edible at this time of the
year, they will have to eke it out with their boots
and pick-handles. Bent always did treat his un-
employed luxuriously.
“ Gundy ” does his boss a service :
Was working for a dairyman recently, and had put in
most of my time skinning dead-uns. The district has for
years been infested with sable hawkers, on the lookout
for a night’s camp. I was sent to show Smoogee Britth
into the shed, one dark night, with strict injunctions
from the boss to do the deed in the dark, as the hay was
uninsured. I forgot to mention that there was a cowhide
pegged on the shed floor, and the darky camped fair on
it. Half-a-dozen dry skins hung above him, and when he
arrived at the kitchen, at breakfast time, with foam in
his whiskers and a hayfork in his hand, he was a tem-
pestuous and somewhat undressed Pagan. He inquired
earnestly for me. It took the boss all he knew to con-
vince the Hindu that the occurrence was due to my
ignorance of his religions prejudices. During the six
months which elapsed between that episode and my
honorable retirement from the cocky’s service, there was
never sight or smell of a Hindu about the homestead.

Alexander Montgomery, the braw Maori-
land Scot who is the one man in Australia to have
the job of State mining engineer, lately made an
inventory of the teams dragging things over the
tracks which branch out from Mt. Magnet,
in the wild north-west of Westralia, and
mostly pull up at Nunngarra or Sandstone
Horse teams, 33 ; camel teams, 6 ; donkey teams,
10; mule ditto, 2. There were some light horse
teams besides, but that list is relied upon to fairly
show the relative popularity of the various beasts
that the “bullocky” swears at in the West.
Curiously enough, there isn’t one real bullock in
the lot.
“ Poodgeregah ” : Re Harry Stockdale’s
criticism of the Arab horse (B. 23/7/’08), in which
he says:—
He has much to recommend him, in his good looks, his
docility, his courage, and, above all, in the incomparable
soundness of his legs and feet.
I agree with him as regards the docility and
tracta’oility of Arab stock, and I have seen scores
of them handled and broken-in, as two,- three- and
four-year-olds; but as regards being “incom-
parably sound” on their legs and feet, he is
undoubtedly wrong. I know two stations in
Southern Queensland that had Arab horses stand-
ing for a couple of seasons, and then gave them up
in disgust, as 90 percent, of the stock were weedy
and unsafe in the legs. The sire in each case was
a fine-looking horse, and was crossed with big,
well-bred mares.
THE BUSHMEN OF THE PAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VIC.
Though they be ours, the splendid skies,
The prospects wide and vast,
They once belonged to other eyes—
The Bushmen’s of the Past.
They knew the old familiar trees,
The lichen-mottled boles,
The hollow limb that harbored bees,
The hoof-marred waterholes.
They knew the saplings, tall and thin,
The yellow, wheel-ridged ruts,
And the gre}' tracks that twisted in
To little grey-slabb. d huts.
They bad their troubles on the roads
With bullock-team and dray ;
With shaking bogs, and shifted loads,
And horses gone astray.
Through all those little things ol life
We reach to them our hand ;
The petty details of their strife
We know and understand.
The star-bestudded night above,
The arc of splendor vast,
Reminds us of their sturdy love—
The Bushmen of the Past
Who valued all things high and great ;
And, being men that build,
They roughly hewed the frame of State
That we thereafter gild.
They loved the things we love, that were
Keen spirits, kind and just—
That much of comradeship we bear
To their forgotten dust 1
Still, in life’s little things akin,
Dike us they feel—who knows?
Some breath of August enter in
When white epacris blows.
So we, linked still to them with lore
Of life in bush-ways cast,
Hail those strong souls who went before—
The Bushmen of the Past.
C. G. A. COLLES.
DAM THE RAIN!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Oh dam the rain !” the bushman said.
“ Oh dam, dam, dam ; oh dam the rain ! ”
The parson gravely shook his head ;
Such dreadful language gave him pain !
“ Consider, friend,” said he, “you know
How you have fared in seasons dry,
When scarce a blade of grass would grow
Beneath a cloudless brassy sky ! ”
“ Oh dam the rain ! ” the bushman cried.
In angry accents yet again ;
Although the sorrowing parson sighed,
“ Oh dam, dam, dam ; oh dam the rain ! ”
The parson turned to leave in haste.
“ Hold hard !” the bushman cried, “’tis plain
We let this rainfall go to waste
Whereas we ought to dam the rain 1 ”
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
Lyme X)ror: A good deal of foolishness is
written ahout “ the Blood of our Brother ” and how ic
is “crying from the ground.” If the inferior and un-
progressive races are wantonly killed off, that is plain
murder. If they obligingly die off, that is another thing.
The time must come in this rapidly-filling-up world when
murder of a kind—the murder that consists of the pre-
vention of birth or the slaying of the unborn—will be
necessary through lack of sustenance. And if we, by
strenuous efforts, fill the earth with semi-savage, or semi-
developed or unprogressive races, the progressive races
may later on have to commit suicide to make room. If
the African pigmj', for instance, has no future, why
should we want to encourage the pigmy to multiply
and occupy space that is none too abundant, especi-
ally' if the pigmy has no wild desire to multiply?....

wild indignation about “these respectable citizens.”....

exceeds 630 miles.” Probably if you called the whole lot
650 miles it would be a liberal allowance. (See Nelson’s

“ Henry Phelthatt ” : While all hands are
busy looking for traces of the longest ride, would
you please ask them to keep a lookout for the
longest walk accomplished by any swagged ped.
in one day—length of day no object, so long as it
doesn’t exceed 24 hours. What stands as a record
tramp in these parts was put up by Peter the
Kanaka, a giant of Otaheite. He carried his sick
mate, a white man of average size, and his swag,
also of average size, from Pyramid Creek
(N.S.W.) to Hill End hospital, distance 18 miles.
He used to leave the man and then go back for the
swag, then deposit the swag and proceed with the
man, thus covering the ground three times. Peter
handed the invalid over to an attendant, remark-
ing, “By G.hosh ! heem heavy ! ” Whereupon he
footed it back to Pyramid Creek again. For
stamina and dog-like faithfulness this performance
will be hard to vanquish. Though it took place
40 years ago, it is still a much-talked-of feat
throughout the district.

“ D.” writes ;
, Leopold of Belgium fills up the cup of his subjects’ in-
dignation about once a week these days. Last December
a motor-car belonging to him, and occupied by one of his
young lady friends, ran over a cyclist. The car didn’t,
stop ; it is a fixed practice with Leopold’s chauffeurs
never to stop and examine into the condition of flattened
cyclists. A month ago, an action was brought, by the
Public Prosecutor of Brussels against the chauffeur in
connection with this happening. Leopold, believing
that he was the defendant,, hurriedly put in a plea of
“ diplomatic immunity,” This lie declined to withdraw
till assured that the chauffeur Only was being assailed.
In the end, the drivel wits fined add Ordered to pay
his victim £2O damages, The spectacle Of their Sovereign
pleading “ diplomatic immunity ” to get out of squaring
Up With a citizen who had been crippled through his
agency is Understood to have giyeti the Belgians a worse
turn thail the arrival of Leopolds last infant.
“Sr,”: Beere, the man to Whom Box
Judge, of Melbourne, gave 12 months for a six-
and-sixpenny fraud, possibly deserved more con-
sideration than he received. Bor months Beere
has been an eccentric institution round the city,
and his doings in connection with an American

box appearances and disappearances in many other
respects have suggested doubts as to his sanity.
So did the amazing speech which he made to

isn’t much, anyhow; in the same paper which
told how Box fell like an avalanche on Beere over
that small amount 1 saw that a man filed his
schedule for £lO,OOO.
Maoriland’s Parliament misses an old
identity this session through the removal of
L. M. Grace, for many years chief Maori inter-
preter to the House of Reps., and now promoted
to the editorship of the Maori edition of the
Government Gazette. He was the son of an
Anglican missionary at Taupo, right in the heart
of the native country, and was reared amongst
the semi-civilised tribes of that wild region. Bo
D. M. Grace and his brothers became proficient
Maori scholars at an early age, and they turned
their knowledge to useful account in after years
as historians and linguists. Grace is recognised
as one of the greatest living authorities on Maori
laws, customs and language. The billet of inter-
preter -to the House of Reps, will now be filled
entirely by Grace’s colleague, D. F. G. Barclay.
Barclay has had some strange experiences in his
time, one of them being when the irascible Wi
Pere assaulted him on the floor of the Legislative
Chamber during the progress of a debate, because
Barclay drew the line at some of Wi’s language.
Wi’s language was fairly violent at times.
Harnett, manager of the British football
team, maddened the editor of the Wanganui
(M.L.) paper (also several others) by his stric-
tures on the beer, morals, etc., of God’s own
country. This is how Wanganui smites its de-
tractor :
When Mr. Harnett brought his team to Wanganui he
called in to tell us about it, and for a full ten minutes
talked with all the force ot a tornado. His language was
scarcely suited to a drawing-room. Some people not
over-squeamish might have called it ratherdirtv. He in-
vited us to believe that while he had been forced, in In-
vercargill, to remain as dry as dust, heaps of other folks
were literally tumbling over one another in a disgusting
medley of cosmopolitan drunkenness. People were drunk
in the streets and drunk in the houses—it was a case of
whisky everywhere, and not a drop to drink ! His wiudv
tirade was so obviously exaggerated and so manifestly
ridiculous that we declined to lake his denunciation of
Invercargill any more seriously than we did his blatant
assertion that Dunedin was absolutely the most immoral
city he had ever “ struck ” ; that his men had been per-
sistently pestered by young girls, and that, taking one
thing with the other, while this sort of tiling might be
good enough for Maorilanders, it didn’t suit an English-
man of his calibre.
About an alarming nigger :
Australia has a record-breaker of a kind in William
King, who faced the P’ran (Vic.) court one dav latelv on
a charge of burglary with violence. William is a nigger
of magnificent physique, and as dangerous as maybe.
For the greater portion of the last 20 years the colored
alien has been cooling off in his Majesty’s sanatorium at
Coburg, where he has given exhibitions of ferocity that
have made him ten times an infernal nuisance, and no
less of a terror. Whether inside or out, he lias offered
strong suggestion of mental derangement; but no one—
much less the law—has thought of treating Willie as a
criminal lunatic. His rare spells of liberty have been
brief—simply because his crime lust is quite irrestrain-
able. Of him it may be said that the King can do no
right. His more frequent spells of gaol have been long,
because his crimes were attended, almost alwavs, by
abnormal practices. His burglary was nearly always
supplemented by a filthy defilement of the- place he
robbed ; and, if disturbed, he was invariably a wild beast.
More by luck than judgment he escaped the hangman
on one or two occasions. His last stretch was for ten
years, awarded on Ist June, 1900. He was released on
15th ult., though, on account of his unpleasant conduct
while in his Majesty’s jug, he would not have been en-
titled to quit until 12th February next. Apparently, he
was turned loose on somebody’s recommendation, but,
judging by his last two or three outrages, he should
never have been liberated at all. He received his ten
years for a couple of bimrlaries, one of which was at-
tended by a merciless attack on a young girl at P’ran,
under circumstances too horrible for general mention. He
had been out just 17 days when he was up again, accused
of entering another dwelling and of nearly strangling a
lady in bed. In all, William is too dangerous to be at
large, and looks like the fittest candidate for an indeter-
minate sentence or a lunatic asylum that has appeared
up to date. Anyhow, P’ran is really thankful that, he has
been gathered in again. During his 17 days of liberty,
those that knew of him lived in a State of jumps arid
timorous speculations. They understood him far better
than the law does.
EXPERIENTIA DOCET.
EDITOR (to budding novelist): “Yes, that yarn
of yours was very good. You appear to
have had some experience in fiction."
THE ASPIRANT (cheerfully): “Oh, yes! I used to explain things for a land-agent."
“Bill”: Should Bryan, the Democratic
nominee for the TJ.S. Presidency, succeed in
defeating Taft, the Republican nominee, be will
be the first man who ever Won at the third attempt,
Jefferson, Jackson and Glover Cleveland were the
only ones to be elected after even a first defeat.
Cleveland’s case is unique. He was elected in
1884, defeated in 1888, and elected again in 1892.
The notion regarding the hopelessness of the case
of a candidate who has been twice defeated
amounts to almost a superstition in Yankeeland.
A VICTORIAN VISION.
No, it is not that at all I It is Governor
Carmichael standing alongside of Premier Bent.
Chief Justice Griffith delivered his Trade
Marks judgment in the icy, unemotional tone that
used to aggravate the hot-blooded politicians of
the old Queensland Parliament. The Palmers,
Mcllwraiths, and Lumley Hills were wont to call
Griffith names on account of his frigidity. The
average temperature of the Queensland Assembly
towards midnight in those flushed and drinking
times used to rise to about 100°, and Moll wraith’s
head used to smoke visibly. Then Griffith would
make a “deliverance” —people got into the habit
of calling his speeches “deliverances’’—that was
like iced soda without a stick in it, and the tem-
perature would drop so violently that Mcllwraith
would reach for his overcoat. It is said that it
was the draught from one of Griffith’s speeches
that started Moll wraith’s fatal illness.

Lord Crewe, speaking of Lord Dudley, Australia’s future
Governor-General, rejoiced in his capacity to do serious
work “without abandoning the joie de vivre which was
the possession that every man ih public life ought to
cling- to with the utmost desperation,”—S. M. HeralD

There came a time Wheh the pub’s cheery lights
No more attracted me ;
Arguments left me Cold ; eVen dog-fights
1 eyed lack-lUstrel.V.
BeCr lost its savor ; so did whisky, riini
Aild gin. 1 sourly steered
Away from drink, add remained coid and ddmli,
Wliile gladsome dipsos, cheered.
You Would have felt disgusted had you irtet
Me at a music-hall .
In those drear days. The dainty footlights’ pet
Allured me not at all.
Her red,nosed friend, the lodger, 1 abhorred ;
No adulat’ry smiles
He Won from me, for f was tired and bored
With lodgers and their wiles.
Then, the Administration of the day
A Justice of the Peace
Created me, I fell to straightaway.
And struggled sans surcease—
Struggled ’gainst Gloom, and on behalf of Glee ;
Being in Public Life,
I joined (on principle) in revelry
Wherever it was rife.
’Tu ns bitter hard at first to have to quaff
Champagne and smoke cigars,
And merry make, and utter the loud laugh
That on sta d virtue jars,
I loathed embracing ladies, yet I grew
A jaw, and kissed them all,
Not once, but many times—for thus I knew
I answered Duty’s call.
Day after day I fouglit the weary fight
Nor Joy of Life. At last
Through a thick drink-haze one beer-smitten night
I saw the worst was past.
Blind in a bar I sat, bubbling with fnn,
A barmaid on each knee ;
Joy hal returned, and the good fight was done—
Mine was the victory*

PRICE, AND HIS OMINOUS COLLAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The speaker wore a high collar.—S. M. Herald’s
pained commentary ( S/8/’08 ) on J. E. Price, Socialist.
I sing of Price, J. E.
Pew philosophic citizens will swear
About the way a Price adjusts his hair ;
Ho one forbids a Socialist to wear
Hat, boots—even of socks a modest pair ;
But this arrests one’s journalistic flair,
Shocking it sore—or, as the Scotch say, “sair.”
It should not, should not be.
Price Wore his collar high !
Record the shameful fact.on lasting brass,
Scarify this Invader of our class,
Things have, in sooth, attained a pretty pass
When Prices—common fellows, callow, crass—
Pre-empt a patch on the sartorial grass
Reserved till now for folks outside the mass
Of those who Greid defy.
How coarsely insolent!
It’s not as though J. Price were in a bank
Or wholesale house ; if so the world would thank
Him for his neck-gear, eloquent of rank
And social standing. Price calls liquor “ swank ”
One doubts not ; and strikes matches on his flank
And swears—in fact’s an anarchistic crank,
Possibly violent.
Price brings stark Pear on deck.
He, I feel sure’s the Dread G. Reid foretold
A while ago. Banks will yield up their gold
To such as he. Red Sosh will get a hold.
In Paddy’s Market maidens will be sold
By weight. I see the Serpent Drink unfold
Its alcoholic tail and grow more bold
Por lack of scotching. Friends I would not scold
Unduly, but my blood runs icy cold
What time I brood on Price, and his throat-mould.
An omen ’tis — Anti-Sosh drowned and rolled,
Mangled ’boat Labor’s neck !
N.S.W.
D. JOHN.
