ONLY ONE WAY TO CUEE
KHEUMATISM
Drive it Out op the Blood,
A Severe Case Cured This Way by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills—Suffered for years
—Knees Stiff and Painpul—Hobbling
About on a Stick—Not an Ache or
Pain Now.
Because rheumatism is a blood disease, abd Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills are a medicine for the blood,
there’s hope and healing for pain-racked rheu-
matics in every dose of them. Pill by pill, dose
by dose they help to form new health-giving blood
free from the acid that causes the burning twinges
of rheumatism. The cure begins with the
first dose.
Because rheumatism is in the blood, liniments,
lotions and hot applications won’t cure it. It’s not
common-sense to expect it. Rheumatism isn’t
skin deep. It’s blood deep. Therefore to cure it:
a blood medicine.
That Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured rheu-
matism isn’t a claim and a boast: it’s an estab-
lished fact. Week after week reports of cures
have appeared in the newspapers. Some hundreds
of cases have been published all over Australia,
with name and street address of the cured person
given. It’s open to anyone who lives near to call
and find out for themselves. Amongst the latest
cures is Mrs. Mary Schaefer, 29 Middle Street,
Sydney, who says
“Even as a girl I was always subject to rheu-
matism,” said Mrs. Schaefer. “At first both
shins were affected. Later on in life it attacked
me in both hips—in fact, the pain was all up my
legs. I’d get rid of it for a little while every now
and then, but it always came back. For years I
walked with a stick'about the house, and never
ventured outside without one. I always had a
gnawing, nagging pain in both legs. I felt it
especially when I got warm in bed, and I’d lie
awake for hours. When rain was hanging about
I’d always feel worse. If I moved, a spasm of
pain would shoot through me, and make me cry
out. I could only hobble about. My knees ached,
and got so stiff that I could not kneel—to do a
little scrubbing was out of the question. Fortu-
nately, I saw Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People advertised, and I thought that I would give
them a trial. The second box did me good. When
I started the third box the stiffness was not nearly
so bad. From that on I began to improve rapidly,
the constant nagging pain left my legs, and I
could sleep at night. In the end, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills cured my rheumatism completely, and
I have never had a return of it since.”
If you are not quite sure whether your illness is
caused by bad blood, write to The Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co. of Australasia, Ltd., Sydney.
From the same address you can also order the
genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills by mail, 2s. 9d.
a box ; six boxes 15s. 3d. post free. Hints as to
diet, etc., sent free.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A troubled ghost hoisted itself up and
waved a tentacle at the Speaker in N.S.W.
Assembly last week, when Varney Parkes,
M.L.A., thought he had found somewhere an
“understanding ” to the effect that the Australian
Parliament was to be out of Melbourne by the end
of 1908. The Government regretfully informed
Parkes that it couldn’t find the alleged under-
standing, and the subject dropped on the floor.
Some curious charges are brought against
the Australian Government these times. Said Trea-
surer Wilson of Westralia in a recent two-column
wail: “ The point on which I now join issue is
that they propose to charge loan expenditure to
revenue And the awful Wilson seemed to really
believe that the Commonwealth, its sin of not
getting into debt being thus exposed, would
stagger out covered with shame, and hang itself
by the neck on a thistle.
STOLEN BLESSINGS.
Isaaac said: “The voice is the voice of Jacob, but the
hands are the hands of Esau.... Art thou my very son
Exau?” And he Jacob said, “lam! ’’—Scripture.
The Bent Government makes the most Liberal
promises, but it makes them out of a Conservative
mouth. —AGE.
In 1906 and 1907 the number of em-
ployees in the factories of Sydney and suburbs
increased by 9036, and the number of factories
increased by 150. It isn’t a bad result for a city
that is supposed to be ruined by Protection and
Federal tyranny. If the Australian Government
has any more ruins of the kind to spare,
Sydney will accept them thankfully. In fact, it
is just yearning for ruins.
A co. with £lOO,OOO capital expects, thanks
to the new Australian tariff, to have a big, new
woollen mill at work at Marrickville (Sydney)
by next February. Twenty-two years agoN.S.W.
had eight woollen mills, but despite the fact that
the best wool supply on earth was at its doors the
industry faded under the Freetrade curse till only
three survived. Now there are five, and more are
coming. Yet the anti-Australian and importing
person still holds forth in the Sydney papers about
the tariff being devised “ wholly in Victoria’s in-
terests.”
Laborite Cornelius O’Brien, who won the
Central Province Legislative Council election in
Westralia, got there by the sharpest nose on
record. The verdict at the declaration of the poll
was “ a dead heat.” The returning officer
gave his casting vote for O’Brien. A recount of
all formal ballot papers then took place and the
result gave O’Brien a lead of five. The votes
rejected as informal were then gone through and
equitable re-adjustment produced another dead
heat. Once more .the returning officer’s casting
vote was added to the figure total, and this time
Cornelius definitely squeezed home.
It is always the unexpected that happens,
and in Maoriland a fool stipendiary magistrate
who gave a girl three months for stealing a purse
and smote a wife-beater with a 20s. fine,
has proved a great law reformer. His vagaries
have roused Parliament, and the Inferior Courts
Procedure Bill contains a few salutary clauses
which will do something towards equalising the
punishment for offences against property and
offences against the person. At present wife-
beating is about the cheapest form of illegal
amusement in Maoriland, but when the new Act
is passed it is expected to become much dearer.
Judging by the brands on some com-
pressors and other heavy mining machinery
received the other day at Cloncurry (Q.), the Jap
has pushed his nose into that business too. Un-
less the brands lie, either the Monkey has been
up to his old trick of imitating well-known
American manufactures, or the American firm of
compressor and rock-drill makers has put up
works in Japan in order to make use of Japanese
engineers at 11s. a week and foundry laborers at
sd. a day. And that’s the sort of competition
that Knox (who bulks large in Cloncurry mining,
by the way), Irvine, and the rest of the “ Corner ”
crowd reckons can be met with a Protection of
about per cent !
A thousand times “the backbone of the
country” and “the great primary industries”
have been assured that they can’t possibly gain
anything from Protection and the encouragement
of manufactures. But the Farmer and Settler,
the sheet issued in the interests of the N.S.W.
Farmers and Settlers’ Association, says “Rats !”
to that, and blows in with the following :
According to the Chief Railway Commissioner, the
principal cause of delay in getting additional stock trucks,
when the shortage became evident, was the fact that the
N.S.W. Government imports its axles and wheels from
England. If we had had a healthy iron and steel industry
at Lithgow, whether protected by Customs duties or
encouraged by a bonus, we might have had those stock
trucks turned out expeditiously at local works. Under
the policy of buying- nothing at home that we can get
abroad a fraction cheaper, there is naturally great delay,
and our stock die while the required axles and wheels are
doing the grand tour of the world.
When the Sydney tram employees went on
strike Premier Wade insisted that the men should
go back to work unconditionally, and then he
would set the machinery of the Industrial Disputes
Act in motion. The men went back to work, but
so far the machinery of the Act is motionless.
When the rich ironmaster, Hoskins, locked out
his employees the Premier did not discover that
Mr. Hoskins was
a law breaker, nor
did he insist that
he should reopen
his works uncon-
ditionally till the
Court had de-
cided whether his
action was justifi-
able. The Court
is moving like the
mills of God, ex-
ceedingly slowq
and the works re-
main closed up to
time of writing.
This is one of the
proofs that the
Wade Act is ab-
solutely fair to
both parties, and
possibly ill us-
trates what Min-
ister Wood meant
when he said that
the strike would
show who was
“top dog.” It is
not often that a
Minister is frank enough to admit that he and his
crowd are dogs. Anyhow, if he was bound to
allude to animals, why didn’t he describe his
crowd as the cows on top ?
' Already whispers circulate about a “com-
pliment ” to N.S.W. Premier Wade in commemo-
ration of his “firm constitutional attitude ” during
the Sydney tram strike. The notion is to send
him to England on a holiday to gather in the
kudos that seems to abound there for men who
are, or are accounted to be, able to keep the Aus-
tralian workman in his place. Irvine is quoted as
a precedent. Further, Wade is encouraged to
take the Premiership with him. Of course, the
idea is that he should go on some little “ mission ”
or other which will serve as an excuse for a vote
of public money. Reid took £5OO away with him,
and Carruthers, though minus the Premiership,
bagged £l2OO.
By a lawyer :
The N.S.W. Industrial Disputes Act was supposed to
provide an absolutely final industrial court, from which
there could be no appeal. But already an attempt has been
made to “ prohibit ” a decision of the Court. The attempt
failed, but some of the Judges of the State Pull Court
seemed dubious. The “ unappealable Court,” as a matter
of fact, is a delusion. Its decision can always be appealed
against on the ground that in coming to the decision it
“exceeded its jurisdiction,” and, of course, when it ex-
ceeds its jurisdiction it is no longer an Arbitration Court,
unappealable or otherwise, but merely a gentleman in a
wig talking through his neck. The only way to make
the Arbitration Court absolutely final seems to be to pass
an Act stating that the Arbitration Judge can do what he
darned well pleases while sitting in his judicial capacity,
and, as he might then order Hoskins,,of Lithgow, to be
hanged, or a whole trades union to be beheaded with a
blunt axe, that wouldn’t do.
Sydney Telegraph, blowing the Free-
trade trumpet the other day, declared that,
owing to Freetrade, not one loaf of rye-bread, not
one dog or one horse is eaten in Britain. Well,
Ireland under Freetrade is often reduced to using
potato-bread, and the people of the north of Free-
trade Scotland live largely on oats. As to the
horse, how could the English railway servants
(say) on 16s. a week afford to eat horse ?
“ WHEN YOUR PANTS BEGIN TO GO."
Perth, Friday.—The police have had reported to them
a theft of pearls from Onslow, valued at between £2OOO
and £3OOO, the property of the brothers Dan and Louis
M’Daniel.
The story told by the brothers is that they decided to
secrete their pearls, and accordingly placed them in the
lining of a suit of clothes and in the heels of boots. This
apparel was then placed in an old portmanteau and left
at an hotel in Onslow.
This was last December, and the brothers returned to
their fleet. A few more pearls were discovered, and
months later one of the brothers returned to Onslow to
add these to the secreted store. He then found the port-
manteau had been stolen, presumably by someone hard
up for a suit of clothes.
The proposal that N.S.W. Parliament
should meet at 2.30 p.m, instead of 4.30 has failed
again. One time, in the old mixed Council days,
Parliament did meet at the earlier hour, but very
few members appeared till after four. The
Government used to rush through all the nefarious
jobs in that sparsely-attended interval. “ I would
advise,” said Robert Lowe, when elected for St.
Vincent, “ constituencies never to return a lawyer
to represent them in this Council, as while he is
detained in court the country may suffer.” He
added that he was late once, being detained in
court over a matter of 155., and he got to the
House to find a £26,000 swindle just put through.
Lowe was a loud shouter for political purity in
those days, and when there was a job to be done
it was postponed till the Government scout re-
ported that Lowe was busy somewhere.
“ Edyson ” writes : —-
Everything plays into the hands of the speculator, and
the “ shindykit,” and the Trust, When cold storage came
along, it was to be an undiluted blessing to mankind, but
it has been left too much to Private Enterprise, and
to-day it is the strongest instrument in the hands of the
blighter who is out to corner the people’s meals. No
sooner are there hints and indications of a coming short-
age in any line of perishable comestibles than the specu-
lator, who profits by the misfortunes of his kind, gets
after the available supplies, and hastens the climax by
cold-storing all the goods he can lay paws upon. The
coming of the Americans has established an enormous
demand for meat, fruit, vegetables, butter, eggs, etc., at
a time when there is an unfortunate natural shortage,
and the speculative harpies have stocked every available
foot of cold storage to take full advantage of a great
opportunity, with the result that the family of the day-
laborer in Melbourne is compelled to do something very
like a perish. This is likely to occasion a tepid welcome
for Uncle Samuel’s sailors on the part of the workers, who
are led to look upon the coming friendly invasion as some-
thing in the nature of a plague of locusts.
“Goril”: Willy Kelly told a bevy of
shuddering old women at Kensington (Sydney)
during the tram strike that —
He had heard it stated that if the Federal Labor Party
were in power the police would not be used in this con-
nection to maintain order. These were considerations of
the gravest moment. They would have to think of what
they would get if a Ministry holding those views were in
power.
Now I, “Goril,” heard it stated that if Willy
Kelly had been in power he would have ordered
the military to load up with explosive bullets, fire
promiscuously down George-street at everybody
in sight, and then, cursing horribly, to
charge with the bayonet on the assembled
masses, and give no quarter. Of course, I have no
proof that Willy would have done this, but that
doesn’t matter a hang. I maintain that my yarn
is more feasible than Willy’s, if only for this
reason : If W. Kelly had been in power, he would
have possessed some, authority over the military.
Had the Federal Labor Party been in power, it
would have had no more authority over the
N.S.W. police than has Long Lan Tun, taotai of
Swatow.
Stuart-Robertson, M.L.A. for Camper-
down, dealt N.S.W. Premier Wade a heavy one
on the jaw re the row that the Carruthers-Wade
Government made over the proposal to concen-
trate the Federal stamp-printing business at
Adelaide. People thought from the shindy that
arose that a most important industry was going to
be dragged from the Ma State. Instead of which,
it turns out the great industry amounts to 10 men,
one hand copper-plate machine, two letterpress
machines, two perforating machines, value in all
£1360, with a wage sheet of £1656. Premier-
Wade replied: “Are not ten men worth
fighting for?” They are; but so far Pre-
mier Wade has not shown any inclination
to fight for some hundreds or thousands of
men by having his railways material made in -
the State. If all the engines and rolling stock
of N.S.W. were made in home foundries, N.S.W.
would never miss the loss of the ten men in the
stamp printing office. But very naturally a man
with the eye of an insect could only detect little
things such as employ 10 men. Industries that
might employ thousands are invisible to the crea-
ture owing to the small range of its outlook.
The Bulletin notices with regret the
entire absence from the daily press of any scath-
ing references to the “mutiny” or “revolt” or
“rebellion” at Perth (W.A.). There certain
journalists, not content with the accommodation
provided for reporters at Parliament House, went
on strike—just as the Sydney tramway men did.
The effect of the “strike” was to paralyse the
Government of the country, for the House
wouldn’t proceed with its debates in the absence
of the reporters, and the session had to be hurried
to a close. But the daily papers seem to treat
the incident lightly ; and any sympathies they
have are with the reporters who have thus dared
to bail up the highest institution in the State.
From the “ Old Soldier ” :
Some people have “militarism” on the brain. The
S. M. Herald, the other day, announced that things
during the tram strike might have been as bad in Sydney
as they were in Paris if it hadn’t been “by reason of our
freedom from militarism.” As to that, note first this
from the Australian Defence Act. Section 98 kindly pro-
vides :—“No member of the Defence Force shall be sen-
tenced to death by any court-martial except for mutiny,
desertion to the enemy, or traitorously delivering up to
the enemy any garrison.” French “militarism” has
abolished even that remnant of savagery. When the
Clemenceau Government came into power, and General
Picquart was made War Minister, courts-martial were
abolished once for all in the French army. The Herald
doesn’t know that universal service is the very negation
of militarism, because young men of all classes are swept
into the ranks, and, while reasonable discipline is easily
maintained, arbitrary tyranny is sure to be resented with
effect. It is only volunteer armies like the British or the
Australian, or the French or German armies of the 18th
century, that require a Draconian system of punishments
to keep them in order.
Under the N.S.W. Liquor Law the
jeuncsse dore of the State must not play billiards
on licensed premises if under 18 years old (the
jcunesse dord, not the premises). Recently a
Uralla publican was fined £2 for permitting a
young gent to play on his table only 17 years and
10 months old (the young gent, not the table).
Bung strongly objected on the ground that the
cueist didn’t have his birth certificate about him,
and he (Bung) couldn’t tell his age by his teeth. He
couldn’t say whether the offending player was 17
and three months or 18 years and one month. Per
medium of expensive counsel he came all the way
to Sydney to ask Justice Sly if he could be
expected to. Mr. Sly agreed that it was prepos-
terous, or something like that, and some more of
the bottom fell out of Wade’s Beer Act.
Notable that the Deformers who snuffle
virtuously over the illegal conduct of tram drivers
and conductors in leaving.their cars as a protest
against inequitable treatment, are the same old
Deformers who supported Carruthers in violently
breaking the law, the other day, in regard to his
wire-netting seizure. Deform argues that the
tramway strike leaders deserve exclusion from
their billets for their “contemptuous treatment of
the law of the land,” and in the same breath
urges that the law-contemptuous Carruthers
should be paid special allowances to represent Ma
State in England. Deformers are inconsistent
people, and Deform is a muddled, illogical old
creed in very sooth these days. ■■ ■• ,
BULL'S BOUNTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the Old-Age Pensions Bill was before the House
of Lords, Lord Cromer said it was a “ financial revolu-
tion.” Viscount St. Aldwyn said, “The financial aspects
are very grave.” Lord Lansdowne said if the Bill were
passed “ the country must stand in a much weaker posi-
tion. It is a burden from which the country will never
secure emancipation.” Lord Rosebery said, “It is
Socialism, pure and simple, and the prospect fills me with
despair.”
The British lord delights in thrift
And dignity of Labor.
Workers, till you are dead, uplift
Bull’s pick or saw or sabre.
A helping hand is proffer-ed
To Toil; the agitator
Can draw a pension when he’s dead,
Or thereabouts, or later.
Why should the Briton emigrate
And be a reckless roamer ?
John Bull’s new bounty runs the State
To ruin, says Lord Cromer.
If British working men have sense
To sweat enough, and meekly,
At 65, in affluence
They’ll draw their Five Bob weekly.
Lords grind false teeth, and pull their hair
In wild exasperation.
The prospect fills them with despair.
It weakens Balfour’s nation.
“ Why should we help the working men,”
They cry, “ to reckless living?
We’ll get much Cheaper Labor when
No idle bread we’re giving.
“ With fireless room and empty maw
Let Toil be aye surrounded 1 ”
This is the Gospel and the Law
By Noble Lords expounded.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ . Sydney, August 10, 1908.
My Dear Arini,—
Orgies, mostly made up of frocks, food,
scandal, Bridge, frenzied finance, and the break-
ing of one of the most important of the com-
mandments, is what Upton Sinclair’s new book
lhe Metropolis ’ (Roycroft Library) suggests,
it is not literature. Upton doesn’t write litera-
ture. Large, crude slabs of allegations about the
Smart Set—the New York Four Hundred-
are thrown at the reader like coals, and
remind one of the large masses of unsavory
animal food in the same writer’s “Jungle.”
Some of the statements about the efforts of the
women to out-dress and outmoneyscatter (I in-
vented that word) one another make the imagina-
tion stretch out like a racked indiarubber heretic.
One woman has a 200,000-dollar fur coat, but she
is eclipsed by another, who owns a coat worth
1,000,000 dollars. This coat is insured for 500,000
dollars. It is made of the soft plumage of rare
birds from Hawaii, where the royal house of
Kamehameha used to hang out, and it took
20 years to build. Every feather was crescent-
shaped, and there were wonderful designs in gold
crimson and black. A leader of the Smart Set
asks the hero to come home with her “to see her
babies.” Up she goes in his estimation. But the
babies are a pair of tiny dogs. (That idea isn’t
new; Mark Twain used it up 35 years ago
ln .j le Gilded Age.”) For these animals the
horrid female keeps an expert nurse, to whom she
pays 100 dollars a month ; also the brutes have a
footman and a special cuisine, a regular dentist
and a physician. “ I know women,” says some-
one, “ who have a dozen sets of furs —ermine,
chinchilla, black fox, baby lamb, and musk and
sable ; and I know a man whose chauffeur quit
him because he wouldn’t buy him a 10,000 dollar
fur coat.”
In Sinclair s amazing tale, several thousand
dollars are paid for gowns that are only worn
twice or thrice. One Smart Setter, who would
pay 50,000 dollars “at a clip” for a few frocks,
never wore hers more than once. Then her
butler solemnly cremated them as if he were
burning the dead. Another had just had 14
trunks of gowns sent up from the Customs House.
Each one came stuffed with tissue paper, and
attached to each was a piece of the material from
which the shoemaker would make shoes or slippers
to match. There were street costumes, tea-gowns,
ball and dinner gowns. Most of the latter were
to be embroidered with jewellery before they were
worn, and imitation gems were sewn on to show
where the real ones should be placed. Some of
these gowns were so filmy that the women who
made them had to sit in damp cellars, because the
sunlight would dry the fine threads, and they
would break ; a single yard of the lace repre-
sented 40 days of labor, and it was so fine that it
was hardly even rabbit-proof. There was a
pastel batiste de soie Pompadour robe that
had cost 1000 dollars; there was a hat to
go with it that had cost 125, and shoes of grey
antelope skin, buckled with mother-of-pearl, that
had cost 40. Then there was a gorgeous and
intricate ball dress of pale-green chiffon satin,
with orchids embroidered in oxidised silver, and a
long Court train studded with diamonds. This
cost 6000 dollars, without the jewels.
None of these Smart Setters would consider her
wardrobe complete without coats and opera wraps
of “ white unborn baby lamb.” The fact that the
baby lamb fur is “unborn” is stressed until the
reader gets the exhausted sensation. Gold or
jewelled-handled parasols are made to match each
gown. A woman, married or unmarried, explains
one of the leaders, might travel with a man all
over Europe, and everyone know she did so, but
it would make no difference so long as she did not
do it in America. A comic opera prima donna
was engaged by a bunch of Trust magnates to sing
at a banquet. She arrived towards the close of
the feast, and her song was “Won’t You Come
and Play With Me ? ” At the third verse the
magnates took the invitation literally, and the
lady escaped from the room with half her clothing
torn off her. If “The Metropolis” be only
half a correct picture, the guns of the American
Fleet ought to bombard the two miles of Fifth
Avenue, and Madison Avenue, and Riverside
Drive—of course when the whole of the collective
millionaire and family is at home.
Reception by the Ladies of Sydney
to .
Mrs. Noah Brusso
(Mrs. Tommy Burns).
The remainder of the invitation doesn’t matter.
I’d rather miss the Northcotes’ ball in Fleet week
than that function. It occurs to-morrow. If I
could only induce Mrs. Tommy Burns, after the
tea and cake, to do six rounds with one of our
Smart Set for a reasonable purse !
lhe attractive little widow of an English rector,
Mrs. Venables Williams, daughter of Sir William
Pink, of Shrover Hall, Hants, who arrived
by the Mongolia, and was married to Mr.
J. Hamilton Dobbie at Scots’ Church, Mel-
bourne, to-day. The wedded brace will live
m a pretty humpy, known as “The Camp,” at
Double Bay. The father of the bridegroom-elect
was a dour Scotch parson who used to correspond
some with the late Queen Victoria on the Seven
Deadly Virtues—and dogs. He was a very doggy
man, and on the subject of collies was more read-
able than when he was discoursing on the impor-
tant and picturesque. Sonny J. H. used to be an
enthusiastic dog exhibitor in this State. Major
George Steward, official secretary to the Governor-
General, lent his moral support to the bridegroom
at the ceremony when the Voice Breathed.
Arrangements for the reception and swearing-in
of the Dudley personage are in the hands of Major
Steward, who will come to Sydney to take
delivery of the valuable package.
Three bright-haired, pinky-faced sisters, Him-
melhoeh by name, took the fancy of your Akenehi
at the Grand Fancy Dress Carnival at the Glaci-
anum. They were got up as dairymaids, and
carried little milking-stools, and the men with them
had wisps of hay sticking out of their mouths and
in their ha ts. Otherwise they (the men) were garbed
as dairymaids’ associates, or rather aides-de-camp-
in-waiting to the Cow. The girls looked ice
2?, ai £ en ®> rem inded of some in Hans Andersen’s
bluffy Tales, and of Aurora Borealis and Norway
and tinkling sleigh bells across the snow. Doubt-
less the cows they milked were of the reindeer
persuasion. Another good bunch was the “Uncle
lorn s Cabin ” combination. Mrs. W. Baumann,
got up as Aunt Dinah, nigger-wigged, blacked,
and frocked just as H. Beecher Stowe described
her, was the limit. Her little daughter was
lopsy and -wheeled the Cabin. Mrs. Alec
Hay, Misjs Daisie Willis, and Miss Burdekin
ntted accurately the disguise of Dresden i
China. The Motor Team was about the
best-carried-out trick on the icy premises. The
teamsters were dressed correctly in wind-
proof frocks and veils, and the men had coats made
of woolly dog, or some such matter, and their
acetylenes streamed into the darkness a mile
ahead. (N.B. —This isn’t true, because there was
no darkness inside the Glaciarium.) Some girls
named Mann and Miss Holly Coffee were the
motorists jvho didn’t run over anything.
A capital idea for Hospital Saturday collectors
was supplied by four men. Don’t know
what they called themselves, but they were
as long as the Python when he stands
on his tail and puts a Baden Powell hat on
his head apd drapes a long black garment from
his neck to the ground. They skated splendidly,
and were able to look condescendingly into the
eyes of the gallery, and to thrust inquisitive heads
far over amongst the spectators outside the rail-
ings. Hospital collectors got up like them might
stand on tljie footway in Bent-street, and insert a
coin-seeking head into the upper storey windows
of the Unibn Club, where the selfish bachelor sits
reading a sporting paper. Also, in residential
neighborhoods they might pass the plate down
the chimney.
Consummate artiste as Miss Anglin is, the
continuousness of her stage lying is beginning
to pall. It is a curious fact that the three
plays in which she has faced Sydney all ex-
hibit her as an astounding liar/ There is a
play called “The Liars’’—probably because
the name was pre-empted, Clyde Fitch called
his bag of fabrications “ The Truth.” In
“The Truth,” which was put on at Her
Majesty’s on Saturday night, Miss Anglin
drops falsehoods all the way, but does it
so gracefully that one is doped sometimes into
thinking qf a rain of pearls, and forgetting the
underlying vulgarity of the character. The play
ends happily. Becky isn’t going to tell any more
falsehoods, and hubby takes her back ; but every-
one knows that the real liar—the instinctive liar
such as Becky—is never reformed. The drunkard,
and the person with the Blotted Past, and the
thief may be reformed, but never the chronic
departer from the truth. More than one of Syd-
ney’s haute noblesse ought to be doing, at time of
writing, three years’ light manual labor for lying.
“The Truth” is a well-frocked play. Miss
Alma Vaughan starts the Untruths in a Princess
gown of something that seems expensive. It is
prune in color, with two long, black tails of
Liberty satin, finished with tassels, falling from
the region of the heart almost to the
frock-tail. I The yoke, of clear tucked net and
insertion, is a marvel in the way of “fit.”
The only woman who speaks the truth in the play
is Miss Eileen Anglin (who is on the programme
as Miss Warren), and she scarcely speaks at all.
But she looks charming in black Liberty satin,
with bright pink roSes over her ribs (she’s mar-
vellously slender) and on her wide black hat.
When big sister Margaret Anglin comes on in a
grey-blue chiffon satin, with a wide high belt of
gold and Asiatic embroidery, and the three, the
Blue, the Black, and the Prune, with beautiful
furs, go up stage and down stage and pad about
like angry felines, one feels that sohemers
schemed to; some purpose in this color scheme.
Miss Anglin does “business” with a dainty
drawing-robm chair, upholstered in blue, same as
her frock. Its woodwork is gold, same as her
belt. Its build is doubtful, same as her assertions.
Another Anglin frock is of white silk muslin
oyer mauve, and tucked and laced. A mauve
girdle and a bunch of white and mauve lilac help
to make heij fibbing endurable. She expresses her
breaking heart and her intention of dropping the
lying habit, as if it were portable property, in a
brown crepe frock with a lot of Val. lace about the
sleeves and yoke. The expression is admirable,
but she contradicts it with a flamingo hat and a
bit of flamingo at her throat. Miss Susie Vaughan
as the widowed landlady, in shiny black and cheap
turquoise blue ribbon, would be an adorable per-
son to have with you on a wet day to make you
laugh. Her lies about her family and her French
education are merely humorous, and don’t suggest
moral dry rot.
Mrs. Ford, wife of the imported-from-America
comedian now playing in “ The Red Mill,” will
soon be given a chance to fling an airy fairy toe in
Sydney’s face. She turns out to be a noted dancer,
but didn’t give the fact away until she got bored
with the wet outlook and the patter of rain. Rain
has pattered since she and the man whom she is
pledged to obey reached these shores, and the
dancing of the raindrops in the mud puddles
keeps reminding her that her feet are elastic, and
that her tread doesn’t permanently crush the
slight harebell any more than did the boots
of Walter Scott’s Lady of the Lake. And she
is a peach in the matter of looks, with the
fine height, the blue eyes, and the infantile fair-
ness of hail 1 and complexion that most of the
American stage and other women who come here
possess. The U.S. is full of Gretchens and
Wilhelms, so, in many cases, perhaps there is
Teutonic ancestry to account for the flaxen top-
knot, the turquoise blue eyes, and the creamy
skins of the visitors. The baby-faced Vikingess
Nance O’Neil, Mrs, Kolker, Mrs. Ford, the
little Tivoli charmer Miss Kokin, are white and
gold and blue. Miss Anglin, Miss Ola Humphrey,
and the lady who was Miss Maud Jeffries are
mezzo in coloring. But not one is brunette.
Mr. Stanley Mackay, the progressive young
Muff whose name used to bob up whenever the
Muffs gave a dramatic performance, has cast off
the shackles of the bank in which for ten years he
held a stiddy job. His alternative job was fancy
and historical costume designing and hiring out,
and what was at first only a hobby developed
until, when at last he had to make a choice between
removal to a country branch of the bank (with
promotion) and his glorified old clo’ business, it
paid him to choose the clo’. He stage-managed
Mr. Arthur Adams’ ‘ ‘Blamed Cat,’’and did it quite
Herbert Flemmingly. Persons named Stanley
should have a lot of go about them to justify
Marmion’s last yelp on the battlefield of Flodderi.
Then he urged Stanley to shove ahead, and
advised Chester to charge, and I presume Chester
at once marked up all his prices and called it a
cheap sale.
A scream from a Sydney girl in Adelaide:
“Miss Beatrice Day wears a gown in ‘Simple
Simon’ that almost drew the eyes out of my head.
It is of rose-point lace with mother-of-pearl scales
and gold sequins. And with it she wears a ravish-
ing sable motor-coat and hat, and inuff of brown.
Polyphemus, the cyclops-eyed cat that is used
in ‘ The Morals of Marcus,’ has been distinguishing
himself. After his part in the play he walked
over the footlights and climbed to the topmost
box, from which bad eminence he watched the
rest of the performance out of his splendid one
eye.
The orange blossoms of the brides of this winter
won’t wilt through dryness. Miss Zelma Aronson
wore hers on a rainy day in last dripping week.
She has married Mr. Stuart Macphail, who is
something in the partner way in a big wholesale
warehouse. Her ivory Empire robe of satin
duchesse with Limerick lace swathed a pretty
bride. Her ma, Mrs. Fred Aronson, will now go
to live in Melbourne, minus the company of her
one ewe offspring.
Another wet-day bride was Miss Lindeman
(daughter of a retired R.N.), who married one
Anthony Ivo Pelham, a brother of the Earl of
Chichester. He earns bloater-paste for his crust
in a billet on some Asiatic railways.
Two hundred pounds and over were collected for
the Lady Northcote presentation. The classic,
gold loving-cup, or tear-jug, is being sown with
Australian gems, and will soon be ready for
the pretty speech with which the Lady will
receive The Empire Leaguers have already
given her Ex. their sweet, sad remembrancer. It
is a silver box with a big pearl in the middle, and
other Australian-found gems planted on the four
corners.
One of the nice girl visitors lured here by the
coming of the White Armada is Miss Victoria
Crowther, a daughter of Dr. E. L. Crowther,
M.H.A., of Tasmania. She is staying with Dr.
Jessie Aspinall’s ma at Rose Bay.
The military Hoad brace put in a sirocco of a
time in Sydney adieuing their friends, enemies,
and neutrals. Their gigantic second son goes
hence with them. Their only daughter died some
years ago ; she burst a blood vessel in the brain
whilst skipping.
Mrs. Ambrose Pratt is in Sydney on a visit to
her sister, Mrs. Harry Austin, and defying the
ungentle rain that falls alike on blondes and
brunettes. A some years’ residence in Melbourne,
where “the line of beauty is Bent,” hasn’t im-
paired the visitor’s gift of wearing brown with
pink corals, and brown and pink roses.
The Australia Hotel Uhr Smith family is going
to quit the big caravanserai with the two bronze
damsels at its entrance and take a house at Darling
Point. Pa Smith, the manager, isn’t quitt ng the
management, so he can’t Vie missed, but the going
of Mrs. will create a horrible vacuum. She was
the confidante of the couples that got engaged
there, and the joyful sympathiser with the
honeymoon pair, and the vigorous comforter of
the sick tourist, and the imported songstress or
other artiste who gets red and swollen eyes be-
cause she is so far from her beloved motherling.
Some of them do that weep even though the
beloved motherling died when they were born,
and they never saw her. In that case it is merely
an affecting pose.
Manager Major Ramaciotti, with Mrs., has
returned from Cairns, and reports that the
weather welcome he and the other Sydneysiders
encountered was warm. The rain was heavier
and even more persistent than that from which.,lie
fled. The Agricultural Show, various costumes,
and a lot of tempers were spoilt by the gentle slop
from Heaven. ;
Miss Ella Caspers, the young Australian con-
tralto, will give a matinee concert in the Town
Hall next Saturday. Excellency Rawson has
granted his patronage.
Still another of the remarkable Harnbourg
family has become famous. Besides Mark, who
shook his mane in a Chopin programme in Sydney
Town Hall on Friday, there were already two
brothers whom musical papers call famous—Jan,
the violinist, and Boris, who has conquered the
violin’s big brother, the ’cello. Now id is a sister,
Galia, and her fame comes through an extraordi-
nary divorce case. Reginald Grey Coke, a rackety
nephew of the Earl of Leicester, was married to
an Englishwoman. After one or two reconcilia-
tions the Earl’s nephew went to America,
drifted out to Nebraska for a few days,
and notified in an Omaha paper that if
his lawful wedded wife didn’t turn up in
24 hours or thereabouts he would get somebody
else to cook his chop. The Leicester family
doesn’t seem to take in the Omaha papers as a
regular thing, so the lady didn’t turn up ; and then
Nebraska promptly presented the husband with a
divorce decree. Whereupon the Leicester offshoot
sought out Miss Galia, and honored Mark Ham-
bourg by making him his brother-in-law, to the
enormous surprise of the wife he'd left behind
him—she didn’t know a word about it till she saw
her husband’s marriage notice in a London news-
paper. Then she went to a Divorce Judge, who
said stinging things about Nebraska, and declined
to recognise its divorce decrees, and treated the
Leicester offshoot’s second marriage as mere
bigamy. So whether Mark is really related to
the Earl of Leicester or not, I don’t know. He is
in Nebraska, but lie doesn’t seem to be in England.
But the affair threatens to be what lawyers call
a “ leading case,” anyway.
Which recalls that the Bishop of New Guinea
has been telling the other gaiters in England how
the natives manage these matters in his improve-
ment-lease corner of the vineyard. In Papua,
according to Stone-Wigg, if the gentleman nigger
won’t eat the missionary or other trifle which the
lady nigger has cooked for his supper, she accepts
it as a divorce, and leaves his wigwam. It seems
drastic. But still one can’t be too careful of one’s
indigestion.
Yours Affectionately,

The Lone Hand is going to be very
interesting to Australians during the next six
months, with its articles on the Asiatic menace.
See that you get each number.
It is asserted on unreliable authority that
a very ancient profession is making vast prepara-
tions in Melbourne for the entertainment of
Uncle Sam when lie happens along. Obscure
villas in obscurer neighborhoods are being fitted
upas branch establishments, and new and com-
modious premises have been engaged in the city.
That may be true or not; but it cannot be gain-
said that an extravagant amount of sham com-
plexion and open work stocking of the more
obvious sort is to be seen in one or two of Mel-
bourne’s seaside suburbs just now.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 10, 1908.
My Dear Yarrie,—
• ne ou Governor-Lady drives daily seeing the
sights. She does the Zoo and the Museum, and
perhaps the Waxworks. Her Ex. is kept warm
the while by magnificent sables that nearly incite
women worshippers to a revolution, and dowdy
T 1 *® Y? at rn , a ' :e tlle,n bill down and thank heaven
that their hats are not as other hats are. The
two nieces—fair, stodgy little girls—wear broad-
brimmed head garniture tied uncompromisingly
under their prim little chins. One is named
lenelope, so it seems probable that the other
is labitha though really I don’t know what’s
wrong with her being Deborah.
Lady Gibson-Carmichael, our new Governoress
is cosmopolitan or nothing. She was a Nugent of
the Irish family that has its head in Lord West-
meath. One of her uncles is a baron of the Aus-
trian Empire. Lord Southampton, an Ena-lish-
man of the Angles, is among her cousins, and her
grandfather was one Theodore Baltazzi, of Con-
stantinople.
Celia Ghiloni s husband provides another warn-
ing to young men who risk matrimony with dam-
sels who possess theatrical tendencies, together
with natural qualifications for the stage. Celia
was only 19 when Barnett Breslau, aged 22, took
her for better or for worse and congratulated him-
self on having secured a prize. His bonnie bride
was presently singing for Manager Rickards, and
three years later she joined the Williamson Opera
Co. 1 rom that time the domestic life of Breslau be-
came fitful and uncertain. The lady “followed her
career ” and the husband followed her from Mel-
bourne to Sydney, making his home there. But
her career took her so often from home in Sydney
that he returned to Melbourne with the idea of
getting her wifely companionship whenever she
happened in that city. The career, however, had
given her a distaste for domesticity and Breslau
In due course she wrote to him: “Apart from
our incompatibility of temper, my professional
demands preclude the idea of my living with you
again.” A divorce on the ground of desertion.
Curtain.
Everybody who was anybody drifted up to the
Exhibition last week “to see about a dorg.”
I he annual show of canines proclaims itself with-
out advertisement. If you own a prize poodle
you plunge valiantly through the bark and roar
and pull up ecstatically at that particqlar port.
I hen you discuss its points above the clamor to
any wayfarer who will listen. All are happy but
the dogs. At these flea-bitten functions the
bulldogs mostly maintain a gloomy, cvnical
silence, and the puckered foreheads and bored
expression of the Dachshunds, with their sausagy
toes turned out, is pathetic.
One stand at the Exhibition made an inter-
national flag—the Lion and the Lamb, also the
Monkey and the Bear, lying down together A
prize bulldog was beside a typical American bull
pup, which glowered at a miniature Japanese
spaniel boxed next to a cream-silk Siberian aris-
tocrat, and the crowd was flanked by German
Pomeranians matching their black fur against
two Chinese dogs—black as ink, foxy-furred
and blue-tongued. These Chinese dogs belong
to Miss Estelle de Beere, a Maorilander,
famous both as a dancer and a
fancier Miss E. de B. has shown some
of her dozen or so dogs all over the world, from
Christchurch to London. She teaches Maoriland
to dance when she is not whirling a lightsome toe
herself, or inciting a canine to walk on its hind
limbs. -Three sets of dogs belong to the North-
cote-Clarkes —the black Pomeranians, the ab-
surdly affected little Japanese prize-winner, and
the altogether adorable silky-woolly Si berian.' The
Siberian is a creamy white. She is the only one
of her km in Melbourne, and is like a big fox
with a generous forehead. This superior lady-
dog is used to trotting the young Northcote-
Clarkes about in a small red-harnessed buggy
and has concentrated devotion in her eyes. The
Carmichael-Governors visited the show on
Wednesday, the very youthful Carmichael niece
squealing with delight when the Clarke offspring
offered her a ride behind Woolly-Bear. The
Beebels put in three magnificent Great Danes
When I covet in real earnest, I covet a Great Dane
though there is something alluring and subtly
compelling about a baby bulldog—it is so crumpled
and fatuous, and it grins so affectionately and
idiotically at a fondling hand.
Judkins and all the Methody ministers collected
to lend pomp and dignity to the stone-laying of
new quarters at the Burwood Boys’ Training
Home—that up-to-date Farm instituted by Big
Edgar of the Central Mission. Chief Justice Mad
den had been booked for the trowel work, but wired
h' s Bendigo sessions wouldn’t let him
off m tune. (Yet, if he left off the practice of
holding forth at great length to the defendant
before passing sentence, he might save a whole lot
nil j 01 ? - ' t^le P res ident of the Methodists
filled the gap, and the others took turns at talking
on the masonry. Various donors of cheques ex-
pressed their fervor by laying a brick. Iceberg
Irvine, member for the district, and his brother
politicians, Mauger and Sampson, motored out
for the show, whilst “ Juddy,” who possesses a
farm near by,' had merely to climb over the fence
and come along. The new building, it seems, will
provide dormitories for 30 more boys ; also a huge
schoolroom. Rev. G. H. Cole manages the place
a practical farmer as well as a sky-pilot—
and m addition to the common or earthy farm-
work, a big industry is made of canning and
bottling fruit from the orchards. The Home
s kreet-boys sent by the Mission for practical
drilling and training, before they are placed as
handy lads ” under the care of farmers, who
teach them strict .economy and attention to busi-
ness, and bring them to a full knowledge of how
many pumpkins make five.
A ball rolled itself giddily round the C.L.C.
(Catholic Ladies.’ College) on Monday night at
Melbourne Town Hall. The Maddens blazed in
full frill, also the gaud Mayor and his untirable
Lady. Mrs. Senator St. Ledger and Mrs.
Eugenie Duggan Anderson humped things, as
Atlas did, on their fair shoulders, and altogether
there was more energy put into the show than
has whipped up for years for a dance. One must
be festive or freeze these cold nights. “Sets”
were the wild interest of the occasion. Mrs. St.
Ledger evolved a “Portia” set. Mrs. levers’’
girls, in Dresden China, looked as if they’d break.
There were Wattle sets, and Flatne-tree frisks,
and debutantes who formed their own white
never-been-kissed ring. Di Giiio played soulfully
for the whirly toe.
That pink-and-white-cheeked flower of youthful
law-expounders, Lieutenant Gerald Something-
hyphen Blackbourne, returns to Melbourne this
week with his new little bride, after a Sydney
honeymooning. The new Mrs. Blackbourne is a
daughter of solicitor Maxwell, of Ballarat.
A multi-millionaire’s son, Alexander K. Watson
(a cork of that old bottle, whisky-distiller Watson)
has been wandering round Australia and Maori-
land takingaplacdoverlookat thetriumphalmarch
of his own particular brand of cussed der-rink. The
Australasian head of the firm, who was acting, as
guide, found quite a hold-up in Dunedin
When eventually he dragged His Whiskyship
to Melbourne, that young man spent all his
time despatching cables to Pa-in-Scotland and
Ma-travelling-on-the-Continent. announcing his
discovery of “the dearest girl in the world.”
Now Pa, Ma and The Firm have cabled
blessings and congratulations, and an enamored
youth has bolted back to Dunedin to hustle the
banns. The young lady ,is a daughter of Dr.
McPherson, of Dunedin. All the lassies are
bonnie and sonsie and Scotch in that Edinboro’ of
the South.
Two Melbourne girls make readv for a free and
unchaperoned festivity on uncommon lines. They
intend to “do” Java, Borneo, and Burmah in
one comprehensive rampage. Justice Hood’s
pretty daughter Rate and her .chum, Mab Kelly
(the George Kellys’ hopeful), comprise the valiant
pair.
There almost happened a serious block in the
march of “The Merry Widow” on a recent
Saturday night. A friend of Carrie-the-Merry,
who owns a big German motor, took that young
lady for a spin to Geelong. On the way back,
somewhere near Werribee, the imported and un-
reliable box of tricks broke down. Carrie danced
with excitement, but the implement remained
dead and torpid. A bucolic person’s buggy was
plodding past, and she begged a lift. As she
settled into her seat she fervently thanked
Plodder, the farmer, and explained the importance
of being in Melbourne. “I’m Carrie Moore,’’she
volunteered. “ Down you get! ” sternly said the
heifer-chaser. I don t hold with play-acting.”
And behold an amazed Carrie was left standing
in the country with afternoon travelling
madly on to theatre time. The next sail* on
the horizon was a motor cycle. The undaunted
Carrie appealed to he-of-the-goggles, and climbed
up behind. She rode on the back of that cycle
till she nearly died with hanging on. Then a
poor, puffing, one-seated motor hove in sight, and
its owner gladly took the distracted little mum-
meress aboard, and puffed and wheezed with her
into Melbourne. She got to the theatre just in
time to change her dress—just when Higginson
was turning green and yellow, and Manager Tallis
was tearing his hair, and understudy Ivy Scott
was anxiously repeating her lines. Alas ! for the
understudy who thus finds the cup of opportunity
dashed from her lips.
The dames and spinsters of the Australian
Kennel Club entertained the Wiggses of the
Cabbage Patch at tea in the South Yarra Skating
Rink last Wednesday. There were no dogs, no
cabbages and no skating ; also there was no Patch.
Which shows how misleading are words in some
cases. But there was the cup that does not
inebriate, and harmless bun and much talk.
Mrs. Wiggs-Dwyer ran in for 10 minutes. She
was sprinting to see how many farewell teas it
was possible to do in one afternoon. I never
heard the result—the lady was too exhausted by
the plethora of Bun to disclose the figures, and
the unofficial time-keeper had dropped out half
way.
The other ladies of the company did not
attempt gymnastics ; they stayed right there with
the Kennel Club combination, and talked Yankee.
Miss Busby said, “ We’ve all bin so ill with colds
we really haven’t had time to get ’quainted,” and
now they all leave for Adelaide, and at Fleet
Time (dread sound) for Sydney. By the way
Miss Raymond is the most real.thing in self-
sacrifice I have ever seen. She is a beautiful girl-
beautiful .in feature, and figure, and coloring •
but as Miss Eichorn in “The Cabbage Patch,”
she distorts herself as thoroughly as the “comic
relief ”in a raging melodrama. Histrionic art has
much to answer for.
Prime Minister Deakin opened a private view
of still some more pictures on Saturday afternoon.
Hans Heysen. the young South Australian artist,
is the claimant for notice, and his claim is a shout.
His work is very strong. An occasional canvas
has been shown at the V.A.S., and one rather un-
characteristic painting is in the Gallery, but so far
Heysen has been little known. Most of the
works in his present collection have been painted
within the last two years—and yet there are about
150 shown.
Hans Heysen has caught the spirit of the Gum
Tree. There are gums jn .every stage of their
lives—isome dark and sombre and stately, others
ragged, with the curled slats of bark tearing and
flapping and streaking along the ground. There
are, too, the great giants with naked white bodies
.strong m the sunlight, Oneof these, “ A Lord of
the Bush, ” has been (or is to be) purchased by the
trustees of the Melbourne Gallery.
Another probable purchase for the Gallery is
“A Midsummer Morning”—an excellent atmo-
spheric effect in the half-awakened dawn. The
sheep have a grotesque look—but it is the
natural clumsy tumble of the baas over rough
ground as they are driven quickly from grazing.
The sense of movement is good, and the muttons
have the browny duskiness of Australian sheep.
Artists so often want to paint them snowy white,
as in the clean, neat fields of England. Our running
muttons are never white except for a few days
after shearing.
The show will be open until the 22nd August,
and already the well-beloved scarlet dot marks
the collection in growing clusters.
~ Mark Hambourg rose to inspired heights on
Saturday night. Maybe the special spurt was
inspired by a wonderful appreciation penned by
an expert Professor in one of the dailies. It was
certainly calculated to “buck up” any fitful artist
temperament. Chopin’s B flat minor sonata was
the meat of the programme, and its impressive
Marche Funebre was so ghostly as to make the
audience’s respective hairs stand on end.
throughout this number the house was hypno-
tised—not a sound and not a rustle. Women
forgot to look at the other Cat’s dress. There was
not even acough, which proves the hypnotic miracle
theory, for all through the rest of the concert
the cough—the irritating, pecking, barkingcough—
rattled round the echoing, cold, cavernous hall
likea'rifleshot. One man would bark, and straight-
way he would be answered in every key from
every corner. The hall was full of the bliie-and-
white school-girl miss. She came in flocks, with
her giggle and her rustle and her woollen
“fascinator” for her pig-tailed head. Mrs.
Hambourg was there again with her soulful gaze
and her big fur coat. Marshall-Hall had Schliiter,
the fair anaemic-looking Dane, along with him.
The unfortunate Brasseys have suffered exciting
times. A fire destroyed the whole of one wing of
their country house. Normanhurst Court, at
Battle (the place always marked with a sword on
English map's), in Sussex. The baronial Brasseys
had a big house-party on at the time, and all the
guests gave a hand to the pump. His lordship
himself danced about on the roof and dodged the
debris—without once falling off, although he is
now 73 and might have been expected to contri-
bute several sickening thuds to the catastrophe.
Evidently the old gentleman improves with age.
Much of his treasure collected on the voyages of
the Sunbeam (he fell over 300,000 knots in all,
I m told) was burnt, in spite of the heroism of the
fashionables who fought the flames.
“Mario” writes from Brisbane :
The opening of the Show festivities was heralded on
Saturday by mixed weather and the first influx of country
visitors. Despite the mixed weather, Mr. and Mrs
Kubelik wandered down from the Gresham to Flavelle’s’
and Mrs. K. admitted that “the beau-ti-ful black opal
quite won my heart.” Even the smileless Jan of the
tombstone countenance was moved to admiration by the
local gems, and the couple are reputed to be taking away
a sackful of them. In the afternoon the weather dried
itself with blotting paper to some extent, and the Jan lot
got as far as the gloomy Council Chamber, where his
podgy Worship and the plump Misses tendered them
Bananaland’s warm, if somewhat haphazard, welcome.
Meanwhile the Governor and his partner, accompanied
by their youngest hopeful, were spilling words of wisdom
at the British Picture Show,, amid a fashionable throng
and an artist or two. The pallid Excellency is always
right on the note, so to speak, and his suggestion that if
the guarantors (of whom he is one) get back their money
they should hand it over again to purchase some memento
for the very poorly-stocked gallery here, was a brilliant
idea from the public point of view. The temporary gal-
lery (it’s a pity that it is poked away in such a back corner)
looks very well now that it is dressed up, so to speak, and
one of the finest sightsin it is the Prince of Wales Empire
Cup, won twice by the local Grammar School boys.
Great was the splendor of the annual hop of the naval
and military forces, on Thursday evening, on the sloping
floor of the Exhibition Hall. There was not a hitch,
and the supper, served in a marquee which seated 300,’
was a high-class item. Lady Chelmsford, in grev silver-
spangled gauze ; Mrs. Military Commandant Lvster, in
heliotrope and gold embroideries ; and two charming-
ladies in precisely the same tint of blue and white stripes,
were features of the evening. So was the Naval Com-
mandant, just back from the North, and quite recovered
from the ’flu’.
The Challenger men and a heap of society folk frivolled
on board on Wednesday evening. The ship looked as
beautiful as a picture with its hundreds of lights reflected
in the waters of Garden Reach.
There was also a very swagger At Home at the John-
sonian Club on Friday evening, when 250 guests, includ-
ing Government House en masse, attended. The one
curious contretemps was the non-arrival of a certain
Society pianiste, who had undertaken to tinkle for the
benefit of the guests.
We had a citizens’ banquet the other night—l say “ we,”
because the women were invited to look on, and, later, to
sit up and take some light nourishment. Why the banquet
occurred, I forget, but it seemed full of bush aldermen.
They fed in the Exhibition Hall, about 800 bushies and
others, and put away the food on a generous scale. Then
there were speeches, and the elderly acting-Premier
Barlow got quite humorous.
A trouble from which we have long been free has come
upon us again. This is the lady who goes about demanding
to be invited where she isn’t wanted. Our present
sample is said to have been at four functions already
without an invitation. Some people are getting annoyed,
and are saying that a body may be found in the river
almost any day if the grievance continues.
“Bosna” writes from Adelaide:—
Ethel Hantke, who gave a farewell torrent of song in
the Town Hall t’other evening, with the intention of sail-
ing back to Britain, has suddenly changed her mind. She
is going to drop her profession with a thud, and take up
the steady job of matrimony (“ the holy bonds of pad-
lock,” as someone has aptly put it). The man to whom
Providence has been good is Mr. Billy Cowan, one of a
moneyed squattocratic family, which hangs out its private
shingle at Narracoorte.
’Tis rumored that Abe Shannon, B.C. (Bachelor of
Clothes), is engaged to be married, and that the lady who
will look after his wardrobe in future is the daughter of
another shekeled South Australian. The beauteous Abe
has recently been seen gazing at ties in Bond-street,
London, in her company. The rumor is probably an un-
true lie, however, as Adelaide’s chief frock-coater would
have no trunk-space to spare for feminine frills and
furbelows.
The Premier’s welcomes are still going strong. Thebar-
ton, another portion of St. Peter’s, has offered him the
official hand, and sundry institutions and societies have
added their quota of speeches. If Mr. Price hadn’t towed
a motor hohie, wherewith he can speed from one function
to another, his life would be spent in wild leapings from
a cab to a hall, and from a hall back to a cab. In the
middle of the month he and Mrs. Price speed over to
Sydney to shake hands with the U.S. Admiral, and the
bos’n, and the ship’s cook.
The School of Mines boys had a very large attack of
energy before their ball in the Brookman Hall, the other
evening—a joyous episode at which some hundreds of
jolly youngsters disported themselves. The hosts took
the whole business on their own shoulders, and not a
sister or a cousin or an aunt was permitted to even put a
finger on the decorations. Among them the boys manufac-
tured some 20,000 or so pink paper blooms, and with these
and long ropes of asparagus fern they festooned the whole
landscape until that landscape’s own parent wouldn’t
have known it. The school colors, blue and gold, were
draped round the walls; and a few thousand more dainty
boy-manufactured blooms and a ton or so of palms made
a dreamland of the stage. Langdon Bonython and some
of his staff and its wife, and some of the Council and its
very best half, lent an air of dignity to the proceedings.
Also the Premier and his wife came in on the heel of one
of their many welcomes, and stayed to the end. They
had a young daughter among the revellers. Mrs. Price’s
raiment was a beauteous black silk with soft lace tucker
and scarf; the daughter was a pictureof simplicity in-a
white muslin and lace confection.
Same ballroom and decorations were used on Fridav for
the conversazione and dance in honor of the visiting
Varsity hockey girls. It’s the first time any sweet iriri
under-graduates have come from other States to seek for
laurels on this side, and the local damsels were on their
mettle to give them a good time. Next dav the home
team carried the visitors on a picnic jaunt'to the hills
and on Sunday they all tea’d and bunned at Montefiore
with the Chancellor Ways. "tenore

Bad Results of
Tram Accident.
The Case of Mrs. Mackie.
Nothing but good can result in giving to the
public some hint as to the various incidents in
which Mrs. Kate Mackie, of No. 2 Stanlev-street,
Woolloomooloo, once played a prominent part.
In beginning her story, Mrs. Mackie said
“The ups and downs of life experienced by me
since arriving from Kilkenny, fifty years ago,
would make interesting reading, and I must say
that, taken altogether, I have a great deal to be
thankful for.”
“You have had your share of bad luck, as well
as good luck, like most people,” suggested the
pressman, who had called on a mission of enquiry.
“ Indeed, I have, and I consider the worst mis-
fortune that befel me was in connection with an
accident that happened two years ago, when I
was unfortunately the central figure. I intended
visiting Lavender Bay, and when I got as far as
Circular Quay in the tram, I was in the act of
alighting, when the car started off, and I was
thrown on to my face and hands on to the road.
A notable bump, such as 1 got, does not agree
with a woman well up in years, and my system
suffered severely from the terrible shock I received.
I was thrown out of gear altogether, and it looked
for some time as though the consequences were
going to be very serious, for I might have died.
My nerves were fairly broken up. To give you an
idea of the fright I received, I may tell you that I
have not been in a tram since. I have a positive
dread of them. I believe if I boarded one of them
now I would take a fit, my aversion is so strong.”
“ Perhaps you are still suffering from nervous-
n6 “ S Oh, don’t you believe it; for I was not long
in getting something that attended to my troubles
in respect to nerves, and I chanced to read of it
Clements Tonic—in the papers. The advertise-
ment I read appeared before me one day, like a
beacon acting as a guide to a mariner in distress.
The case fitted in with my own in some respects,
and something whispered to me that Clements
Tonic was what I required to put my system to-
gether again. And, believe me, sir, I was fear-
fully weak and ill, not a vestige of strength
remaining in my body. It must have been dread-
ful for other people to see me shaking like I did,
and I could.not have written with a pen for any-
thing. But how wretched I was at night, not a
wink of sleep ever coming to my relief, while the
thoughts that ran through my brain were some-
thing horrible. The same round of things went
on from day to day—no appetite for breakfast,
dinner, or tea ; a head that felt like bursting with
aches from daylight till dark, and then on through
the night; when I stood up, a giddy feeling that
made things appear to be turning around in the
room ; terrible pains in the chest, and no life or
energy to help me withstand it —no wonder I felt
that I would rather be dead than alive.”
“Yes ; there is not much to live for when one’s
trials are so great.”
“And another thing I suffered from for years
was palpitation of the heart, and had tried nearly
every known medicine in the hope of getting
relief, but no—not till I bought some Clements
Tonic, and then I certainly did get something for
my money. And, oh ! what a joyful surprise it
was when I at length realised that my nervous
affections were commencing to subside, for the
possibility that threatened of being a confirmed
invalid for life became more remote as each bottle
of Clements Tonic was finished. The constant
twitching of the nerves at last gave way to a
steadiness that had hitherto been unknown to me,
and in every respect (in regard to my digestion,
especially) I was reaping benefit each day. 1
thanked God for my good fortune in coming into
contact with Clements Tonic, as it was undoubt-
edly the one remedy that saved me. My illness
had pulled me down a lot, but through sleeping
so soundly, and eating so well, under the influence
of my latest medicine, I picked up wonderfully,
and was soon as right as could be.”
“This can be published, Mrs. Mackie?”
“Yes; in any form your mind suggests.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
While he was up North Minister for Customs
Chapman saw the start of another Australian industry.
At Childers he was introduced to the Commonwealth
pioneer plant for treating sisal hemp- ft is the property of
T. H. Wells, of Farnborough plantation. Wells, in conse-
quence of the bonus (riven by the Australian Government,
has started hemp cultivation on an extensive scale. The
sisal plant is much the same as the ordinary or Chinese
garden variety of banana ; and the Kamerunga State
nursery has alreadv proved that it thrives in Queens-
land.
* * * *
“ Mallarapoonie ”: This scribe hasn’t been
much outside Australia, and for that reason often
Wonders whether J. Hull leaves things—old iron
especially—lying round in other parts of the globe as he
does here. If so, the Chow and the nigger and the other
Occasional Person must also wonder what manner of
curious casual being Bull is. When the Australian aban-
dons a mine he mostly leaves only a heap of mullock, the
hole the mullock came out of, a bough shed, some char-
coal, and the stump of a tree that his anvil stood
on. But not so Bull. Starting from the old
Blinman mine, in S.A., there is a trail of old
iron, abandoned mines, defunct smelter sites, and
scrap heaps generally right round to as far north as
Mount Garnet in Queensland. After that there is the old
iron of Einasleigh (now being renewed), and the holes
and the old iron strewn across the Etheridge to Croydon.
Then come the old smelting works way back in Olon-
curr.v. Further out, in the Northern Territory, Bull has
laid up for himself treasures of old iron on earth, but,
alas, the rust and the white ant corrupt and the
nigger bruiseth up Bull’s iron into spear-heads. And so on
to the West is the same old trail of rust, the same old tale
of white ants, even to the confines of human habitation,
where the skeletons of Bull’s old batteries bleach on the
edge of Westralian deserts. Some day Bull will drag a
lot of old iron out on to Sturt’s Stony Desert and leave it
there, and then he’ll have the world’s record for pro-
miscuousness in old iron.
* * * *
Maoriland has had another illustration of how
“Private Enterprise can always do the thing better.”
Seven years ago dining cars were put on the express
trains between Christchurch arid Dunedin. There was
such a howl when J. Ward proposed that the Government
should do the catering that the right to feed the hungry
public was let by tender, and for five years good old
Private Enterprise served up a menu that never varied.
The same old leather masqueraded as steak, and the same
cold joints were hurled at the defenceless traveller. The
stops had been cut down to bedrock, the longest
being eight minutes, and the refreshment-rooms along
the line soon went out of business ; so the traveller had
to take what Private Enterprise offered him or starve.
One eventful day, J. Ward had occasion to use a
car, and results followed. When the lessee’s term
expired, Private Enterprise (which hadn’t shown any
enterprise worth a cent) was passed into outer
darkness, and the Government took a hand. It
appointed its own cooks, waiters and managers.
Most important of all, it began to consider the public.
Now, on the through daily expresses between Christ-
church and the Bluff, passengers can get a good break-
fast, a four-course dinner, and a warm tea. The meals
are well cooked, the menu is lengthy and varied, the atten-
tion all that can be desired, and the car and tables are clean
and attractive ; and yet the price is the same as it was
when Private Enterprise was having its fling with the
adamantine beefsteak and the dried and haggard mustard
pot.
A co. is getting to work on the deposits of
guano which exist in Olsen’s and Johansen’s caves, 1,(5
miles from Rockhampton (Q.). The guano,, so far as it
has been probed, averages 10 per cent, phosphoric acid,,
I’s per cent, nitrogen, and I's per cent, potash. The co..
talks in big figures—reckons that the manure heap may
be worth anything up to £2,000,000 ; and a plant is to be
erected on the Fitzroy River to prepare the stuff for ship-
ment. The managing director is journalist Chaplin, who
was for a brief spell Director of Agriculture in Westralia.

J. J. O’Brien, of Capella, Clermont, is the first
landowner in that section of Central Queensland to prove
his faith in the cotton industry. He has commenced to
plant an area of 30 acres. The farm is to be managed by
Rowan, an American, who has proved his ability to pick
cotton in Australia profitably by gathering at the rate of
2901 b. per day of ten hours, which at id. per lb. means
Is. 2£d. per hour. In America, during the flush of a busy
season, he has picked up to 5001 b. per day. With the
better wage conditions in Australia he would thus easily
earn over 20s. per da3 T . Yet cotton growing is the busi-
ness above all others which the Tory, who loves any labor
but white, was quite sure demanded something cheap and
nasty and colored.
* * * *
“ Stony ” : America’s mail-order firms, referred
to in B. 23/4’/08, provide Australia with a bunch of stun-
ning object-lessons in the distribution of commodities.
Why, asks Emily, should one pay Hobson, Choice and
Co.'ss. lid. for one’s gloves when they can be got at
Fluffem’s for ss. 6d. ? And why should Emily waste time
between shops when she can buy gloves at the Government
stores, all the year round, for a fixed price, 3s. 9d. ? We
haven’t any Government stores ? No, but we could do
worse than start them. The big American mail-order
houses (which, in our case, might be State stores) succeed
in business by supplying goods below shop-window
prices. They have no shop-windows, and no shop-
walkers, and waste no time in being polite, except
in cold type or typewriting. They sell their goods
as cheaply as possible, and keep their customers ; and
their motto is “ One price only, and one price all the year
round if possible.” Writer has had mail-order dealings,
while in the U.S.A., with Sears, of Chicago, and found
the transactions satisfactory all through. If .your gloves
split when you pub them on, or don’t fit, or are the wrong
color, you send them back, and the firm pays the carriage
both ways and refunds your cash. It then probably gets
vour custom when you want a sewing machine. Would
Hobson, Choice and Co., with no State stores to keep
them in check, be as kind as this? If the State were in
the game on these lines, and ignored the order for a hun-
dred dozen of the gloves at Is. 9d. (which might be
Fluffern’s order), and dealt at first in simple, standardised
necessities like spuds and towels and tinned meats and
socks and so forth, it would be doing something for the
mass of middle-class toilers who have bought Hobson his
big house at Malvern, and Choice his bunch of broad acres
in the Western District, and taken most of Fluffern’s ances-
tral goldware out of pawn. ‘ Those who wished to go and
be robbed by the private stores could still do so.
* * * *
“Silica”: “Kodak’s” uncertainty, when he
went to court (B. 2/7/’08), as to whether Bop Yang
isn’t Hip Yah, and his mystification as to the identity of
Ling Hap and Lue Tong, who might have been the same
Chow if they weren’t Ah Yin, reminds me of an old Queens-
land bailiff. When he has to serve a summons on Con-
fucius, he goes to the nearest Chow warren and serves it
on the first Chow he meets. This may be Confucius, but
mostly it isn’t. Then the bailiff goes contentedly home,
counts up his mileage, and confidently awaits develop-
ments. It is not given to foreign devils to understand
the workings of the Asiatic mind in the presence of a
writ, or to trace the devious path of the blue bit of paper
until it reaches Confucius, or Hip Yen, or Ah Lue, or
whoever Confucius may be when he owes money. Any-
way, on the day duly appointed Confucius, or his alias,
or his nom-de-guerre, turns up to satisfy the claim, and
the . majesty of the law is again vindicated. And the
bailiff gets an enthusiastic mileage.
* « * *
“ Bundaberg”: Re the sugar octopus (B. 9/7/’OS)
and the remark about that reptile becoming so bold as to
be almost truculent. It is quite evident that some people
south do not regard the O. S. R. Co. as a monopoly. I
do. It is common talk up here that the shareholders of
it and of the bank that runs “ the small local concern ”
alluded to in your article are very good cousins, and are
working in fuli agreement. The local place is capable of
turning out 40,000 to 45,000 tons of refined sugar a year,
but is limited by agreement in order to keep up the
price of refined sugar; consequently it turns out
under 20,000 tons a year. The working people here
(Bundaberg) are the greatest sufferers, as they have
only about seven months’ work in the year at
irregular intervals. For the good of the sugar industry
the nationalisation of the refining business is about the
simplest plan ; the expansion of the sugar industry could
then follow legitimately. Under present conditions Aus-
tralian refined sugar is dumped on foreign markets, some-
times even at a slight loss, to keep up prices here to an
artificial level. So the consuming industries (jam-making,
confectionery, etc.) are crippled by the high price of
refined sugar —and all to maintain a regular 15 per cent,
dividend on the C. S. R. Co.’s capita), which keeps ex-
panding like a balloon.
THE CAREFUL MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a burly man of means,
With anger in his eye
He stood, his pockets full of beans,
And watched the trams go by.
He was a man of means, with stacks
Of oof and splosh and gilt;
From good, selected greys and blacks
His well-cut clothes were built.
In wrath he watched the trams go through
To Carlton and Prahran ;
He stood alone, an angry, blue,
Soured, solitary man.
And still the trams went lounging past
With room for more than he,
And still he stared up-street, and fast
The dark came, stealthily.
At last, with rage that scored his brow,
He blessed the whole earth: “Damn!”
He said, “ I’ve waited ages now
For that cheap workman’s tram ! ”
VIC.

BAD BREATH.
The Tongue is the Index.
Your breath is foul, but you do not perceive it
yourself. You are like a person who is always
among the oils and varnishes—you get used to
what is to many people a most obnoxious smell.
It is most disagreeable to those with whom you
come in contract. A slight headache, a bad
breath, and a coated tongue are the first symptoms
of stomachic disease. Take time by the forelock,
and intercept germination ; arrest the germs ere
they develop into disease. A pill in time may
save days of suffering. Look at your tongue m
the looking glass every night and morning, and if
it is at all discolored take a couple of Dr. Morse s
Indian Root Pills. They will remove the objection-
able matter from the stomach, regulate the system,
and sweeten the breath. One or two of these pills,
taken once or twice a week, will ensure a well-
regulated system, fortified against all disease.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a searching
remedy, and are a permanent and positive remedy
for biliousness, indigestion, constipation, bad
breath, headaches, sallow complexion, liver and
kidney troubles, piles, pimples and blotches, and
for all female ailments.
