The Marylebone Club having duly eaten the
leek, the Australian Board of Control has accepted its-in-
vitation to send a team to England next season. Which
seems almost a pity. What is sauce for Marylebone is
dressing for the Board of Control, and it is a thousand
pities that the Board did not demand a guarantee of
£lO,OOO, as England did on its last visit. However, it is
too late to discuss that now, and there are g'ood reasons
why it should not have done so. Next time it may be in
a position to drop that little thunderbolt on Marylebone’s
toes. But one tiling should be discussed right away, and
that is the abolition of the ridiculous system under which
the players go home to pocket all the profits, secure in
the knowledge that any deficit will have to be made good
by the Board of Control. The next team should go home
under terms by which each man will receive a stated sum
for loss of time, and all profits above actual costs and
expense should go to the Board of Control.
The only people who really benefited by the visit
of the last XI. to Australia were the Marylebone Club,
the grounds trustees and the players. The total takings,
excluding matches against Tasmania and Queensland,
which resulted in dead loss, anyhow, were £19,496 18s. 2d.
The Marylebone Club grabbed £lO,OOO, Melbourne Ground
got away with £1283 11s. 6d., Sydney Cricket Ground
mopped up £1526 9s. 6d., other grounds £297 3s. lOd.—a
total of £3107 4s. lOd. The players made away with £2387
2s. 5d., and in the end all the Board had to divide among
the various associations was £2244 7s. 6d., or £142145. lid.
less than the blessed players received. • Which hardly
seems a fair deal.
Several things require reforming very badly.
To begin with, the ground charges in the case of Sydney
and Melbourne are downright extortion, for the £3107
does not represent all they got out of the deal. Take the
case of Sydney ground : In consequence of the visit of the
English XI. the membership jumped from 2608 in 1907, to
2970 in ’OB, an increase of 362 members. Each of these
paid £3 3s. entrance-fee, and £2 2s. annual fee, which
meant £l9OO 10s. revenue for that year alone, and a
regular income of £760 per annum each year afterwards.
In addition to this, the ground members got all their fun
for nothing, and the Board of Control never got a six-
pence for the 2970 members who, no doubt, loaned their
tickets to members of their families when they didn’t go
themselves.
Considering that, with the exception of the five
Tests, the Australians were playing in their own cities,
and living at home the while, £2387 seems an enormous
sum for amateurs to draw for loss of time. If it were cut
to one half it would still be too large. If the Board had
its own grounds it could afford to pay players these fancy
prices ; but it hasn’t, and the players of the future will
have to be content with a lot less than £25 per match
and expenses. If it got the profits from a few good
English tours, the Board might manage to get together a
fund out of which, in time, it could construct its own
grounds, and bring the hungry trustees of Sydney and
Melbourne Cricket and Metropolitan grounds to accept a
reasonable price for Grade and International and Inter-
state matches.
On Wednesday last week a person named Bill
arrived in Sydney, and was welcomed home at Bateman’s
Hotel by a huge and enthusiastic crowd, which drank his
health with a three times three ; while the O’Sullivan,
the Eoley, and other great men said charming things
about him, and Colonel Ryrie swore he was the sort of
man we wanted to defend the hearths and homes.
Squires, though hard and rugged, did not look as though
he possessed the abounding health that makes the
perfect boxer. He was quiet, and quite unexcited
by the wild applause and the vociferous welcome,
and contented himself by saying that since he had gone
abroad he had learned many things. He hoped the better
man would win on the 24th, and lie devoutly hoped to be
the better man. All that he could do to make himself so
would be done, and he was confident that if he did not
win he would give Burns the fight of his life. He had
never enjoyed g'ood health while abroad, but now, hack in
his native climate, he was feeling fit to put up the scrim-
mage of his existence.
On Tuesday last Tommy Burns arrived to find
an enthusiastic crowd, including Squires, awaiting him
at the same place. That well-known sporting scribe,
VV. F. Corbett, made an interesting speech, in which he
demonstrated that Burns was the first man for many
years who could legitimately claim the title of Champion
of the World. He had beaten the champions of
England, Ireland, and Australia, and was, by deed
and laurels won, a champion of champions. In
appearance Burns is a heavy-weight replica of
Napoleon in face and figure. The likeness is
startling. His width is tremendous, especially in pro-
portion to his height, for he is a short man. His hitting
power must also be tremendous, for both legs and arms
show great muscular development, and taper beautifully,
his hands being unusually small. Sartorially, he was not
beautiful on the day of his arrival, wearing a fawn over-
coat with a velvet collar of the chameleon-like tint that
usually accompanies fawn. Horrible to relate, he wore a
little round hat of green plush, and the effect was
grotesque. He spoke in a low, decisive manner, and very
much to the point. And while the speakers followed one
another and assured the sardine-like crowd they were
real glad and very happy to be there that day, he looked
somewhat bored. Nobody could be really sad at being
there that day, for the Mclntosh had laid on the best
brand of champagne, and unlimited quantities of it.
Having gazed upon Burns in the flesh and
regarded Squires in the bone, writer does not see how
Squires can win. Burns’ hitting power must be tre-
mendous, and to be struck by that fist is to collide with
the sharp point of a wedge. And, provided the body
behind the arm and fist that make the wedge is in con-
dition, a long good-night to Boshter Bill.

“Silas”: Tommy Burns plumes himself on the
amount of boxing he has done during the last 12
months, but Tommy’s record is far behind that of Bill
Lang, who just about reached the possible with 11
battles in 11 months. Lang, once a Broken Hill miner,
came to Melbourne, about three years ago, with a little
knowledge of the game and heaps of grit. He came to
make his fortune, and, being of the type of boxer that
doesn’t squander the beans monkeying with strong
drink and other weaknesses, he is in a fair way to
arrive. He is now something of a capitalist, atl< ‘ , ls
partner in a profitable biz. Squires, Cripps, Ed. Wil-
liams, Frank Thorn, and two or three others demon-
strate that the game pays with judicious economy and
careful avoidance of liquor and lures, even if you are
not a 30,000d01. champion.

Judging by its first appearances in Sydney, the
'Anglo-Welsh Rugby team has acquired all tfie objection-
able points of Maoriland play and added them to its own ;
and its own do not seem to be conspicuous by rarity.
The one redeeming feature of the team’s display was the
line kicking. Its accent is classic English, and beautiful
to hear, and the richness thereof attracts attention to
the spectacle of certain young men in evening dress,
without hats or caps, chasing Florence the Smiler down
the dark recesses of Phillip-street. One thing that can be
said in favor of the rackety section of the team is that
it’s not hypocritical. It doesn’t care a-hang who sees it
chasin'? Flossie down the street. The absence of head-
gear at nights suggests that some of the team were
originally Blueeoat boys, who go naked on the head at
all times.
In both Wednesday and Saturday matches the
Waters were up against the fact that they were
meeting a team that had played together for a consider-
able time, and which the hard boot of the Maorilander
had kicked into one solid lump, so to speak. It was a
machine, and apart from its pointing and trickery, which
usually got it penalised more than anything else, it was
far and away the better team. On Wednesday the ground
was filthy ; on Saturday it was obscene. In a quagmire
of slush the teams struggled, and although on Wednesday
the British team won b.v only three to nil, it had all
the best of the mud-larking. On Saturday the awful
ground was impossible to the Children of the Sun,
and though, of course, the Sons of the Fog were-more at
home in it, they did not find it to their liking. The line
kicking of the British, under the circumstances, was
superb, mystic, wonderful. The N. S. Wales team played
gamely, but in football as in war, pluck without skill is
a useless thing. Any fool can be game ; and a coward
who knotvs the businessis often better than thegamestman
who ever lived. The Cornstalk team had no combina-
tion and mighty little generalship. The other crowd had
both, and the figures were eight to nil. The First Aus-
tralian Rugby Team left Sydney for Britain ori Saturday
last.

David Leslie’s description of a good Covenanter
was to the effect that he was one could
sit out on a wet hillside for four hours listening
to a sermon on predestination. Something of the
sort defines vour real dyed-in-the-wool football en-
thusiast., and'when 25,000 of these dour beings were
gathered together at Sydney Cricket Ground to watch
the Anglo-Welsh team roll the N.S.W. men in the bog,
there were not many left for the League’s various dis-
plays. The Agricultural Ground, the League’s battle-
field, was in just as horrible a state as the Cricket
Ground, and had not nearly so much shelter. So
the resumption of the League’s first-grade matches was
a monetary frost. Eastern Suburbs, minus Messenger,
fell on the struggling men from Western Suburbs and
smothered them. It was a swim-away match, East win-
ning by 24 points to two. And what a mud frolic it was .
Qne pool on the ground was fully 18in. deep, but in spite
of the horrible conditions the Eastern backs got off
several passing charges that crumpled the Western men
right up, and left what spectators there were filled with
amazement. At, Wentworth Park, South Sydney and
Newcastle got swamped in turn. The Southern forwards
were a tough crowd, and, with their eyes glued on the
ball, swam and splashed for it every time. It was the
fixed resolve to be always on the ball that decided
the day; but there was not so much difference
between the teams, South winning only by eight
points to three. Two matches were played at
Birchgrove, in the earlier of wdiich North Sydney drowned
Newtown by 21 points to nil; and, in the second, Balmain
downed Glebe by ten points to five.
Eastern Suburbs and South Sydney are still in
the front rank with 18 points each. South Sydney is in
second place with 16 ; North Sydney, 14 ; and Newcastle,
10. Below that is the limbo of single figures inhabited
by Balmain, Newtown, Cumberland, and Western
Suburbs.
« * * *
It doesn’t look as though doubt and uncertainty
would be eliminated from the Vic. League position till
the last game of the round is concluded on September 5.
As thing's stand, the winner of the South v. ’Wood game
on that date is a certainty for fourth place in the semi-
finals, provided neither loses the intervening game next
Saturday ; but if one of them does, and either Melbourne
or ’Varsity can win both its games, the question will
have to be decided on averages—points scored for and
against through the season. Also, if St. Hilda isass
enough to lose both its games it may likewise be outed by
any one of the four clubs mentioned. The probable outcome
of the uproar will be that Carlton and Essendon will battle
out the final game ; but a still more likely happening
is that some “unexpected reversals” will supervene,
and the man who bets on a certainty will fall in the mud
as usual, the other fellow remarking, also as usual, I
told you so ; I knew when the season started they’d be
in the final.” At present, the pubs are full of useless dis-
cussion. On Saturday, Melbourne collided with Essendon,
but the lopsided and switchbacked team from the M.C.C.
w'as on one of its descending grades, and its performance
was painful and saddening to a degree. The ’Dons made
119 points to 59. Richmond—which had just beaten
’Wood, after going under to Geelong—encountered
South. “Skinner’s Hirelings” recollected that their
final chance might come to a matter of points, so they
piled them on regardless of Richmond’s lacerated feel-
ings, 120 to 28 being on the board when the bell closed
the disaster. ’Varsity, having successively walloped
Saints, South and Melbourne, proceeded to Fitzroy full
of hope and cocksureness. Playing steadily and con-
certedly, the students forged ahead, till at half-time
they had 10 goals 7 behinds to credit against
’Roy’s 6 goals 5 behinds; but after the inter-
val' they acted as if they had been doped or
niozzed or something ; likewise their skill vanished and
their science evaporated—even as Willie Kelly evaporates
when breathed on by Bill Lyne—while the hopeless but
determined Maroons bogged in ever more and more
strongly. At three-quarter time, the ’Varsity tally was
11 goals 7 behinds ; ’Roy’s, 8 goals 10 behinds. A strenu-
ous rush or two in the last quarter would have saved them
the game ; even a strong defence might have availed;
but paralysis seized the Students and they plunged
around to' no earthly purpose while ’Roy added 5 goals
6 behinds. The Students left the field with 73 points to
’Roy’s 94 ; and behind them, to all practical purposes, lay
their semi-final hopes in this their first League season.
The already-outed Geelong club spelled six of its best
men in the game against Carlton, and the youngsters
who replaced them put up a fight that, aided by Carl-
ton’s perverted- kicking, left them, only 10 points in
default—39 to 49. There is a large-sized moral
herein for Geelong’s committee, if it will but digest it.
The heftiest engagement of the day was fought at Col-
lingwood, where the Magpies, knowing that they had to
beat Saints, put up an exhibition of determined play that
compelled a victory, though they lost two good men in
the process. Up to half-time the organised, concerted
play of the middle-weights held its own comfortably
against the strenuous rushing of the hefty holy men ;
after that the latter took advantage of a weak umpire,
disregarded the rule as to holding the ball, and charged
their opponents deliberately. Naturally, the latter
followed suit, and an uproarious and unmannerly finish
ensued, with players being rushed and slung regardless.
Still the concerted play triumphed,’Wood finishing with
70 points to St. Hilda’s 51.

The chief Association struggle was between
Brunswick and Brighton, at Brunswick, whither Bent
was inveigled to bellow for his suburb and have borak
poked at him when it lost the day. The brickies knew
that defeat meant the loss of their semi-final chances, so
they played steadily, without haste and without waste,
while Bent’s constituents toiled for all they were
worth, with the knowledge that if they could beat their
opponents and Prahran next week, they might even yet
bring more glory to the suburb than Thomas himself.
But, like the rage of the heathen, their bogging-in availed
them nought. Brunswick won bv 61 points to 48. That
team has but to beat the fearsome Ports next Saturday
to reach the semi-final or the cemetery. Ports
would as soon send it to one destination as the other.
Prahran, which still.has a chance of joining the elect
four, beat Preston by 78 to 50. Footscra.v thrashed the
subdued and discredited Ports by 77 to 44; ’Town beat
Northcote by 88 to 62, and Essendon put up a neat little
124 points to North’s 44.
During the week the Vic. Association considered
the episode of the previous Saturday, when the Port-
Brighton collision became a battle, and a Port player
peeled off his jersey and incited the whole Brighton team
to come on and be stoushed, and when the umpire was
laid out with a woodener on the jaw. Having pondered
the matter diligently the Ass. opined that certain irregu-
larities had transpired and that the suspension of three
Port players—two till Oct., ’O9, and one till June, ’lO
—would express its disapproval. Also it censured
a Port delegate for having, at a previous match,
denounced another umpire and sooled the players on
to him. The Port delegates were much aggrieved
at these decisions, and remarked that the club
mio-ht as well be out of the Association, and this
paper is inclined to agree with them. Remembering
their last season’s capers, when their ground had to be
disqualified because it was unsafe for a civilised team to
play there and recollecting the fact that many times
extra police have had to be laid on to curb the exuber-
ance of the Port barrackers, and keeping in mind the
disgraceful conduct of these latest Port ruffians and the
charming courtesy of the above-mentioned delegates, this
paper can only express its surprise that the Asso- <
ciation didn’t fire the whole lot of Ports out there
and then; and if there were any way of
wiping the club out of existence, the earth would be
the sweeter and tidier for its departure. As for the
pluckv individual who smote the umpire from behind the
door when he wasn’t looking, the Ass., finding that he
was merely a Port barracker who had inserted himself
where he had no business, instructed its officers to have
him pros'ecuted as speedily as might be. Just here, this
paper would point out to Port’s committee that, if its
professions of sportsmanship were genuine, or if it had
any self-respect at all, it would have served a summons
on this precious supporter before the Ass. got a chance to
interfere. Its failure to do so is another reason why it
should be bundled out of decent people’s society. '

“Maori Mac’’ : Rugby, as played in parts of
Maoriland, is a fearsome game—for the players. For the
spectators it is Thrill —joyous Thrill. To see a crowd of
hardened barrackers speculating as to whether an injured
combatant has received a fractured skull or merely had
an ear torn off, what time the doctor, who invariably
lights a cigarette, ambles placidly over the field, is not a
sight for the gods. The Melrose team has won the Wel-
lington championship this season per medium of seven
good boosters and a “ kiyer out.”. The latter is a star
artist in his line. The crowd looks to him to supply the
Thrill, and he does it. His duties are comparatively
simple. Following close up to the charging forwards, he
springs on an opponent who -has just lost the ball. His
favorite tackle is left arm round neck, right hand across
face. When the opponent is on the ground a well-pro-
pelled boot does the rest. The referee, with one eye on
the ball, and t’other on the seven charging forwards, fails
to observe the “layer-out.” The Melrose team has had
two men put off the field in a fortnight, but the “ layer-
out ” was not one of them. This individual cut short the
career of Thompson, Maoriland’s crack three-quarter, a
few weeks since, and now McLeod, Wellington’s rep. five-
eighoh, has fallen a victim. The latter’s “ accident ” was a
gouge, pure and simple, and he was lucky not to lose an
eye. Harding, captain of the Anglo-Welshmen, after
witnessing Melrose defeat Oriental, inquired what the
Rugby death-rate was in Wellington. But the crowd
gets its Thrill, and the Union the sixpences. And for the
players—-there’s the ambulance.

Giltinan, of the League, showed conclusively
that he had none of the fine old crusted port qualities of

The most interesting feature of the recent fifth
annual motor regatta of the International Sporting Club
at Monaco was the show of hydroplanes. It did not in-
crease the general knowledge of hydroplanes very much,
but it demonstrated again that in proportion to engine-
power the fastest speed is attained with the hydroplane
hull. The fastest hydro at the meeting was the French
Rapiere 111., a 26ft. 100-h.p. Tellier boat, which made 28’I
nautical miles per hour. Greater speeds have been
attained by smaller powered boats, but they have all
been accomplished on the smooth water of canals and
lakes, whereas the racing at Monaco was in the open bay
with a jobble and quite a fresh breeze. The spectacle
was cheerful, the boats fairly jumping from wave crest to
wave crest, and being entirely out of water at times.
From the front the view' suggested a number of enor-
mous fiat stones playing “ducks and drakes” along the
surface. Previously it had been commonly held that a
hydroplane w'ould be quite unmanageable in rough water,
but the regatta demonstrated that, although wind and
sea reduce their speed, they do not put them out of com-
mission.
The most disquieting lesson of the gathering
was the destruction by fire during a practice run of the
Italian champion, Fiat-Gallinari. Hitherto it has been
fondly believed that there is no danger of fire on a motor-
boat, and that explosions were impossible. The experi-
ence of the Italian boat suggests that, there are circum-
stances under which both are possible. This disaster
completely eliminated Italy from the star events, and
left England and France to fight it out between them.
The gems of the fleet were as follows

The English boat was unable to start in the Champion-
ship of the Sea race, the 200 kilometre star event, owing
to clutch troubles, and the Panhard-Levassor won easily
at a speed of 33 nautical miles per hour for 107 - 92 nauti-
cal miles. The winner of this event was defeated by the
Wolseley-Siddeley in the Prix de Monte Carlo 50 metres
race by 2min. 13sec., travelling at the rate of 28'19 miles
per hour. A curious thing was that the Panhard-Levassor
gained 300yds. in the starting jump, but the British boat
overhauled the Frenchman and won easily, so apparently
the Frenchman never got near his speed in the Champion-
ship of the Sea. However, in the Mile and Kilometre
events the French boat won, and in spite of the Prix de
Monte Carlo event the general opinion was that the
French boat was in reality the faster of the two, the
difference of 9ft. in their lengths giving the French boat
greater stability in rough water. Last Saturday the
Wolseley-Siddeley met the Yankees for the Harmsworth
Cup at Huntington Bay, Long Island, U.S.A.
* * * *
It is said that the Yankees who drove the
Thomas car on the Round-the-World race will claim first
place on the ground that they should be credited with
the 17 days they gained on other competitors while cross-
ing the States, and which they afterwards lost roaming
through Alaska after that territory had been cut out of
the contest. What other countries thought of the per-
formances of the Thomas people is indicated by the fact
that its proprietors received shoals of threatening letters
promising that the car would be blown up or otherwise
disposed of while crossing Siberia. The Thomas people
communicated with St. Petersburg, which ordered special
protection to be given while the car crossed Siberia. The
Russian Government’s guarantee didn’t count for much,
though—of late it has been unable to drive a cart with
treasure in it through one of its own big cities without
being bushranged by its own subjects; but as the car got
through safely, the threats were probably a mere expres-
sion of disgust for the unsportsmanlike methods of the
Americans.
* * * *
The hog in the car is in for a frosty time as far
as Great Britain is concerned, for the almost almighty
British Automobile Club has ukased that he is to be
killed off. And it has followed up the ukase with the
appointment of scouts in places where road-hoggism is
common. These men will give the police all possible
assistance to identify and punish road hogs, and will
supply the club with the numbers of the cars and photo-
graphs of the drivers. Members of the club who are
proved to be hogs will be requested to resign when two
complaints have been lodged and proved by the club’s
own scouts, or on the second conviction by the police.
If they decline to resign, the official boot will be applied,
and a man who has been expelled by the Automobile
Club will be a motor Ishmaelite, against whom any man’s
word will be taken. Men who are not members of the
club will be reported to any club to which they do belong,
or to their employers, and the employers will be reminded
of the danger of furious driving. The Auto. Club of
Great Britain is a sensible sort of association, with heaps
of influence and tons of money. Also, it is patriotic, and
insists that men who drive cars for payment are
“ motormen ” and not “ chauffeurs,” and in many of its
contests special prizes are given for cars built in British
factories.

So far Thorougligood has not accepted Pearce’s
challenge to scull over the Parramatta course for £lOO
a-side and the championship of Australia. He has to
consult his backers, and a meeting was to be held .on
Wednesday night, after this issue had gone to press.
Thorougligood himself is willing, but is said to favor the
Hunter River course opposite Raymond Terrace. Unfor-
tunately the Terrace course is never a good one for gate,
and the increased takings on the Parramatta River will
probably induce Thoroughgood to row in strange water.
It should be a splendid race, if it comes off.
Champion Webb is now pronounced to be as
sound as a bell, and quite recovered from the recent
attack of pleurisy. At time of writing, he had not re-
sumed rowing, and, realising that Arnst is a very devil to
pull, he will take no risks. There are still nearly four
months to go before the date of the race, and he will not
resume training till the weather has improved and the
cold, dreary wind that sweeps down the Wanganui River
in the winter months has subsided somewhat.
Five horses out of 16 starters finished the four
miles of big Steeplechase at Caulfield last Saturday. Two
or three others ran off the track in sheer disgust at being
expected to jump so often in so long a journey. Butithe
remainder mostly fell, and two of them —Cardinal land
Maine —were so broken down that they had to be mur-
dered soon afterwards in the name of humanity. Car-
dinal’s previous appearance in public had been his Grand .
National victory at Flemington—and this W'as the end of
him. Nigel, who was led to the course (like Lazarus)
covered with bandages, but full of condition as the result
proved, won the race by 15 lengths ; therefore he may be
reasonably expected to suffer a fatal accident next time
he goes for a big steeplechase. In these hotly-contested
long-distance jumping races the winner of to-day is the
corpse of to-morrow.
On August 22nd a Victorian hockey team will
meet a N.S.W. XI. in an inter-State match. The N.S.W.
team, with the exception of the 11th man, has been
chosen, and good practice work is being carried out. On
Saturday last, in spite of the fearsome state of the ground,
the Probables and Possibles met at Rushcutters Bay and
put up a sterling game. The Possibles made a grim
attack, and met as stubborn a defence, and when the re-
pulse was complete the Probables followed up, and arriving
within shooting distance put in some pretty work which
resulted in a score. This was succeeded by a deter-
mined charge, which completely overwhelmed the Pos-
sibles, who realised that the Lord had delivered them into
the hand of their opponent. However, they took their
gruel, and once, by 7 a sudden and well-sustained charge,
broke through and smashed the egg. But that was the
end of it. Probables w'on by five goals to one, and justi-
fied the choice of the selectors. Amen!
In a final game of 2000 up for the Vic. Amateur
Billiard Championship the holder of the title—Jacoby—
was beaten bv his old opponent, Tricks, whom he de-
feated last year. Tricks, without a doubt, deserved his
victory. He is the one Vic. amateur who from time to
time displays a “ professional ” skill and accuracy in his
manipulation of the balls. In the final game he made no
break higher than 80 odd, but his form was pretty con-
sistently good, and he scored on the average 100 points to
Jacoby’s 75—a very decisive win. Seeing that play was
an advance on his previous best, the friends of Tricks
expect him to put up a good fight with the N.S.W.
champion when they meet a few weeks hence.

If the ascending generation, now in the
stickjaw and hopscotch stage, does not acquire a
better all-round geographical knowledge of Aus-
tralasia than did its fathers and mothers, it will
not be the fault of H. E. C. Robinson, compiler
of the Lady Northcote Atlas, a book which
has nothing wrong with it save the ab-
surd name. (Why not the Alfred Deakin
Atlas?) This paper feels envious when it
remembers the skimpy imported publica-
tion from which it had to glean its first knowledge
of our own continent. The old-time foreign
atlas generally tucked Australia away in a
corner of the map of Polynesia or gave
it a bare page adjacent to the back cover.
The Northcote Atlas is a large, well-printed book
of 51 pages, containing 18 maps, a concise histori-
cal introduction, and an exhaustive index. It
shows trade routes, cables, the world’s spheres of
influence, temperatures, geological formation, and
generally bristles with information regarding the
earth, air and seas in this 'part of the globe.
The employment of the clerks in the
Tramway Department in the place of strikers
during the recent Sydney trouble has annoyed the
Clerks’ Union, which claims that, in requesting
the men to do this work, the Department was
practically using coercion ; and the union is going
to have the matter brought up in Parliament. As
a matter of fact, several of the clerks refused car-
duty, and others would probably have joined them
had they dared. It is a cheerful thing to at last
see a union battling for the unfortunate clerk.
When labor demands better conditions capital
always reckons his clerical staff in with his fight-
ing forces, and strikes a happy average between
the blackleg to whom he has to pay£l a day and
the clerk to whom he probably pays £1 a week.
He doesn’t think it necessary to ask the clerk
about it; he just tells him to go out and toil.
And mostly the clerk goes out, and does his best,
and soils his clothes, and blisters his hands, and
salves his wounds with the thought that he
belongs to the capitalistic class. Which he cer-
tainly does —just as a dog belongs to its master.
The N.S.W. Cabinet has decided not to
suspend Green, the murderer of Lily Bridge at
Allandale, but it gives no reason for its decision,
so no one knows whether George Reid’s plea of
“impulsive insanity” had any weight or not.
The Full Court confirmed the sentence, so
apparently, as far as the law is concerned, “im-
pulsive insanity ” is no more a defence now than
it was when Judge Burton and the Executive
Council hanged Knatchbull in 1844, despite the
tremendous energy with which Robert Lowe
defended him.
Defence Minister Ewing looks with a dull,
suspicious eye on the proposal of 300 returned
farm-combustibles to form themselves into a body
under the title of the South African Reserve.
Why not the South African Residue, or the
African Sediment? The fictitious glamor and
glory of the African scramble departed years ago,
and it is now admitted by everybody short of a
maniac that the war was a poor, mean thing,
as wars go, and one of the most awful
botches in Britain’s long list of fights
—dog and otherwise. Yet, notwithstanding
all this, the returned contingenter—or the least
sensible portion of him—still clings desperately to
his kharkee putties and his hat that looks like a
murdered Gladstone bag, and clamors to be allowed
to parade by himself in one heap, so that man-
kind generally may know him as the trusty com-
panion of undesirables in many a hard-robbed
fowlyard. Ewing should take that benighted 300
aside and quietly explain to them that South
Africa is something they’ve got to live down, not
live up to.
In 1907, deaths in France exceeded births
by 19,920. That seems to be the beginning of
national suicide in real earnest.

IN THE HIGH COURT OF AUSTRALIA
NEW SOUTH WALES REGISTRY ON
APPEAL FROM THE SUPREME
COURT OF NEW SOUTH WALES
Between
MAHALA BRADFORD
(Petitioner) Appellant and
WILLIAM SYDNEY BRADFORD
Respondent.
TAKE NOTICE that the above-named Apel-
lant by Special Leave of the High Court of
Australia granted on Wednesday the twenty-
ninth day of July instant appeals against the
Judgment of The Honorable George Bowen
Simpson Judge of the Matrimonial Causes Court
in the Supreme Court of New South Wales pro-
nounced on Monday the fifteenth day of June last
passed upon a Petition for Divorce presented by the
Appellant under Section 16 (a) of the Matrimonial
Causes Act 1899 Number 14 whereby His Honor
dismissed the said Petition upon the following
grounds
I.—THAT the evidence does not show that
the (Petitioner) Appellant was a consenting
party to the desertion of her by the Re-
spondent.
2.—-THAT upon the facts disclosed in evi-
dence the (Petitioner) Appellant is entitled to
a Decree for the dissolution of her marriage.
3.—That His Honor rejected evidence by the
(Petitioner) Appellant that the letter she last
wrote to the Respondent was an affectionate
letter though she could not remember the
precise terms she used.
4.—THAT in the circumstances of the case
the conduct of the (Petitioner) Appellant did
not prevent the conduct of the Respondent
from amounting to the offence of desertion
under section 16 (a) of the said Act.
DATED this Thirty-first day of July in the
year one thousand nine hundred and eight.
L. WHITFIELD, Counsel for Apellant.
THOMAS ROSE, Appellant’s Solicitor.
NOTE. —This Notice of Appeal has been filed
by THOMAS ROSE of Lyndhurst Chambers,
Number 84 Elizabeth Street Sydney in the State
of New South Wales Solicitor for the Appellant.

WINTER WISDOM
AS APPLIED TO CHAMBERLAIN’S COUGH REMEDY.
HOW TO PREVENT
COLDS.
COMMON-SENSE ADVICE.
Habitual colds are due to an ill-kept skin on the
outside, and by dyspeptic mucous membrane on
the inside, the result of indigestion, coupled with
carelessness. Cold water, proper food, and com-
mon sense are the foundations upon which a pre-
ventive must rest. A cold sponge bath, one to
three minutes long, before breakfast, with a brisk,
dry rub, is excellent—usually all that is necessary
to keep the cutaneous circulation alive, and the
skin reactive to sudden changes of temperature.
For those unaccustomed to cold water, tolerance
can be gained in three weeks’ time by the use of
water at any comfortable temperature, making it
one degree colder each day, until it can be
employed, without dread, as cold as it will run.
Sea salt may be added to the water for its stimu-
lating effect. Cold water, intelligently used, does
not steal vitality, but fosters it. It stimulates the
nerves that control the expansion and contraction
of the blood vessels, and regulates the cutaneous
circulation. One should “keep moving” when
wet or! chilly, and not stand on a street corner or
elsewhere without taking deep breath. The lungs
used in this way act as a pump, and drive the
blood along. This practice, with the others
named, will reduce to a shadow the liability of
taking cold every little while. If you should
contract a cold, get rid of it as quickly as possible,
for every cold weakens the lungs, lowers the
vitality, and paves the way for more serious
diseases. This is best accomplished by the use of
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy, a preparation that
can always be depended upon, and that not only
cures the cold, but counteracts any tendency
toward pneumonia.
HACKING COUGHS
CAN BE PROMPTLY CHECKED.
For Hacking Coughs—the kind that almost tear
you to pieces—that make your head ache —your
throat and lungs sore and inflamed—take
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy. For Hacking
Coughs that rob you of your sleep till your system
becomes so run down that you are in grave danger
of Pneumonia and Consumption, take Chamber-
lain’s Cough Remedy, and be quickly cured. It
soothes and heals the inflamed air passages, stops
the tickling in the throat, and by its tonic effect
enables you to throw off the disease. Every
bottle of Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy is guaran-
teed to give immediate relief, and cure your cough.
THE CHILD’S LIFE
AND DANGERS THAT
THREATEN.
The worst danger to which the children of
Australia are exposed is the neglected cold. If
your baby has a cold, don’t say, “It will wear
off,” and let it go at that. If your children have
the snuffles, or sore throats, or watery eyes, don’t
hesitate to take them from school from a day or
two so that you may break up the colds they have.
Every parent can add to the healthy condition of
the family and to his or her own peace of mind by
guarding against any and every kind of cold.
Generally the child does not know how it caught
cold. It may be impossible to prevent it exposing
its sensitive body to those dangers which older
persons shunt But the chances of contracting
colds can be easily diminished by a little care <rnd
forethought. If the children get colds, don’t
waste any time. Don’t trifle with a cold. This
warning should be heeded by all. The lives of
many children are sacrificed each year by
neglecting the colds which they take ; others con-
tract chronic catarrh, from which they never fully
recover. Of all the cough mixtures sold, Cham-
berlain’s Cough Remedy certainly heads the list
as the most efficacious for colds, croup and whoop-
ing cough in children, and the one remedy that
always can be depended upon to effect a speedy
cure, and that is pleasant and safe to take. This
remedy contains no opium or other harmful drug,
and may be given as confidently to a baby as to an
adult. Its great worth and merit in the treatment
of these diseases has been attested by many
thousands of mothers.
COMMON COLDS.
We claim that if catching colds could be
avoided, a long list of dangerous ailments would
never be heard of. Everyone knows that pneu-
monia and consumption originate from a cold ;
that chronic catarrh, chronic bronchitis, and all
throat and lung troubles are aggravated and
rendered more serious by each fresh attack. Do
not risk your life or take chances when you have a
cold, even though you do call it a common cold.
If taken in time, Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy
will cure it before these diseases can develop.
This remedy contains no opium , morphine or other
harmful drugs , and has thirty years of reputation
back of it, gained by its cures under every
condition.
CHILD’S NEGLECTED
COLD
AND THE RESULT.
Colds are the special banes of childhood, and
often lay the foundation for the more serious
diseases of after-life. One child catches cold, and
scares its mother into hysterics by having croup in
the dead of night. Another child catches cold, and
before it has fully recovered from it, takes another
cold, and perhaps before that is over contracts a
fresh one. This succession of colds, or even the
neglect of a common cold, causes the inflammation
of the mucous membrane, which was at first acute,
to become chronic, and the doctor will tell
you that the child has chronic catarrh, from which
it will never fully recover. Another child catches
cold. Little attention is paid to it, as the child
has often had colds, and always recovered from
them ; but in this instance a stubborn cough is
developed. The parents are filled with fore-
bodings. Still another child catches cold, and
could easily have been cured by proper treatment,
but, being neglected, develops the most fatal
malady of childhood, pneumonia. The doctor is
called, and pronounces it lung fever, and if the
child is lucky enciugh to live, it has developed
weak lungs, making it susceptible to all lung
diseases. Every one of these children could have
been cured by the prompt use of Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy. A few doses of it, and the child’s
cold is gone. It counteracts any tendency of a
cold to result in pneumonia, quickly dispelling all
fear of that dangerous disease. It is also a
certain cure for croup, and has never been
known to fail. When given as soon as the
croupy cough appears, it will prevent the attack.
A medicine of such great worth and merit as
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy should be kept in
every house where there are young children.
Don’t wait till the child is sick, and then have to
send for it, but keep it on hand, ready for instant
use when needed.
Every Bottle Guaranteed.'
If you fail to get beneficial results after using
two-thirds of a bottle of
CHAMBERLAIN’S
COUGH REMEDY
according to directions, return it to your dealer,
and get your money back. It is well known for
its prompt cures of coughs, colds, and croup.
HOW COLDS ARE
CONTRACTED
AND PROPER TREATMENT. ,
A cold is always the result of undue exposure
to low temperatures. The rapid cooling of: the
surface, when not balanced by proper reaction,
produces congestion and inflammation of the
nasal and bronchial membranes. Obviously, such
an ailment is not communicable, in the ordinary
sense, from one individual to another. As the
slightest “cold ” predisposes the individual to at-
tacks of the most severe and dangerous catarrhal
affections, the necessity for its quick cure need
not be enforced. Thele facts emphasise the
necessity of extra precautionary measures
against the ordinary cold. Everyone, cannot change
his climate at will, but may make the most of
what he has at home —viz., take Chamberlain s
Cough Remedy as soon as the first indication
of the cold appears. It not Only cures a bold
quickly, but counteracts any tendency of the cold
to result in pneumonia. .This fact has.been fully
proven during the epidemics of colds and influenza
of the past few years. No case of either of these
diseases having resulted in pneumonia when this
remedy was used has ever been reported to the
manufacturers, and thousands of bottles of it are
sold every day. This shows conclusively that it
is not only the best and quickest cure for colds,
but a certain preventive of that dangerous disease,
pneumonia.
SORE THROAT.
A SIGN OF WEAKNESS.
A sore throat indicates there is some weakness
in the bronchial tubes, There is an irritation; a
feeling of some foreign substance lodged there ;
and a choking sensation is experienced. If this is
neglected it soon develops into some chronic com-
plaint. People who are troubled this way are
most susceptible to Influenza, contract Pneumonia
readily, and fall easy victims to Consumption.
You must remove this irritation, soothe and heal
the inflamed tubes. There is nothing equal to
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy for this. It gives
tone to the weakened tissues of the throat,
strengthens the bronchial tubes, and at the same
time helps build up the system. When the
throat is healthy, the influenza or consumptive
germs can obtain no foothold.
“Wyung” discourses on the Victorian police
gun:—
The Victorian police regulations insist on certain men
carrying six-shooters, and “permit ” the balance to take a
gun if they want to. There is a regulation pistol—a
VVebley. But it is out of date, and too ponderous to be
carried in a pocket, and too conspicuous to be carried
round the neck on a chain. So it is not used—except
by the mounted men, who drag it around on a hprse.
The old-fashioned “service Webley,” with its old-
fashioned spindle extractor, is part of the regulation
equipment of every trooper. But the trooper is never
required to practice with it —which may be fortunate in
some ways, but is fat-headed in others. A while ago I
backed my revolver against a Gippsland trooper’s official
“ gun.” The trooper shot first, and at the second fire his
pistol worked loose. At the third shot the extractor
worked out, and when the gun was empty several of the
parts had fallen out on the grass. Not a shot found the
target. The John said he’d get the station revolver. He
did. It was a muzzle-loading Colt, navy pattern. It
transpired that this venerable machine and its paper
cartridges form part of the armament of most country
police stations.
But even among the mounted men, with whom the
carrying of revolvers is compulsory, knowledge of the
weapons and ability to use them without calamitous
results to disinterested bystanders is not required. Under
the regulations, all watchhouses must have a service
pistol always loaded and ready for use. It is the old-
fashioned pattern aforesaid —the one that comes to pieces
on its own account. But not even the watchhouse
keeper, who has charge of it, is required to practise
shooting with it.
When any shooting has to be done by the bobby force
in Victoria, things happen. One morning a Carlton con-
stable pulled a gun on a burglar, and fired in the air by
way of a hint or suggestion. The shot got the quarry in
the leg. This was not a service revolver. It
was one of a kind generally carried by the metro-,,
politan police for three reasons—it is small, light, and
cheap. It shoots him who ought not to be shot, and
leaves unshot him who ought to be shot, and there is no
good cartridge in it. Most of the Melbourne city police
go armed with this brand of gun—price 7s. fid. Within
the last six months, four thieves have been challenged by
John, with his seven-and-sixpence worth in his hand, to
“ stand,” on pain of being shot, and each of the four has
told John, with r.he calmness of sure knowledge, to
“ shoot; away and be Not even a solitary pushite,
nowadays, is disturbed by the apparition of a John’s gun.
He continues to bash his “ cop ” whole-heartedly and with-
out concern, though citizens in the vicinity flee for shelter.

of Sydney University,” or medical school, no one

ney’s famous ex-Mayor, who propounded the great

After long thought and much soul-wrestling
as to the present and prospective values of sympa-
thy and cash, the monopolistic Melb. Tram Co.
decided that the bird in the hand was worth more
than all that there may be in the bush, and
declined to give free tram-rides to American
fleet sailors in uniform. Its letter of refusal was
a masterpiece of snuffle. “ My directors,” wrote
Secretary Sprigg to the fleet committee, “have con-
sidered your suggestion, and I am to inform you
that they are quite unable to adopt the suggestion,
as to do so would interfere with the regular traffic
and involve great interference to the public.”
The company’s tender regard for the public—
especially the public that hangs on by the skin of
its teeth when leaving a football match-—is a
touching circumstance, and about reaches the
limit. The co also refused to carry the cadets
to Flemington free on review day, but that action
is understandable, as the average boy or youth
considers it a merit to do the eo. out of a fare,
wherefore the co. loathes the nipper.
“ First-timer ” writes :—-
A few words from the other point of view in reference
to the Victorian Indeterminate Sentences Act for con-
firmed criminals, which will shortly come into operation.
Without going- into the larger question that crime is a
disease, it strikes me that the very term “confirmed
criminal is an impeachment of the present penal sys-
tem. If any man is crank enough to prefer doing time to
working for an honest living, I reckon he is a fit subject
for examination by a pair of medicos. I have had a pro-
tracted residence at Pentridge, and I know- that 75 per
cent, of the prisoners would be only too clad to do honest
work if they could get it. Just consider the prospect
that faces a man after his first slip. Only one trade is
open to first offenders at Pentridge, and that is printing ;
all the other trades—carpentering, plumbing, woollen
manufacture, tinsmithing, brush-making, flax treatment,
etc.—are confined to old offenders. A man serves (say) a
five years’ sentence. On his discharge he is, if he is lucky,
an indifferent compositor. He may have spent the whole
time feeding a machine or sewing books. He receives £6
at the gate. It is a million to one that his former occu-
pation is closed to him from lack of references. He knows
just enough about type-setting to never get a job at it.
In effect, a man is given no chance to learn a trade till
the “system” has made a confirmed criminal of him.
The’ide'a of shutting a man up for life for being the thing
you made him is just on a par with the whole administra-
tion of the Victorian Penal Code. Surely it would pay to
give wrongdoers a chance of earning a living if only to
save the cost of maintenance. A good 60 per cent, of the
criminal class thieve from necessity, and the bulk of the
remaining 40 per cent, are just plain cranks, and should
be treated as such
As a rule, the lethargy of Victoria’s Bent
Government these days is fearful to witness. In-
deed, people marvel at the violence with which it
can remain so tired, and at the energy which
urges it to do nothing at 60 miles an hour. So'
when it does betray a little animation, and slum-
bers a little less than usual, folk can’t understand
what has disturbed the calm. On a recent Tues-
day, five dead-weary gentlemen gave notice of
intention to bring in the following hew Bills,
some time before Gabriel starts the last shriek :
(1) A Bill to amend the Mining Development
Act: (2) a Bill to regulate the use of motors ; (3)
a Bill to provide for the licensing of estate and
other agents ; (4) a Bill to.provide for the inspec-
tion of scaffolding ; (5) a Bill to amend the law
relating to moneylenders. Then five dead-weary
gentlemen resumed their fatigue, and waited
with magnificent patience for the adjournment,
which, it is suggested, ought to be abolished,
since the moving of adjournments is about the
only thing that brings members to the House at
all. They would so like to stay at home and
have their wages brought out by deaf mutes
wearing felt slippers and with their thoughts
muffled, so that the slumbers of Victoria’s chosen
legislators wouldn’t be interrupted.
Hosea Biglow’s “ pious editor,” who did
“believe in Freedom’s cause as fur away as Paris
is,” but was mighty tender to slavery at home,
has a whole-hearted follower in the Australasian...
In its affection for the big landlord and other
Tory circumstances it licks even its daily Argus ;■
but, having lately cast its eve, over the, system of'
tenant-farming along the Western line in N.,S.
Wales, it is appalled :— ' ,
A more deplorable system of land occupation it would,
be impossible to conceive. The leases were of irregular
length ; some were renewed from year to year ; others
extended over a considerable period. Where t ie latter
prevailed, farms were, as might be expected, in slightly
better trim than those under an annual tenure. But on
none did there appear anything of credit to either owner
or lessee. The leases were, as they are in a majority of
cases in this State, entirely without any arrangements
governing improvements. The aim of the landlord ap-
peared to be to get the last shilling in rent with a mini-
mum of expenditure.
Yet the same could be said ten times over about'
Victoria, which is far more shocking than N.S.W.;
in fact, the same could be said wherever the farm
is merely rented or occupied on shares. The Aus-
tralasian says the remedy is a Landlord and
Tenants Act—which wouldn’t be a remedy at all,
but only a palliative, and not very much of that.
A letter from one Mohan Singh, published
in the Bridgetown (W.A.) paper, indicates in
interesting fashion the strange, muddled view the
more or less educated nig. takes of life’s problems.
Mohan hasa grievance against a person named Mot-
tram, who supported another person, named'Young,
in the theory that Young was justified in running
a road through Mohan’s suburban block (No. 3) at
Manjimup Brook. After some desultory palaver
in disparagement of Mottram —but utterly uncon-
nected with the subject of trespass —the Asiatic
unwinds his vocabulary in very earnest. Hb
writes : —
Mr. Mottram thinks that he has a brain to work on,
but. my opinion about his brain is that it must be a weak
one, or iie does not know how to use it. He must not
think that I came down with the last shower of rain. I
am here to fight for my rights with my last penny. This
is the British rule who made the goat and tiger to drink
water from one pool. I belong to the Sikh race who
relieved Oawnpore, Lucknow and Delhi in 1857’s mu’iny,
arid kept the British flag flying over India ever since. Now
that trouble is recurring in India, this is the only race that
the British can depend upon. I am very proud of my
color, thank God. The late Queen Victoria had great
faith in. the Sikh race, and I hope that King Edward
VII. will follow his mother’s footsteps, as he promised on
Coronation Day.
Mohan’s regard for British rule, “rights” and
the laws of King Ned is academic mainly. Thus
in the last couple of years this worthy Sikh has
been haulbd before the beak to answer charges
ranging from causing a disturbance to attempt-;
ing to puncture the local publican with a meat-
carver.
Mrs.-Bridget Jones, a registered’dairy-
keeper of Kyneton (Vic.) was summoned (22/7/’08)
for keeping her, premises in a- state of unclean-
liness. According to next day’s Melbourne
Argus ’
The evidence disclosed a disgusting state of affairs. The
dairy floor was covered with muck, the dairy utensils
were filthily dirty, and the dirt could be scraped off a
table there. In a lean-to shed on one side of the dairy
was aheap of remnants of the carcases of cows that had
died. These bones, defendant told Inspector Ward, had
been under the shed for seven days. They smelt offen-
sively. In a pigsty, half a dozen pigs were feeding on
some similar offal, served to them raw, and a sheep that
had died had also been thrown in to them without having
its entrails removed. The sty was in a filthy state, and
an insanitary, evil-smelling drain was found to traverse
the yard. . .. . )
And instead of summarily condemning Bridget to
be torn asunder by wild odors, the Kyneton
Bench fined her a paltry sovereign and sent her
home to resume her evil-smelling career at the
old address.

The co., with a capital of £lO,OOO and reserves as shown
above, has “ sundry creditors ” for £12,310. On the other
side, besides liquid assets (£5955), mortgages (£4575), and
property (£3125), it has “sundry debtors, £17,429.”
Apparently it borrows money to lend it out again ; and,
though the transactions may be explained (for instance,
these maj' be advances to trust estates), the position is
much too vaguely expressed. If the co. simply takes
deposits to lend them out in the ordinal way of business,
then the law shouldn’t allow it in the case of a trustee
co.—it doesn’t in Victoria. The business is profitable, as
all trustee businesses are; on capital and reserves com-
bined the profits for the past 12 months show a return of
£7 14s. 2d. per cent.

The co.’s paid capital is £15,000, and the reserves are
climbing up to the same amount. There is no doubt
about these reserves either, as there is about those of
many cos. : £lBOO is in advances (mortgages) and £2200

per cent, of trust estates per annum, of which a shade
more than half goes in the co.’s expenses, leaving the
balance as profit. These and other trustee cos. are doing
an excellent service, but, based on capital invested, their
profits are very high. In this case, on capital and
reserves combined, the half-year’s profit shows a return of
£l4 11s. sd. per cent. A revision of the charges made by
these cos. seems inevitable sooner or later.

This co., like many others in the gas business, doesn’t
say what provision it makes to cover the general rust and
decay of mains, plant, etc. If a co.’s mains will last only
20years, then obviously it ought to put aside enough
profits to wipe out the cost of the mains in 20 years. If
the thains cost £lO,OOO, then £250 ought to be charged
against profits every half year, and accumulated either in
cash or (if additions are called for) in new plant, etc. If
this charge is not made—that is to say, if a co. declares
its full profit without any deduction—then that co. is
steadily using up its capital (represented by gas mains,
etc.), and is calling the capital profit, which it isn’t. Gas
cos. have, however, got into bad habits in many
places. The fact is that a few big concerns, being
afraid of possible municipal enterprise, have felt it
necessary to smother up their afEairs ; and, in N. S.
Wales and Queensland especially, the smaller cos. have
followed this bad lead, even where the same reason does
not apply. In this case one doesn’t know what (if any)
provision is made for general wear and tear ; and that is
another way of saying that the profits may or may not be
wholly genuine. Five years ago the plant, land and
buildings had a book value of £76,946 ; they are now
valued at £82,348. Thus £5402 has been spent on plant,
etc., in five years, and Charged to capital account; but
there is no way of telling whether anything, and, if any-
thing, how niuch, has been spent and provided out of
profits. In such cases it is necessary for the investor to
know more than the published accounts tell him—or
stand off. This co. has 70,000 ordinary shares fully paid to
10s., 50,000 pref. shares paid to 7s. (3s. may still be called);
also there are 80,000 unissued ordinaries. Besides, there is
£15,000 worth of 4 per cent, debentures ; but the debts
beyond that only amount to £1521. The preference
shares have a first claim to 6 per cent, and rank with
ordinaries in anything above that. Brisbane appreciates
both classes of shares as an investment, and rarely nowa-
days lets them pass below 100 per cent. premium. With
the prefs. there is always the possibility of the other 3s.
being called up to share in the high dividend. The
debentures are due in 1915.

The co. has lately taken over the small works for the
supply of the northern (Newport) end of Williamstown.
This Newport property is taken in the balance-sheet at
£5OOO. To pay for this the co. has realised its Board of
Works and other debentures (£2100), and has turned a
credit balance of £869 into an overdraft of £2ol6—before
payment of dividend in each case. The co., in common
with most Victorian gas-suppliers, publishes ample manu-
facturing and profit and loss accounts, so that people may
know just what has been spent on repairs and renewals,
and what also has been charged against revenue to cover
depreciation (shown above as written off). Plant, mains
and meters, apart from the Newport purchase, represent
£25,129 ; so that the amount written off is about 2J per
cent, per annum. The co. has had a long and prosperous
career, and looks as though it were carefully handled.
* * * *
An enterprising Victorian has found pearls in
some of the mussels that pervade certain Gippsland
creeks. There is nothing new about the mussel pearl,
but some of those shown to Premier Bent are valued
at £5 apiece, which makes them worth hunting. The
discoverer would not have bothered to cast his pearls
before Bent; only he doesn’t seem to be able to acquire
any right; title or lease of the locality of the find, and he
wants Thomas to help him to a reward claim or some-
thing similar. The Mines Department says its jurisdic-
tion doesn’t extend to sea-going minerals or to those
that dwell in fresh waters ; and the State Rivers and
Waters Commission offers to sell the prospector a lot of
water at a cheap rate, but can give him no right to the
fish found therein. Possibly Bent might proclaim the
pearl-mussel a “ thistle,” and appoint the pearler a bailiff
over the district of his discovery, with sole authority to
capture the beasts wherever found. Anyhow, it is a
pleasing discovery, and goes to show that Socialistic
legislation hasn’t yet driven all the gems out of the
country, though on account of Bent’s roaring some of
them have taken to the water.

HE TERRIBLE ATTACKS which
Influenza makes upon the popula-
A tion from time to time are only too'
well known to enlarge upon them here.
Severe headache, extending to the eyeballs
and behind the eyes, is one of the most
marked and painful symptoms of this
insidious disease. These headaches occur
more particularly with those persons who
suffer from liver disorders and whose
bowels are frequently constipated. The
poison of Influenza seems to fasten upon
the centre of least resistance in the consti-
tution, and it is, therefore, highly essential
in every case to keep the bowels well regu-
lated, the liver active, the kidneys cleansed,
and the whole of the digestive organs in
perfect condition. This is what Kutnow’s
Powder does, and this is the reason why
Kutnow’s Powder acts as a prophylactic
in disease ; or, in other words, is a pre-
ventive of the trouble.

The following circular letter, issued by the
Brisbane Telephone Exchange, is a rarity in its
way
CIRCULAR LETTER TO TELEPHONE SUBSCRIBERS.
With reference to the notice (three months’) of inten-
tion to discontinue the use of a telephone line, or exten-
sion required under the existing Telephone Regulations,
I have to inform you the question having been raised as
to the notice required from subscribers who entered into
agreements under old Regulations in which provision is
not made for such notice to be given, or which provide
that the agreements shall be terminable on the expiration
of notice less than that required by the present Regula-
tions, it has been decided that the services of all sub-
scribers under such agreements will, after the expiration
of the period covered by the agreements, be continued
only in accordance with the Telephone Regulations for
the time being in force.
A thing like that is more than a circular ; it is a
circumbendibus, or circumlocution, or whirligig
with deviations.
A Victorian in London writes of begging
as a fine art: —■
I had sampled the cadgers of Ceylon, had a rough-up
with a crowd .at Port Said that would beg the bone off a
lost dog, and fell in with the working classes of Egypt
who will run a mile to cadge a copper ; but I didn’t know
what begging was till I touched London. Possibly the
people here don’t live by begging alone, but so far nearly
all I have come in contact with have been out in pursuit
of tips. They don’t yell “Backsheesh!” at you, or
yabber “ Gib it tshillin’! ” but they get there just the
same ; and if I were an Englishman I would shut up
tight about the beggars of other countries, while
London literally swarms with people working in
scores of capacities, who are absolute beggars, and
nothing better. To see a long row of servants lined
up in the hall of a hotel waiting to touch the parting
guest, who has been robbed in the bill for everything he
has had, seen, heard, smelt, or eaten, is to appreciate the
worst phase of abject beggary. Here everybody wants a
tip, especially the porters, and particularly the police-
men. lam certain that if there is anything in the Dar-
winian theory of development, the Cockney policeman of
a few generations hence will have a third arm, growing
out of the middle of his back.
The Oldest Inhabitant maunders vaguely
about public-houses and other ancient memories :
Apropos the Star and Garter (King-street, Sydney),
which recently passed out as an inn. The house was in
the late ’forties the Hibernian printing office. William
Baker, who annually printed a sheet almanac, was the
proprietor. He was the publisher of “ Heads of the
People,” after Kenny Meadows’ style, and issued
“Charles O’Malley” in weekly or monthly parts, with
illustrations done in Sydney by a deaf and dumb man,
who was a marvellous engraver. On the other side of
the lane was the Albion printing office —T. Trood, proprie-
tor. The Star and Garter corner is owned by Mrs. Mann,
a daughter of the late Thomas Farrell, who kept a wine
and spirit store in King-street, at the rear of the now
K., S. and A. Bank. The banking house was formerly
known as the Welsh Harp, and was kept by T. Farrell’s
father, Michael Farrell, who afterwards kept the Farriers’
Arms in George-street, opposite the Royal Hotel, and next
to the farriery of William Garland. Mrs. Thomas Farrell
was, a sister of Moses Murphy, who kept the Adelphi
hotel in front of Malcolm’s amphitheatre (or circus) in
York-street, afterwards the Lyceum, and in 1880 kept by
Larry Foley. Mr. Thomas Farrell died a very rich man,
being'the owner of Petty’s hotel and a lot of city pro-
perty. Ex-Sergeant-Major Baynes, afterwards Colonel
Baynes, kept the Farriers’ Arms in the late ’fifties and
early ’sixties. The Sergeant-Major, on his retirement
from the 11th Regiment, opened a pub near the old toll-
bar on the Paddington-road, which he named the Colonel
Bloomfield hotel, after the popular commander of his
regiment. The house stood on the corner of what is now
Glenmore-road.

The Voice from the Skull.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The mummy of Prince Khnumu Nekht, wrapped up
some 4400 years ago, was unrolled on May 6 in the Man-
chester University. It is the oldest mummy that has
undergone this treatment, and the result was awaited
with keen interest. . . . The mummy was swathed in
several layers of bandages in a style quite different from
that of the last mummy that was unwrapped. That was
one of the 26th dynasty. When the last bandage had
been removed, the bones crumbled into dust, with the
exception of the skull, which remained intact. News
item.
SWATHED in my grave-cloths, I slept where
the darkness was bearded and grey ;
Soft-footed centuries passed me and indo-
lent years oozed away
Until, rough-handed, ye dr: g ;ed me from dreams
of my long-vanished day.
Weary was I of the vain pomp of kings—of the
plotting and lust,
When they laid me away like a worn-out sword in
silence to rust;
Rare spices sweetened the breath of the grave
that would turn me to dust.
Every vain art of man that could keep the great
corse from decay
Guarded me well from the merciful worm I would
welcome to-day.
So, tightly bound in my wrappings, I dreamed
till e’en Time swooned away.
Lost and forgotten of devils and gods in that deep
well of night,
Sunk in the slow-flowing dreams of the dead that
are endless delight,
Thus did I dwell in the kingdom of Death safely
hid from Man’s sight.
Life is the madness the gods give to men at the
moment of birth ;
Life—the blind instinct that urges us on to our
fight with the earth,
And the fierce gods looking down on the struggle
are moved to loud mirth.
This is the truth slowly gleaned in the worlds of
the sun and the shade :
“ Strive not to fly the embrace of the earth out of
which ye are made.
Only as dust shall ye taste of true rest. Lie ye
down unafraid.”
N.S.W.
E.O’F.
The Tale of Ling Ah Too.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HIS earliest distinct recollection came
from the day when he strutted out of his
home in Little Lonsdale-street and
marched, gaudy in his dress of red and green,
along Spring-street to Parliament House, batter-
ing the toy drum slung at his waist. He was a
pretty little boy, with solemn unfathomable eyes,
clear olive skin, and a frame which had taken wire
from Ah Foo and brawn from Ah Foo’s wife
Maggie. Maggie had been through many ex-
periences before she came to rest in Ah Foo’s
skinny arms, but this sturdy infant—with the
Saxon name of Cedric stolen from a novelette !
might have been the child of her first healthy
womanhood.
A fat and pompous legislator, coming down
the steps, felt his eye caught by the little blob of
gay color at his feet. This legislator believed
himself a political philosopher, being skilled in
weaving repulsive theories which gave the lie to
facts. He had just made a speech, imploring his
colleagues not to wound the tender susceptibilities
of “ Oriental” nations.
“There,” he said, striking an attitude for the
benefit of the pillars and three-ball lamp-post on
the footpath, “ is a unit of our future-soldiery.
Eastern subtlety and Anglo-Saxon pluck. Some
day, I wager, the little fellow will beat his drum
in a hotter corner than this !” The fancy pleased
him ; he glowed with appreciation of his own dip
into the future. He even paused to put a finger
under the baby’s chin. “Be a real Aus-
tralian soldier when you grow up, hey ? ” he said,
caressingly. The fingers of the other white,
jewelled hand were in his waistcoat pocket, and
Cedric Ah Foo, Australian-born citizen of the
Empire, stood with vague but unbetrayed visions
of a penny. But the legislator was not that sort.
He was feeling for a tram ticket. He found it,
and rode into the city, where he was booked to
lecture on “Naval and Military Defence ” to the
Women's Club. Cedric, though he never knew it,
earned fame instead of copper. Next day he
figured, though not by name, in three newspaper
reports. A week later he was caricatured ; then
forgotten.
He grew up all the same, going to school and
Sunday-school; an apeish semi-Australian by
day, turning into an inscrutable, unintelligible
little Chinese at night. Grown older, he learned
to play billiards with his brother half-castes at the
hotel where Melbourne’s half-castes congregated.
He developed an unceasing amorousness. There
were, some damsels who smiled upon him with
patronage which his insinuating skill was able to
turn into surrender ; others tilted scornful noses,
and audibly called him a Chow. His chubbiness of
beauty had left him, but he was still a handsome
boy. He was treated with large and friendly
tolerance in the junior cricket club, and was a
popular hero on the day when he made his cen-
tury. The legislator, now in Heaven, may have
looked at him with pride from a front seat among
the clouds. But inside the doors from which
Maggie had long since departed—in a hearse—
Cedric stepped across the gulf which splits the
world in two, while Ling Ah Foo smiled and
smoked, and talked nasally to the son he loved.
At the proper age, Cedric began his military
training along with thousands of others. They
were a mixed crew—the “flood of immigration”
had started, and strange people from Europe were
spilling excitedly into Australia. Private Cedric
Foo, among many slack and gaping drillers, was
an exemplary recruit. He shot well, marched
well, and held himself like a soldier.
At the end of his training came War.
Out of the north-west the locusts had gathered.
The swarm was devouring Queensland. In
desperate confusion, with trains breaking down,
ammunition dumped in deserts where no guns had
gone, and lost stores standing piled on forgotten
railway stations, the army went up against it.
Cedric, in obedience to proclamation, reported
himself to a regiment preparing to leave Mel-
bourne. Ling Ah Foo, weeping with fright, was
at the police-station, crying, “Me Blitish sub-
ject!” while he displayed an ancient parchment.
Armed men stood, day and night, guarding Little
Lon. from flames and murder, and Ling Ah Foo
wept afresh with every screw he dropped into the
luxurious “wedding wardrobe” he was making
to order.
* # * *
“ Gawd—spare—me—days ! ” Sergeant Wilkins
said softly, as he looked at the section under his
command. Private Foo, erect and clean, but
with a twitching mouth, stood between an overfed
Polish Jew and a slovenly Greek. “Dagoes an’
Chows ! ” the Sergeant muttered. Dave Linburn,
captain of the company, heard the murmur and
went away to swear to himself.
Everyday the newspapers were horrible with
tales from the north—massacre, pillage and
ravishment. Men and women alike were bearing
arms up there, and both were dying. The stories
of sobbing fugitives, quivering southward along
all the telegraph wires, sent the cities mad with
hate and terror. An officer watched every ten
men who stood with fixed bayonets in Little Lon.
and its lanes. Linburn went to his Colonel. He
wanted no Cedric Foo in his company. The
Colonel, precise and formal, dismissed him with a
sharp word.
Private Foo had read the newspapers—brief,
tragic stories told in tumbled w»rds by the
correspondents, whose pens hardly knew how to
dip into this molten tragedy after so many years
of placid reporting. His eyes grew dark and
sombre. With a half-conscious effort he dragged
into his brain all that was Australian in his life,
his school, his cricket, his training. But when he
spent a day on duty in Little Bourke-street, and
the heavy smell of it blew down to his nostrils, a
haze swept through his mind, and in a livid mist
there surged past his vision all the girls who had
called him Chow. He lay awake at night with an
illusion of Ling’s voice pouring monotonously in
his ears; or fell asleep to dream that he was
luxuriously slitting the throats of the men who
breathed heavily round him. At these moments
it would have been worth while to put slices of
Ah Foo’s brain under a microscope.
At last they went northward.
■*■***
Dave Linburn, officer commanding B Company,
87th Regiment, lay in a little rifle-pit scratched
out on the breast of a long hill, behind which the
railway ran down to Rockhampton. The line
was thin; here there was nothing but the skir-
mishing overflow of a battle which for days had
covered 40 miles of country. Sergeant Wil-
kins, growling and sweating, lay three yards from
his captain. Anyone walking past ten men—had
he walked that far alive—would have found Pri-
vate Cedric Foo resting quietly in another scrabble
in the ground. Near him the Pole was stretched
quaking, his rifle idle. The Greek, having found
a fold in the hill, was worming himself back-
ward and sideways, hoping desperately to hit on a
way to the other side.
In front, wherever the ground undulated, rifles
would lift themselves into view and spit a bullet
every few seconds. Cedric Foo, his barrel thrust
forward cautiously, replied with the same deadly
message. He heard the Greek, with sobbing
breath, crawl behind him.
“ Blanky coward!” muttered the son of Maggie,
and pressed his shoulder to the butt again. A
hundred rifles jerked from the earth in front.
He suddenly felt that he had taken a blow—
where he could not tell. All that he felt was a
fierce sense of outrage and hurt; a peace-destroy-
ing shock which called for murder to avenge it.
As a matter of fact, a bullet had merely flipped
through his arm. Men are wounded like this
without knowing they are hit. There was no
pain; the permanent damage was nothing; but
the bullet, as it flicked through the little
nerves, changed the whole aspect of the
universe for Cedric Ah Foo. The balance in
which his brain had swung was kicked down with
decision. His race was determined. It was an
Asiatic, longing to run amok, who looked into the
noisy air with profound, unseeing eyes. He
rose, and with his bayoneted rifle held correctly
at the charge, walked slowly along the wavy line
of prostrate men. The Pole gasped.
“Mad, by Heffen !” he whispered.
The bullets struck the ground with an infernal
clapping, but Cedric, with Ling’s voice roaring
in his ears, walked on. He came beside the cap-
tain. A bullet, having taken Linburn in the
right shoulder, was burning somewhere near his
liver. He turned as far as he was able. “Lie
down, you damned fool! ” he said, faintly.
There was the ghost of a smile on Foo’s blood-
less lips, but his eyes were expressionless. Two
or three filthy words of Chinese leapt out of his
mouth. He drew his rifle butt back and up, and
with admirable precision plunged the bayonet
between Linburn’s shoulder-blades.
The bullets spat on, and none touched the
Chinaman. But Wilkins, with a little cry like a
hurt child, swung his rifle and fired. Ah Foo
dropped, his lungs pouring blood into his mouth.
The sergeant jumped across to his captain, and
threw one arm round his neck to lift his face from
the dust.
Linburn would never speak again, but Ah Foo
spoke once. “My father,” he gurgled through
the blood that choked him. Wilkins’ muzzle
was not a foot away from Cedric’s head. He
jerked a cartridge into place, and fired again.
“ My Gawd ! if ever I find ’is father,” Wilkins
panted, and began to cry stupidly.
* * * *
But that same evening Ling Ah Foo crept be-
yond the corner of the street to buy the news-
paper which cried that the army was fighting
desperately round Rockhampton. In the morning,
a rigid, bruised old body was found staring at the
slit cut out of the sky by the walls of a narrow
lane.
