Some Mistakes in “The
Banner.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ T am looking for the editor,” said the
I tall, thin man, in a voice of utter sadness.
The editor of The Wild Dog Banner
pointed to the only chair in sight and said, with a
sigh, “Sit down.”
The tall, thin man carefully extracted from his
tail pocket a neatly-folded copy of that day’s
Banner, and, laying it down before the editor,
indicated the centre column, and remarked, with
a profound sigh, “ I am the writer of that article.
Would you mind casting your eye over it ? ”
The editor put on his glasses and started to
road, and the long, thin man sat down and waited
patiently for five minutes.
At the end of that period the editor looked up
and said, in a tone of deep emotion, "I am very
sorry—this is most unfortunate ! ”
“It is, sir—most unfortunate ! ”
“ I’m afraid we have caused you pain ?”
The long man smiled like a suffering martyr.
“ I must admit you have hurt me.”
The editor stroked his head in great agitation,
and glanced hurriedlyover the introduction. “You
know,” he said, “ we do our best to guard against
this sort of thing, but, do what we will , mistakes
will creep in every now and then. We take
extra precautions in the case of special articles,
such as this, and I assure you, sir, we feel as much
annoyed as the author when the thing is marred
by typographical blunders. Of course, in this
case, the mistakes are neither numerous nor very
noticeable. I may say ”
“ Allow me,” interjected the long man, leaning
over and taking the paper. The editor sat silent,
Then the author cleared his throat, and read
as follows:—“‘Childhood is the spring of Life,
and there is no more beautiful thing in the world
than a happy child. Woe to him who loves not
the sight of a babe, or for whom bright eyes
beaming With ignorance have no charm!’ You
know, my dear sir, that word should be ‘ inno-
cence,' sir— ‘innocence’ ! Eyes do not beam with
ignorance ! You know that, surely !”
The editor gazed blankly at the inkpot.
The author searched for a minute, and then
readout this: “As Shakspeare says, ‘ lmmoral
Csesar dead and turned to cla.y.’ Now, sir, I have
no recollection of writing that ! As far as I re-
member, I made no reflections on Cmsar’s charac-
ter in my article. I may have called him immor-
tal, but I certainly did not say he was * immoral.’
Can you explain it?”
“lam afraid my compositor is not a Shafcs-
pearian scholar,” the editor faltered.
“But this isn’t Shakspeare,” muttered the
author, jabbing another paragraph with his index
finger. “‘Yet, in spite of all this; in spite of
rebuffs administered by the elemental forces of
Nature, mankind has kept hopping through the
cages.’ What I said was * hoping through the ages.’
Your compositor, sir, has made that sentence
absurd ! How do you reconcile ‘hopping through
the cages’ with ‘hoping through the ages ’ ?”
“ I don’t! ” moaned the editor.
“To me,” said the author bitterly—“to me
that sentence suggests an ape playing hop-scotch
in a deserted zoo. That isn’t the effect I aimed at
at all! ”
The editor said nothing.
“ Here ! ” shouted the writer suddenly, as his
eye lit on another passage. “ Here is another of
your infernal atrocities ! Listen to this ! ‘ But
oh ! when Love awakes in the heart, this grey
old world, with its cobwebs of memory, is lighted
up by the radiant dawn of sympathy, and we are
young again. In such hours, when we hear the
magpie in the field, bawling to his mate ’ (ugh !)
‘ and the cheap sparrows ’ —cheap sparrows, sir !
‘on the fence, we are filled with’- Oh ! d—n it,
what would you be filled with if you read that 1 ”
“I don’t know,” replied the editor desperately.
“ I give you my word I don’t know ! ”
“When the manuscript left my care, the mag-
pie was ‘ calling ’! ”
“ That’s exactly what I thought !”
“And the sparrows were not ‘ cheap sparrows
on the fence.’ They were ‘ cheeping sparrows on
the fence.’ That is, they were making a row with
their beaks—or something like that.”
“ I understand ! ”
“Yes, but the way that criminal of yours has
mixed it up, it reads as if I’d bought some cheap,
stuffed sparrows and stuck ’em on the fence before
J wrote the d d thing ! ”
“ Yes—yes !”
“ Then, sir, about the magpie ! ”
“ Don’t, I beg of you ! ”
“ One moment, sir ! If you were not an Aus-
tralian, and didn’t happen to know the magpie
was a bird, what would you make out of ‘ the
magpie bawling to his mate?’ Wouldn’t you be
inclined to think it was an oysterman out for the
day, shouting like these confounded people do at
their confounded picnics ? What’s that ?”
“I say, I don’t want to hear any more! ”
“ But you shall, sir ! You shall!” Theauthor,
in a frenzy of rage, caught up the paper again,
and stood over his victim where he sat crumpled
in his chair.
“ ‘Love,’” he shouted. “ ‘Love, the great petri-
fying passion.’ (Purifying, you Goth !) ‘Csesar
victorious was acclaimed with delightful snouts.’
(Shouts, you blackguard!) ‘ Leander swimming
Hell's Pint!’ (You swilling ruffian!) ‘Those
deliciously tender verses of Edgar Allan Poe—
Cannibal Lee.’ (Great Heaven !) And here ! ”
he shouted, tearing out a handful of the page and
slamming it down on the desk with a crash, “read
that last paragraph and go and paste it on your
one-horse brain ! ‘ And, at the end, with thankful-
ness in our hearts, we turn homeward and go
where the ghosts of a million heroes are leaping' —
leaping, mind you! not sleeping! — ‘leaping in
their shrines!’ You abandoned old man!”
he hissed. “You disgrace to letters! If you
aren’t leaping at the benevolent asylum inside
three months it will be because there isn’t any
justice or decency left in the d d country ! ”
He swept up his hat and whirled out of the
room and down the stairs, and the concussion of
the front door shutting upset the inkpot over a
blistering article on “Socialism in the Home.”
The editor sat in a brown study for three
minutes. Then he opened his mouth and roared
“George !”
Instantly, there was a sound of fumbling and
scuffling overhead.
“Come on!” howled the chief, furiously.
“ Come on I Don’t be all the blanky day ! ”
Something hurled itself downstairs and clat-
tered along the corridor. The door was opened
from without, and a touzled head, with wide-open
eyes, said “Wot?”
“Look here, my friend,” said the editor, be-
tween his clenched teeth. ‘ ‘l'm going to shake this
place up ! Hitherto this has been rather a go-as-
you-please crib, but henceforth—also for ever—
I’m going to play hell with every blithering incom-
petent who makes a mess of things. Now, I want
you to tell me straight what drunken swab set
that d d article on ‘The Glory of First Love,’
in to-day’s issue. Out with it now ! ”
George scratched his head for a minute ere he
started, and directed an inky forefinger at his
respected chief.
“Wasn’t that youl” he mumbled. “ You re-
member . . . w’en we was busy . . . lars Sat’d’p /”
The editor grew suddenly purple, and hurriedly
extracted his hat from the waste-paper basket.
“George,” he remarked, feelingly, “I rely on
you as a man of discretion. On this occasion all
will be forgiven. Have one with me ! ”
They went out together as brothers. „
N.S.W.
KODAK.
When Clubs were Trumps.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THOUGH our cavern-dwelling parent
In the days that used to be
Knew no uses for a brush and comb, or
tub.
He possessed a lot of knowledge
That is hid from you and me,
And he knew the varied uses of a club.
Though he may have been a heathen,
And a trifle rude and rough,
When we patiently investigate, we find
That, though ignorant of science
And a lot of modern stuff,
Still he knew the way to deal with womenkind.
When the prehistoric person
Wooed the prehistoric maid,
He was rigorous and rough, and often rude ;
For he hadn’t any maimers,
And, I’m very much afraid,
That his methods might be stigmatised as crude.
By the hair he used to grab her,
As she scuttled through the scrub,
And he dragged her to his cavernous retreat,
Where he ardently caressed her
With a hard and knobby club,
And the sacrament of marriage was complete.
Then the Voice that Breathed o’er Eden
Was the wailing of the bride—
Plus the diapason grunting of her hub—
As he sought the softest places
In her cuticle, or hide,
Which would take the best impressions of a
club.
So, through all the later stages
Of the cave-man’s married life,
Which could scarcely be monotonous or tame,
He evinced consummate knowledge
Of the way to treat a wife,
And he always knew the way to play the game.
In the risky game of marriage
Quite a lot of modern chumps
Lose the “doubles,” and the “singles,” and the
“rubs”
Just through thinking, in their folly,
That, in marriage, hearts are trumps,
While the cave-man always knew the trumps were
clubs.
And his wife was meek and lowly,
And she kept within the range
Of her proper sphere, as women ought to do.
And she never searched his pockets
To relieve him of his change,
For he hadn’t any pockets to go through.
She could fricasee a dodo,
She could mend a green-hide shirt
(We may take it she was never asked to scrub);
And through all her weary struggle
With the squalor and the dirt
She was heartened and encouraged—with a club.
She was spry, and she was active,
And assisted in the chase
When they had to “scratch the gravel” for their
grub;
But, however fleet the quarry,
Or however hot the pace,
Why, she always kept one eye upon the club !
If he lingered till the morning
At the prehistoric pub,
And she looked at all like laying down the law,
Well, he didn’t say, “ My darling,
They detained me at the club,”
But he just let her have it on the jaw ! O
What a sweet, domestic picture
Of the simple married life !
Free from modern complications ; for, of course,
If the club was instrumental
In the winning of a wife,
It was quite as efficacious for divorce.
When the cave-frequenting person
Had a yearning to be free
From a loving wife’s affectionate embrace,
Why, a wallop with a “ waddy”
Was an “ absolute decree,”
And there wasn’t any “nisi ” in the case !
No expensive fees to counsel,
No anxiety of mind,
As petitioners, in cases of the sort,
Feel when active co-respondents—
Leaving unpaid Costs behind—
Skip beyond the jurisdiction of the Court.
What a lot we must be missing
In decadent latter days—
’Spite the scientific lore of which we rave—
For we know far less of women,
And their management, and ways
Than the prehistoric person in the cave.
And the modern wife who waketh
When the early rooster crows,
And who findeth that her head is free from
bumps,
Ought to thank her stars (and garters)—
As the vulgar saying goes—
That she lived not in the days when clubs were
trumps.
